Guardianship Rulings for Non-Eligible Persons
Expands the base text on ineligible guardians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which persons are ineligible for marriage guardianship and explain the Hanafi proofs and exceptions.

Core Idea
A marriage guardian must be free, adult, sane, and Muslim; otherwise the marriage contract is invalid.

In Hanafi law, certain persons lack the legal capacity (wilayah) to act as guardians in marriage. This lesson examines four categories: slaves (including the mukatab), minors, the insane, and disbelievers. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on each, distinguishing between continuous and intermittent madness, and provides Quranic proofs. Understanding these eligibility rules ensures that marriages are validly contracted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four ineligible categories
From the base text: slave, minor, insane, and disbeliever (over a Muslim). List them and note that each lacks guardianship.
The categories are absolute unless explicitly excepted.
2
Understand the rationale: no self-guardianship
The commentary explains that if these persons have no guardianship over themselves, a fortiori they cannot have it over others. This is a qiyas-type argument.
Self-guardianship refers to managing their own marriage affairs.
3
Distinguish continuous vs. intermittent madness
A continuously insane person (mutbiq, one month+) has his guardianship stripped; the woman can be married without waiting. For non-continuous, guardianship remains; one must wait for sanity unless the suitable suitor is lost.
The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) added the exception for losing the suitor.
4
Recognize the mukatab's exception
Although generally a slave lacks guardianship, a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) can marry his own slave girl. This is a known exception.
The passage refers to al-Muhit for this detail.
5
Apply the Quranic proofs for disbelievers
Two verses are cited: 4:141 (no way for disbelievers over believers) and 8:73 (disbelievers are allies of each other). Thus a disbeliever cannot be guardian of a Muslim, but can of his disbelieving child.
These are qat'i proofs (certainty of transmission and meaning).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفسخ ولا ولاية لعبد وصغير ومجنون لا لكافر على مسلم. قوله : (ولا ولاية لصغير وعبد ومجنون) لأنه لا ولاية لهم على أنفسهم فأولى أن لا يثبت على غيرهم، ولأن هذه ولاية نظرية ولا نظر في التفويض إلى هؤلاء أطلق في العبد فشمل المكاتب فلا ولاية له على ولده كذا في المحيط. لكن للمكاتب ولاية في تزويج أمته كما عرف وأراد بالمجنون المطبق وهو شهر يحتاج إلى تقييده به وعليه الفتوى. وفي فتح القدير : لأنه لا حال جنونه مطبقاً أو يزوج حالة إفاقته عن جنون مطبق أو غير مطبق، لكن المعنى أنه إذا كان مطبقاً غير مطبق، ويزوج تسلب ولايته فتزوج ولا ينتظر إفاقته، وغير المطبق الولاية ثابتة له فلا تزوج وتنتظر إفاقته كالنائم. ومقتضى النظر أن الكفء الخاطب إن فات بانتظار إفاقته تزوج وإن لم يكن مطبقاً وإلا انتظر على ما اختاره المتأخرون في غيبة الولي الأقرب ا هـ قوله : (ولا لكافر على مسلم) لقوله تعالى ولن يجعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلاً ولهذا لا تقبل شهادته عليه ولا يتوارثان قيد بالمسلم لأن للكافر ولاية على ولده الكافر لقوله تعالى والذين كفروا بعضهم أولياء بعض ولهذا تقبل شهادتهم على بعضهم
No guardianship for a slave, minor, or insane person; not for a disbeliever over a Muslim. He said: (No guardianship for a minor, slave, and insane) because they have no guardianship over themselves, so it is more appropriate that it is not established over others, and because this is a guardianship of consideration, and there is no consideration in delegating to these. He made it absolute regarding the slave, so it includes the mukatab, so he has no guardianship over his child, as in al-Muhit. But the mukatab has guardianship in marrying his slave girl as is known. He intended by the insane the continuous [madness], which is a month; it needs to be restricted to it, and the fatwa is upon it. In Fath al-Qadir: because he does not marry during his madness, continuous or not, or he marries during his sanity from continuous or non-continuous madness, but the meaning is that if he is continuously mad, his guardianship is removed, so she is married without waiting for his sanity, and for the non-continuous, his guardianship is established, so she is not married and waits for his sanity like the sleeping person. The requirement of consideration is that if the suitable suitor is lost by waiting for his sanity, she is married, even if not continuous, otherwise wait, according to the later scholars' choice in the absence of the nearest guardian. He said: (and not for a disbeliever over a Muslim) due to His statement: 'And never will Allah give the disbelievers over the believers a way' (4:141), and hence his testimony against him is not accepted, nor do they inherit each other. He restricted it to Muslim because the disbeliever has guardianship over his disbelieving child due to His statement: 'And those who disbelieve are allies of one another' (8:73), and hence their testimony against each other is accepted.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, stating the ruling that certain persons lack legal guardianship (wilayah) in marriage. The base text lists four categories: slave, minor, insane person, and disbeliever (over a Muslim). The commentary then explains each. For the slave, this includes the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), except he can marry his own slave girl. For the insane, the ruling distinguishes between continuous madness (mutbiq) and intermittent madness (ghayr mutbiq). In continuous madness, the guardianship is stripped and the woman can be married off without waiting; in intermittent, the guardianship remains and one must wait for sanity. The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) add an exception: if a suitable suitor is lost by waiting, she may be married even if the mad person is not continuous. The disbeliever's lack of guardianship over a Muslim is proven by the Quranic verse (4:141), and the proof for their guardianship over fellow-disbelievers is from (8:73). Thus, a Muslim woman cannot be given in marriage by a non-Muslim guardian.

Key Terms
ولاية (wilayah): Legal authority to contract a marriage on behalf of another.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission with his master, paying in installments.
مطبق (mutbiq): Continuous madness lasting a month or more, stripping the person of legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) His guardianship is transferred to the nearest male relative.

Key Takeaway
A marriage guardian must be free, adult, sane, and Muslim; otherwise the marriage contract is invalid.

Five Differences Between Khiyar al-Bulugh and Khiyar al-Itq
Lists and explains five juristic differences between two marriage options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five differences between khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-‘itq and apply them to marriage scenarios.

Core Idea
Two marriage options in Hanafi fiqh differ in five key ways affecting their exercise and validity.

Khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-‘itq are two important options in marriage contracts. While both allow a party to repudiate a marriage, they operate under distinct rules. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq highlights five precise juristic differences between them. Mastering these distinctions is essential for correctly applying each option in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Options
First, define khiyar al-bulugh (the option of puberty) and khiyar al-‘itq (the option of emancipation). Understand who qualifies for each: minors married before puberty, and slave women freed after marriage, respectively.
Only females are eligible for khiyar al-‘itq, while both genders may have khiyar al-bulugh.
2
List the Five Differences
Read the passage and enumerate each of the five differences. For each, state the ruling for khiyar al-bulugh and the contrasting ruling for khiyar al-‘itq.
Use the key terms: qada (court ruling), silence, gender, ignorance, and leaving the session.
3
Determine the Practical Effect
For each difference, imagine a real scenario: How does the rule affect whether the option is valid? For example, a freed slave woman must act before leaving the court, but a minor girl who is not a virgin does not lose her option by leaving.
Pay attention to the exception for thayyib (previously married) and ghulam (boy) in khiyar al-bulugh.
4
Compare with General Khiyar
Consider how these two options differ from other types of khiyar (e.g., khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-‘ayb). The specific rules here are unique to these two contexts.
Not required from passage, but helpful for broader understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وختاماً وقد علم أن خيار البلوغ يخالف خيار العتق في مسائل: أولها اشتراط القضاء، والثاني أن خيار المعتقة لا يبطل بالسكوت بل يمتد إلى آخر المجلس كما في خيار البلوغ للثيب والغلام، والثالث أن خيار العتق يثبت للأنثى فقط بخلاف خيار البلوغ يثبت لهما، والرابع أن الجهل بخيار البلوغ ليس بعذر بخلافه في خيار العتق، والخامس أن خيار العتق يبطل بالقيام عن المجلس كالمخيرة وخيار البلوغ في حق الثيب والغلام لا يبطل به. كذا في غاية البيان.
And it is known that khiyar al-bulugh differs from khiyar al-‘itq in issues: First: the condition of judicial decree; Second: that the option of the mu‘taqah does not lapse by silence but extends to the end of the session as in khiyar al-bulugh for the thayyib and ghulam; Third: that khiyar al-‘itq is established for the female only, unlike khiyar al-bulugh which is established for both; Fourth: that ignorance of khiyar al-bulugh is not an excuse, unlike in khiyar al-‘itq; Fifth: that khiyar al-‘itq lapses by standing up from the session like the mukhayyarah, while khiyar al-bulugh for the thayyib and ghulam does not lapse by it. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage enumerates five differences between the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) and the option of emancipation (khiyar al-‘itq) in marriage. The first difference is that khiyar al-‘itq requires a court ruling (qada) to be effective, while khiyar al-bulugh does not necessarily require judicial intervention. The second difference concerns the effect of silence: for a freed slave woman (mu‘taqah), silence does not nullify her option; it remains until the end of the session. However, for khiyar al-bulugh, this applies only to a thayyib (previously married woman) or a boy (ghulam); a virgin's option may lapse by silence. The third difference is that khiyar al-‘itq is only for females, whereas khiyar al-bulugh is for both males and females. The fourth difference relates to ignorance: being unaware of the option of puberty is not an excuse that can revive the option after it lapses, whereas ignorance in khiyar al-‘itq can be an excuse. The fifth difference concerns leaving the session: khiyar al-‘itq lapses if the person stands up from the session, like the option of the mukhayyarah (a woman given a choice upon conversion), while khiyar al-bulugh for a thayyib or ghulam does not lapse by merely leaving the session. These distinctions are important for practical application in marriage contracts.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option of puberty: the right of a minor married before puberty to repudiate the marriage upon attaining puberty.
خيار العتق (khiyar al-‘itq): The option of emancipation: the right of a slave woman who is married and then freed to repudiate the marriage.
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has been previously married (not a virgin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about khiyar al-‘itq?
A) It is established for both males and females.
B) Ignorance of this option is not an excuse.
C) It lapses if the person stands up from the session.
D) It does not require a court ruling.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-‘itq differ in five key ways: requirement of court ruling, effect of silence, gender applicability, ignorance as excuse, and effect of leaving the session.

Duration and Indications of Satisfaction in Khiyar al-Bulugh
Explains duration of khiyar al-bulugh and examples of satisfaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the duration of khiyar al-bulugh and identify which actions constitute satisfaction that nullify the option, distinguishing between different categories (thayyib, bikr, ghulam).

Core Idea
Khiyar al-bulugh lasts a lifetime until clear satisfaction; only explicit consent or compelling acts nullify it.

A minor given in marriage upon reaching puberty retains an option to either confirm or annul the marriage. But how long does this option last? And what counts as acceptance? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the option continues indefinitely until evidence of satisfaction appears. It also addresses an apparent disagreement with Imam al-Tahawi, reconciling his statements with the majority Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that khiyar al-bulugh lasts a 'lifetime' because its cause is lack of satisfaction. So it remains until something indicating satisfaction occurs.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Tahawi reportedly said the option lapses by silence for a virgin, but not for a thayyib. The majority holds it does not lapse by silence for either unless there is explicit evidence of satisfaction.
3
Reconcile al-Tahawi's view
The commentator interprets al-Tahawi's 'engaging in something else' as referring to acts that clearly indicate satisfaction, like intercourse or voluntary submission, not mere silence or any action.
This aligns al-Tahawi with the majority.
4
List actions that constitute satisfaction
Acts that nullify: verbal consent, intercourse, voluntary submission, claiming dower or maintenance. Acts that do not nullify: silence, staying with husband, eating his food, serving him.
5
Consider the claim of coercion
If the woman claims she was coerced in submission, she is believed and the option remains. This protects her against forced acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصله أن وقت خيارهما العمر لأن سببه عدم الرضا فيبقى إلى أن يوجد ما يدل على الرضا. على هذا تظافرت كلمتهم كما في غاية البيان. فما نقل عن الطحاوي حيث قال : خيار المدركة يبطل بالسكوت إذا كانت بكراً، وإن كانت ثيباً لم يبطل به، وكذا إذا كان الخيار للزوج لا يبطل إلا بصريح الإبطال أو يجيء منه دليل على إبطال الخيار كما إذا اشتغلت بشيء آخر وأعرضت عن الاختيار بوجه من الوجوه مشكل إذ يقتضي أن الاشتغال بعمل آخر يبطله، وهذا تقييد بالمجلس ضرورة إذ تبدله حقيقة أو حكماً يستلزمه ظاهراً. وفي الجوامع : وإن كانت ثيباً حين بلغها أو كان غلاماً لم يبطل بالسكوت وإن أقامت معه أياماً إلا أن ترضى بلسانها أو يوجد ما يدل على الرضا من الوطء أو التمكين منه طوعاً أو المطالبة بالمهر أو النفقة. وفيه : لو قالت كنت مكرهة في التمكين صدقت ولا يبطل خيارها. وفي الخلاصة : لو أكلت من طعامه أو خدمته فهي على خيارها لا يقال كون القول لها في دعوى الإكراه في التمكين مشكل لأن الظاهر يصدقها كذا في فتح القدير ولا إشكال في عبارة شرح الطحاوي لأن مراده من الاشتغال بشيء آخر عمل يدل على الرضا بالنكاح كالتمكين ونحوه لا مطلق العمل كما يدل عليه سياق كلامه بل قد صرح بأن خيار البلوغ في حق الثيب والغلام لا يبطل بالقيام عن المجلس وإلا فينبغي أن يحمل على ما ذكرناه ليوافق غيره.
In brief, the time of their option is a lifetime because its cause is the lack of satisfaction, so it remains until something indicating satisfaction appears. Their agreement is corroborated on this as in Ghayat al-Bayan. What is transmitted from al-Tahawi where he said: The option of the mature woman lapses by silence if she is a virgin, and if she is a thayyib, it does not lapse by it; similarly if the option belongs to the husband, it does not lapse except by explicit revocation or evidence of revocation of the option, such as if she engages in something else and turns away from choice in any way, is problematic, because it implies that engaging in another action invalidates it. This is a restriction to the session necessarily, since changing it actually or constructively apparently necessitates it. In al-Jawami': If she is a thayyib when she reaches puberty, or is a boy, it does not lapse by silence even if she stays with him for days, unless she consents verbally or there is evidence of satisfaction such as intercourse, voluntary submission, claiming dower or maintenance. And in it: If she says 'I was coerced in submission', she is believed and her option does not lapse. In al-Khulasa: If she ate from his food or served him, her option remains. It is not said that her claim of coercion in submission is problematic because apparently she is believed, as in Fath al-Qadir, and there is no problem in the phrase of Sharh al-Tahawi because his intention by 'engaging in something else' is an act indicating satisfaction with the marriage, such as submission and the like, not absolute action, as indicated by the context of his speech. Rather he explicitly stated that khiyar al-bulugh for the thayyib and ghulam does not lapse by standing up, otherwise it should be interpreted as we mentioned to agree with others.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage discusses the duration of khiyar al-bulugh and what actions nullify it. The general rule is that the option lasts a lifetime until evidence of satisfaction exists. The scholars agree on this. However, a statement from al-Tahawi suggests it lapses by silence for a virgin, but not for a thayyib. The commentator reconciles this by interpreting 'engaging in something else' as referring to acts that clearly indicate satisfaction, not just any action. Specific examples of nullifying actions include: intercourse, allowing intercourse voluntarily, claiming dower or maintenance from the husband. Even if these occur, if the woman claims she was coerced, she is believed and her option remains. Eating from his food or serving him does not nullify the option. For the boy (ghulam) and the thayyib, the option is stronger and does not lapse by silence or leaving the session. The passage resolves the apparent conflict between al-Tahawi and the majority Hanafi view by contextualizing al-Tahawi's words.

Key Terms
خيار المدركة (khiyar al-mudrikah): The option of the woman who has reached puberty (and was married as a minor).
سكوت (sukut): Silence that may indicate acceptance or rejection depending on context.
تمكين (tamkin): Allowing sexual intercourse voluntarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Staying with her husband for three days.
B) Eating from his food.
C) Voluntarily allowing intercourse.
D) Being silent for a month.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-bulugh remains until clear evidence of satisfaction; silence or routine cohabitation does not nullify it.

Duration and Effect of Silence in Khiyar al-Bulugh
Explains the lifetime duration of khiyar al-bulugh and the effect of silence for different types of women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the duration of khiyar al-bulugh in the Hanafi school and explain when silence voids the option for a virgin versus a thayyib or boy.

Core Idea
Silence voids a virgin's khiyar al-bulugh but not a thayyib's; shyness makes silence consent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) lasts a lifetime because its cause—lack of consent—persists until consent appears. The text distinguishes between a virgin and a non-virgin (thayyib) or boy: mere silence voids the former's option due to shyness, while the latter must explicitly consent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the validity of a marriage contracted during minority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the duration of khiyar al-bulugh
The passage states it lasts a lifetime because the cause (lack of consent) remains until consent is shown. Note the principle: 'waqt ikhtiyarihima al-umr'.
This is the default rule; exceptions will be covered.
2
Distinguish between virgin and thayyib/boy on silence
For a virgin, silence voids the option; for a thayyib or a boy, it does not. This is transmitted from al-Tahawi and generally accepted.
The term 'thayyib' includes a non-virgin woman; 'ghulam' refers to a boy.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the difference
The text alludes to shyness (al-haya') as the basis for considering a virgin's silence as consent legally. This is a legal construction that equates silence with speech for the virgin.
This reasoning is distinct from explicit consent, which is required for others.
4
Contrast with khiyar al-‘itq
The option of emancipation is not voided by silence but extends to the end of the session. The passage highlights this difference to clarify boundaries.
This shows that the rule for khiyar al-bulugh is specific and not generalized.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Consider a virgin who becomes silent after reaching puberty. Her option is voided. If a thayyib stays silent, her option remains until she explicitly consents.
Practice with examples: silence vs. speaking.

إن سألت عن اسم الزوج مع علمها به أو سلمت معنى بأن قالت مرحباً للشهود ونحو ذلك يلزمها لكون ذلك مستغنى عنه، أما إذا ردت سلامهم أو كانت جاهلة بالزوج فالسؤال عنه لا يكون كالسكوت والحاصل أن اشتغالها بما لا And its summary is that the time of their option is a lifetime, because its cause is lack of consent, so it remains until something indicating consent appears. On this their statements concur as in Ghayat al-Bayan. So what is transmitted from al-Tahawi where he said: The option of the discerning one is voided by silence if she is a virgin, but if she is a non-virgin it is not voided by it. Likewise, if the option is for the husband, it is not voided except by explicit cancellation or if evidence of cancellation comes from him, such as when she occupies herself with something else and turns away from choice in any manner, is problematic because it would imply that occupying with another action voids it. And this is a restriction to the session necessarily. In al-Jawami': If she was a non-virgin when she attained puberty, or was a boy, it is not voided by silence even if she stays with him for days, unless she consents verbally or something indicating consent occurs from intercourse or allowing it willingly, or demanding the dower or maintenance. And in it: If she said 'I was coerced in allowing,' she is believed and her option is not voided. In al-Khulasa: If she ate from his food or served him, she retains her option. It cannot be said that the claim of coercion in allowing is problematic because the apparent is that she is believed, as in Fath al-Qadir. And there is no problem in the expression of Sharh al-Tahawi because what he meant by occupying with something else is an action indicating consent to marriage, like allowing and the like, not absolute action. Rather he explicitly stated that khiyar al-bulugh for the thayyib and boy is not voided by leaving the session. Then in Fath al-Qadir: The testimony of the two masters is accepted regarding the choice of their slave-girl whom they married off herself when they emancipated her, but the testimony of the agnatic guardians who married her off after puberty that she chose herself is not accepted because the cause of rejection has been severed in the first by emancipation but not in the second as it is lineage which remains. It is known that khiyar al-bulugh differs from khiyar al-‘itq in issues: the requirement of qada’ and that khiyar al-mu‘taqa is not voided by silence but extends to the end of the session as here. So she remains on her option like the option of defect. And what is in al-Tabyin that if she sent her servant when she menstruated to the witnesses and could not find them while she was in a remote place, it becomes binding and she is not excused—this is carried on the case where she did not annul verbally until she did it. And in al-Dhakhira: If she asked about the husband's name or the dower or greeted the witnesses, her option is voided—this is forced and baseless. At most this situation is like the initial state of marriage. If the virgin asked about the husband's name, it does not affect her, likewise about the dower. Although not mentioning it to her does not void her silence being consent according to the disagreement, for that is when she did not ask about it because it appears she is content with any dower, and asking negates the appearance of that, and her consent depends on knowledge of its amount. Likewise greeting the arriving person does not indicate consent. In Fath al-Qadir there is discussion: The voiding of this option is not dependent on something indicating consent, for that is only for the thayyib and boy. As for the virgin, it is voided by mere silence, and there is no doubt that occupying with greeting is above silence. When khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-shuf‘a are combined, she says 'I claim both rights' then begins interpretation with khiyar al-bulugh. It is restricted to the virgin because if she were a thayyib, as if the husband had intercourse before puberty or she was thayyib at the time of contract, it is not voided by her silence; she is like the boy, requiring consent by words or an indicating action. As long as she does not allow him physically, as stated in al-Dhakhira. And it appears that the month and two months are examples of an estimated thing, because her right is established by witnessing, so it is not voided by delay like shuf‘a. Ponder. His statement: 'There is no doubt that occupying with greeting is above silence.' He said in al-Nahr: It is prevented. They transmitted in shuf‘a that his greeting the buyer does not void it because the Prophet said 'Salam' before speech. And there is no doubt that demanding immediate action after knowledge of the sale is voided by silence like khiyar al-bulugh, and if it were above it, it would be voided. And they said: If he said 'Who bought it and for how much did he buy it?' his preemption is not voided, as in al-Bazzaziyya. This supports what is in Fath al-Qadir. Then in al-Ramz after reporting the author's discussion: The answer is that consent is necessary but sometimes it is explicit and sometimes it is indicative for the thayyib and virgin, but mere silence from the virgin is made consent legally and stands in place of speech due to shyness. I say: It should be said that if she asked about the husband's name while knowing it, or greeted meaning she said 'welcome' to the witnesses and the like, it becomes binding because that is dispensable. But if she returned their greeting or was ignorant of the husband, then asking is not like silence. The conclusion is that her occupying herself with that which is not beneficial stands in place of silence and thus becomes binding, not that which she needs for this purpose. His statement: 'When khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-shuf‘a are combined...' he said.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probing the rulings of khiyar al-bulugh (the option of puberty). The author clarifies that the duration of this option is a lifetime because its cause—lack of consent—persists until evidence of consent appears. For a virgin, silence is deemed consent due to shyness (al-haya'), so her option is voided by mere silence. For a thayyib (non-virgin) or a boy, silence does not void the option; explicit verbal consent or an action clearly indicating consent is required, such as allowing intercourse voluntarily, demanding dower or maintenance. The text then addresses objections and nuances: if a thayyib claims she was coerced in allowing intercourse, she is believed and her option remains. Actions like eating food or serving him do not void the option. The passage also distinguishes khiyar al-bulugh from khiyar al-'itq (option of emancipation): the latter is not voided by silence but extends to the end of the session. Regarding actions like sending a servant, asking about the husband's name or dower, or greeting witnesses, there is a difference of opinion. The preferred view is that if the virgin asks about the name knowing it or gives a superfluous greeting, it becomes binding; but if she is ignorant or returns a greeting, it does not. The key principle is that only actions that indicate consent or are unnecessary void the option.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option given to a minor upon reaching puberty to annul or confirm a marriage contract made on her behalf by a guardian.
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has lost her virginity through marriage or fornication; for khiyar al-bulugh, she must explicitly consent.
غلام (ghulam): A boy who has reached puberty; in khiyar al-bulugh, he is treated like a thayyib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a virgin's khiyar al-bulugh if she remains silent after reaching puberty?
A) Her option remains until she explicitly consents.
B) Her option is voided by silence.
C) Her option is voided only if she stays silent for more than a month.
D) Her option transfers to her guardian.

Key Takeaway
Silence voids the option for a virgin but not for a thayyib or boy, because shyness makes silence equivalent to consent for the virgin.

Nikah — Marriage

Actions That Impact Khiyar al-Bulugh
Explains specific actions that affect khiyar al-bulugh and intra-madhhab differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions void or preserve the khiyar al-bulugh for a virgin and a thayyib, and explain the difference of opinion on actions like asking about the husband's name or greeting witnesses.

Core Idea
Actions that indicate consent void the option, but mere inquiries or greetings do not; the key is whether the action is necessary or demonstrates satisfaction.

Building on the general rule, this passage delves into specific actions that may affect the option: asking about the husband's name or dower, greeting witnesses, sending a servant, or engaging in intercourse. The Hanafi school distinguishes between a virgin's silence (which voids) and a thayyib's (which does not), but for non-silence actions, the intent matters. This lesson clarifies the nuances to avoid misapplication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The text discusses a virgin asking about the husband's name or dower, and greeting witnesses. Also, a thayyib claiming coercion in allowing intercourse.
These are specific applications of the general rule.
2
Determine the effect for a virgin
According to Fath al-Qadir, asking about the name if she knows it does not void; if she is ignorant, it does not void either because it is necessary. The key is whether the action is needed or superfluous.
If the action is necessary for her decision, it does not void; if it is unnecessary, it acts like silence and voids.
3
Discuss the disagreement on greeting
Some opinions say greeting voids, but the author argues it does not since it is not necessarily indicative of consent. The text compares with shuf'a rulings.
The author concludes that only actions that are dispensable and indicate satisfaction void.
4
Apply the rule to a thayyib or boy
For them, only explicit consent or actions like intercourse void; greeting or asking about name does not void because silence does not void for them.
The threshold for voiding is higher for thayyib and boy.
5
Contrast with khiyar al-‘itq
The option of emancipation has different rules, but the passage here focuses on khiyar al-bulugh alone.
This step reinforces that different options have different conditions.

And its summary is that the time of their option is a lifetime, because its cause is lack of consent, so it remains until something indicating consent appears. On this their statements concur as in Ghayat al-Bayan. So what is transmitted from al-Tahawi where he said: The option of the discerning one is voided by silence if she is a virgin, but if she is a non-virgin it is not voided by it. Likewise, if the option is for the husband, it is not voided except by explicit cancellation or if evidence of cancellation comes from him, such as when she occupies herself with something else and turns away from choice in any manner, is problematic because it would imply that occupying with another action voids it. And this is a restriction to the session necessarily. In al-Jawami': If she was a non-virgin when she attained puberty, or was a boy, it is not voided by silence even if she stays with him for days, unless she consents verbally or something indicating consent occurs from intercourse or allowing it willingly, or demanding the dower or maintenance. And in it: If she said 'I was coerced in allowing,' she is believed and her option is not voided. In al-Khulasa: If she ate from his food or served him, she retains her option. It cannot be said that the claim of coercion in allowing is problematic because the apparent is that she is believed, as in Fath al-Qadir. And there is no problem in the expression of Sharh al-Tahawi because what he meant by occupying with something else is an action indicating consent to marriage, like allowing and the like, not absolute action. Rather he explicitly stated that khiyar al-bulugh for the thayyib and boy is not voided by leaving the session. Then in Fath al-Qadir: The testimony of the two masters is accepted regarding the choice of their slave-girl whom they married off herself when they emancipated her, but the testimony of the agnatic guardians who married her off after puberty that she chose herself is not accepted because the cause of rejection has been severed in the first by emancipation but not in the second as it is lineage which remains. It is known that khiyar al-bulugh differs from khiyar al-‘itq in issues: the requirement of qada’ and that khiyar al-mu‘taqa is not voided by silence but extends to the end of the session as here. So she remains on her option like the option of defect. And what is in al-Tabyin that if she sent her servant when she menstruated to the witnesses and could not find them while she was in a remote place, it becomes binding and she is not excused—this is carried on the case where she did not annul verbally until she did it. And in al-Dhakhira: If she asked about the husband's name or the dower or greeted the witnesses, her option is voided—this is forced and baseless. At most this situation is like the initial state of marriage. If the virgin asked about the husband's name, it does not affect her, likewise about the dower. Although not mentioning it to her does not void her silence being consent according to the disagreement, for that is when she did not ask about it because it appears she is content with any dower, and asking negates the appearance of that, and her consent depends on knowledge of its amount. Likewise greeting the arriving person does not indicate consent. In Fath al-Qadir there is discussion: The voiding of this option is not dependent on something indicating consent, for that is only for the thayyib and boy. As for the virgin, it is voided by mere silence, and there is no doubt that occupying with greeting is above silence. When khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-shuf‘a are combined, she says 'I claim both rights' then begins interpretation with khiyar al-bulugh. It is restricted to the virgin because if she were a thayyib, as if the husband had intercourse before puberty or she was thayyib at the time of contract, it is not voided by her silence; she is like the boy, requiring consent by words or an indicating action. As long as she does not allow him physically, as stated in al-Dhakhira. And it appears that the month and two months are examples of an estimated thing, because her right is established by witnessing, so it is not voided by delay like shuf‘a. Ponder. His statement: 'There is no doubt that occupying with greeting is above silence.' He said in al-Nahr: It is prevented. They transmitted in shuf‘a that his greeting the buyer does not void it because the Prophet said 'Salam' before speech. And there is no doubt that demanding immediate action after knowledge of the sale is voided by silence like khiyar al-bulugh, and if it were above it, it would be voided. And they said: If he said 'Who bought it and for how much did he buy it?' his preemption is not voided, as in al-Bazzaziyya. This supports what is in Fath al-Qadir. Then in al-Ramz after reporting the author's discussion: The answer is that consent is necessary but sometimes it is explicit and sometimes it is indicative for the thayyib and virgin, but mere silence from the virgin is made consent legally and stands in place of speech due to shyness. I say: It should be said that if she asked about the husband's name while knowing it, or greeted meaning she said 'welcome' to the witnesses and the like, it becomes binding because that is dispensable. But if she returned their greeting or was ignorant of the husband, then asking is not like silence. The conclusion is that her occupying herself with that which is not beneficial stands in place of silence and thus becomes binding, not that which she needs for this purpose. His statement: 'When khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-shuf‘a are combined...' he said.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probing the rulings of khiyar al-bulugh (the option of puberty). The author clarifies that the duration of this option is a lifetime because its cause—lack of consent—persists until evidence of consent appears. For a virgin, silence is deemed consent due to shyness (al-haya'), so her option is voided by mere silence. For a thayyib (non-virgin) or a boy, silence does not void the option; explicit verbal consent or an action clearly indicating consent is required, such as allowing intercourse voluntarily, demanding dower or maintenance. The text then addresses objections and nuances: if a thayyib claims she was coerced in allowing intercourse, she is believed and her option remains. Actions like eating food or serving him do not void the option. The passage also distinguishes khiyar al-bulugh from khiyar al-'itq (option of emancipation): the latter is not voided by silence but extends to the end of the session. Regarding actions like sending a servant, asking about the husband's name or dower, or greeting witnesses, there is a difference of opinion. The preferred view is that if the virgin asks about the name knowing it or gives a superfluous greeting, it becomes binding; but if she is ignorant or returns a greeting, it does not. The key principle is that only actions that indicate consent or are unnecessary void the option.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option given to a minor upon reaching puberty to annul or confirm a marriage contract made on her behalf by a guardian.
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has lost her virginity through marriage or fornication; for khiyar al-bulugh, she must explicitly consent.
سكوت (sukut): Silence; for a virgin, it is deemed consent; for a thayyib, it is not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, what is the ruling if a virgin asks the witnesses about her husband's name after reaching puberty?
A) Her option is voided because she engaged in an action above silence.
B) Her option is not voided because it is a necessary inquiry.
C) Her option is voided only if she asks in a demanding tone.
D) Her option is transferred to her father.

Key Takeaway
Necessary actions like asking about the marriage details do not void the option; only unnecessary actions indicating satisfaction or explicit consent do.

Combining Khiyar al-Bulugh and Shuf'a
Discusses procedure when both rights combine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the procedure when a virgin has both khiyar al-bulugh and shuf'a, and evaluate the opposing view that requires a fixed order.

Core Idea
A general claim preserves both rights; no fixed order is required.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a virgin who attains puberty and discovers she was married as a minor and also has a preemption right. Should she first decide on the marriage or on the property? The commentator argues that a general claim prevents forfeiture of either right, so the order is flexible, contrary to some scholars who insist on a specific sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rights and the case
Recognize that the passage deals with a virgin having both khiyar al-bulugh (option to annul or confirm childhood marriage) and shuf'a (preemption right in property). Note the distinction from a thayyib or boy, who require explicit consent.
2
Understand the general claim and interpretation
The procedure: she says 'I claim both rights' (general claim), then 'interprets' (specifies which she is exercising). This general claim prevents forfeiture of either right due to delay.
The author emphasizes that the early imams gave examples, not obligations.
3
Analyse the dispute and the Hanafi response
Some later scholars argued she must begin with a specific right (e.g., khiyar al-bulugh first or shuf'a first). The Hanafi response: since the general claim already preserves both, order is irrelevant; imposing order causes hardship, which is removed in Sharia.
The commentary cites that weeping or greeting do not void rights, supporting flexibility.

وفي جامع الفصولين ولو ثبت للبكر خيار البلوغ والشفعة تقول طلبت الحقين ثم تفسر وتبدأ بالاختيار، وقيل بالشفعة وقيل تطلب الشفعة وتبكي صراحاً فيصير هذا البكاء رداً للنكاح على قول من يجعله رداً له. أقول : لا أدري ما وجه تعيين البداءة بأحدهما في التفسير بعد طلب الحقين جملة فإنا حيث اعتبرناه هو المانع من السقوط فلا يضر تقديم أحدهما على الآخر، ولا يبطل المؤخر لأنه ثبت بالإحمال المتقدم والألف واللام فيه جامعة لهما، ولو قيل لا حاجة إلى التفسير بعده أصلاً لكان له وجه وجيه وأيضاً فيه تضيق وتعسير ونوع حرج وذلك مرفوع،

His statement: 'When khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-shuf'a are combined...' he said.

In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If a virgin has both khiyar al-bulugh and shuf'a, she says 'I claim both rights' then interprets and begins with choice. It is said with shuf'a, and it is said she claims shuf'a and weeps openly, and this weeping becomes rejection of marriage according to the one who makes it a rejection. I say: I do not know what is the reason for specifying the beginning with one of them in interpretation after claiming both rights generally, for when we consider it as the preventing factor of forfeiture, then precedence of one does not harm, and the subsequent is not voided because it is established by the prior general expression, and the definite article includes both. And if it were said that there is no need for interpretation after it at all, it would have a valid aspect. Also there is difficulty and hardship, and that is removed. And it appears that the early imams mentioned the issue, and some of them said by way of example: She says 'I claim myself and shuf'a.' And some of them said 'I claim shuf'a and myself.' Some later scholars mistakenly thought this was obligatory and binding, but it is not so. Rather she proceeds in interpretation as she wishes. Ponder.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and parsing exercise regarding the procedure when a female has both khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) and shuf'a (preemption) simultaneously. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on a statement from the matn that she should say 'I claim both rights' and then begin interpretation (tafsir) with khiyar al-bulugh. He clarifies that this applies only to a virgin because a thayyib or a boy requires explicit consent, not mere silence. He then addresses a potential objection: some argue that occupying with greeting (salam) is above silence, but he refutes this by citing that greeting does not void shuf'a. The text then from Jami' al-Fusulayn presents a debate: some say she should begin with khiyar al-bulugh, others with shuf'a, and some even consider weeping as rejection. The author (commentator) expresses confusion about why there is a requirement to specify the order, arguing that since claiming both rights generally prevents forfeiture, order does not matter. He emphasizes that the examples given by earlier imams are not obligatory but merely illustrative. The lesson underscores the Hanafi flexibility: there is no rigid sequence; she may interpret (i.e., choose which right to exercise) as she wishes.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Preemption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the property sold to a third party by matching the price.
التفسير (al-tafsir): Interpretation or explanation of one's claim or choice, specifying which right is being exercised.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, what should a virgin do if she has both khiyar al-bulugh and shuf'a?
A) She must first exercise khiyar al-bulugh, then shuf'a.
B) She must first exercise shuf'a, then khiyar al-bulugh.
C) She can claim both rights generally and then interpret in any order she wishes.
D) She loses khiyar al-bulugh if she does not interpret it first.

Key Takeaway
When two rights combine, a general claim preserves both; no fixed order is required in the Hanafi school.

Nullification of Khiyar al-Bulugh for a Virgin
Detailed rulings on nullification of khiyar al-bulugh for a virgin, including refutation of contrary views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules to determine whether a virgin's khiyar al-bulugh is nullified by her actions after reaching puberty.

Core Idea
A virgin's option of puberty is nullified by silence with knowledge, but not by asking about the husband or greeting.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies when a virgin who reaches puberty loses her option to annul the marriage. The author refutes earlier claims that questioning or greeting nullifies the option, establishing that only silence with full knowledge or explicit consent ends it. The distinction turns on whether her action indicates acceptance or merely inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action after puberty
Determine whether the virgin remained silent, asked about the husband's name or mahr, or greeted the witnesses.
Silence with knowledge is the key nullifier.
2
Assess knowledge of the marriage
Check if she knew she was married. Knowledge of the marriage is required for silence to nullify; ignorance excuses her.
She need not know she has an option; ignorance of the law is not excused.
3
Apply the nullification rule
If she was silent with knowledge, the option is nullified. If she asked about the husband or greeted, the option remains.
The author rejects the view from al-Dhakhirah that questioning nullifies.
4
Distinguish virgin from thayyib
For a virgin, silence is consent; for a thayyib, explicit verbal or gestural consent is needed. A thayyib's option is not nullified by silence.
The basis is shyness (hayā').
5
Consider the timing and special cases
The option is nullified by silence with knowledge; it does not extend to the end of the sitting. If she sees blood, she can still annul by speaking, even if she must lie to preserve her right.
Imam Muhammad permits lying in attribution (claiming she just saw blood) to protect her option.

(۱) ولا شك أن طلب المواثبة بعد العلم بالبيع يبطل بالسكوت كخيار البلوغ ولو كان فوقه لبطلت وقالوا: لو قال من اشتراها وبكم اشتراها لا تبطل شفعته كما في البزازية. وهذا يؤيد ما في فتح القدير، نعم ما وجه به في المهر إنما لم يخل أما إذا خلا بها خلوة صحيحة فالوقوف على كميته اشتغال بما لا يفيد لوجوبه بها، فإطلاق عدم سقوطه يتم إذا مما لا ينبغي ا هـ. وفي الرمز بعد نقل بحث المؤلف : والجواب أن الرضا لا بد منه لكنه تارة يكون صريحاً وتارة يكون دلالة في الثيب والبكر لكن مجرد السكوت من البكر جعل رضاً شرعاً وقام مقام القول لعلة الحياء. وأقول : ينبغي أن يقال إن سألت عن اسم الزوج مع علمها به أو سلمت معنى بأن قالت مرحباً للشهود ونحو ذلك يلزمها لكون ذلك مستغنى عنه، أما إذا ردت سلامهم أو كانت جاهلة بالزوج فالسؤال عنه لا يكون كالسكوت والحاصل أن اشتغالها بما لا يفيد يقوم مقام السكوت فيلزمها لا ما تحتاج إليه في هذا المقصود قوله : ( وإذا اجتمع خيار البلوغ والشفعة الخ) قال (۱) رواه الترمذي في كتاب الاستئذان باب ۱۱. ويبطل بسكوتها أن علمت بكر إلا بسكوته ما لم يقل رضيت ولو دلالة وتوارثا قبل الكفاءة، المهر بل يجب نصفه، فالحق أن لا يجعل لهذه المسألة ضابط بل يحكم في كل فرد بما أفاده الدليل . ثم اعلم أن الفرقة ثلاث عشرة فرقة : سبع منها تحتاج إلى القضاء، وست لا تحتاج . أما الأولى فالفرقة بالجب، والفرقة بالعنة والفرقة بخيار البلوغ، والفرقة بعدم والفرقة بنقصان المهر، والفرقة بإباء الزوج عن الإسلام والفرقة باللعان. وإنما توقفت على القضاء لأنها تنبني على سبب خفي لأن الكفاءة شيء لا يعرف بالحس وأسبابها مختلفة وكذا بنقصان مهر المثل، وخيار البلوغ مبني على قصور الشفقة وهو أمر باطن والإباء ربما يوجد وكذا البقية . وأما الثانية فالفرقة بخيار العتق والفرقة بالإيلاء، والفرقة بالرد، والفرقة بتباين الدارين والفرقة بملك أحد الزوجين صاحبه، والفرقة في النكاح الفاسد وإنما لم تتوقف هذه الست على القضاء لأنها تبتني على سبب جلي. ثم قال الإمام المحبوبي في التنقيح : كل فرقة جاءت من قبل المرأة لا بسبب من قبل الزوج فهي فرقة بغير طلاق كالردة من جهة المرأة وخيار البلوغ وخيار العتاقة وعدم الكفاءة، وكل فرقة جاءت من قبل الزوج فهي طلاق كالإيلاء والجب والعنة ولا يلزم على هذا ردة الزوج على قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لأن بالردة ينتفي الملك فينتفي الحل الذي هو من لوازم الملك فإنما حصلت الفرقة بالتنافي والتضاد لا بوجود المباشرة من الزوج بخلاف الإباء من جهة الزوج حيث يكون طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأنه لا تنافي بدليل أن الملك يبقى بعدم الإباء فلهذا افترقا اهـ . قوله: (ويبطل بسكوتها إن علمت بكراً لا بسكوته ما لم يقل رضيت ولو دلالة) أي ويبطل خيار البلوغ بسكوت من بلغت إلى آخره اعتباراً لهذه الحالة بحالة ابتداء النكاح، وسكوت البكر في الابتداء إذن بخلاف سكوت الثيب والغلام وأراد بالعلم العلم بأصل النكاح لأنه لا تتمكن من التصرف إلا به والولي ينفرد به فعذرت ولا يشترط العلم بأن لها خيار البلوغ لأنها تتفرغ لمعرفة أحكام الشرع والدار دار العلم فلم تعذر بالجهل بخلاف المعتقة لأن الأمة لا تتفرغ لمعرفتها فتعذر بالجهل بثبوت الخيار واستفيد من بطلانه بسكوتها أنه لا يمتد إلى آخر المجلس، وعلى هذا قالوا: ينبغي أن يبطل مع رؤية الدم فإن رأته ليلاً تطلب بلسانها فتقول فسخت نكاحي وتشهد إذا أصبحت وتقول رأيت الدم الآن. وقيل لمحمد : كيف يصح وهو كذب وإنما أدركت قبل هذا؟ فقال : لا تصدق في الإسناد فجاز لها أن تكذب كيلا يبطل حقها . ثم إذا اختارت وأشهدت ولم تتقدم إلى القاضي الشهر والشهرين وسيأتي لأنها فرقة حصلت بسبب لا من قبل الزوج فلا يمكن أن تجعل طلاقاً فتجعل فسخاً اهـ. إيضاحه في محله ا هـ فتأمل .
She is upon her option like the option of defect. What is in al-Tabyin that if she sent her servant when she menstruated to the witnesses and could not find them while she was in a remote place, it becomes binding upon her and she is not excused—this is carried on the case where she did not verbally annul until she acted. And what is also in it. In al-Dhakhirah that if she asked about the husband's name or about the mahr or greeted the witnesses, her option is nullified—this is arbitrary, no evidence for it. The utmost is that this state is like the state of the initial marriage. And if the virgin asked about the husband's name, it does not bind her, and likewise about the mahr, even though not mentioning it to her does not nullify that her silence is consent, contrary to the disagreement, for that is when she did not ask about it, because it is apparent that she is satisfied with any mahr, and the question negates its appearance in that; rather her consent depends on knowing its quantity. Likewise, greeting the arriving one does not indicate consent, how, and she only sent for the purpose of testifying to the annulment. In Fath al-Qadir: there is discussion, because the nullification of this option does not depend on what indicates consent, because that is only regarding the thayyib and the boy; as for the virgin, it is nullified by mere silence, and there is no doubt that engaging in greeting is above silence. When the options of puberty and pre-emption come together, she says: I seek both rights. Then he begins the explanation with the option of puberty. It is restricted to the virgin because if she were a thayyib, as if the husband consummated before puberty or she was a thayyib at the time of contract, then it is not nullified by her silence; she is like the boy: consent is necessary by word or by a gesture indicating it. Likewise, as long as she does not enable him over herself, as clarified in al-Dhakhirah, and it appears that a month or two months is an example of a specific extent, for her right is established by testifying, so it is not dropped by delay like pre-emption. Ponder his statement: (And there is no doubt that engaging in greeting is above silence). He said in al-Nahr: This is denied, for they transmitted in pre-emption that his greeting the buyer does not nullify it because the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said 'greeting before speech' (1). And there is no doubt that seeking the fastest after knowledge of the sale is nullified by silence like the option of puberty; if it were above it, it would be nullified. And they said: If the buyer said 'I bought it and for how much you bought it,' his pre-emption is not nullified as in al-Bazzaziyyah. This supports what is in Fath al-Qadir. Yes, what he faced regarding the mahr only was not left; as for when he was alone with her in a valid seclusion, stopping at its quantity is an engagement in what is not beneficial because it is obligatory upon her, so the absolute statement of its non-lapse is not appropriate. End. In al-Rimz after quoting the author's discussion: The answer is that consent is necessary, but sometimes it is explicit and sometimes it is by indication for the thayyib and the virgin, but mere silence of the virgin is considered consent legally and stands in place of speech due to the cause of shyness. And I say: It should be said that if she asked about the husband's name while knowing him, or greeted meaningfully by saying 'welcome' to the witnesses and the like, it binds her because that is dispensable; as for if she refused their greeting or was ignorant of the husband, then asking about it is not like silence. The conclusion is that engaging in what is not beneficial stands in place of silence and binds her, not what she needs for this purpose. His statement: (And when the options of puberty and pre-emption come together, etc.) said (1) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Isti'dhan, Bab 11. And it is nullified by her silence if she knew, for a virgin except by his silence unless he said 'I am satisfied,' even by indication, and they inherit beforehand regarding competency, the mahr, but half is obligatory; so the truth is that no rule should be made for this issue, but judgment is made in each individual case according to what the evidence indicates. Then know that the separations are thirteen groups: seven of them need judicial process, and six do not. As for the first: separation due to impotence, separation due to barrenness, separation by option of puberty, separation due to lack, separation due to deficiency of mahr, separation due to the husband's refusal to embrace Islam, and separation by li'an. They depend on judicial process because they are based on a hidden cause: because competency is not known by sense and its causes are various; likewise deficiency of mahr of the like, and the option of puberty is based on deficiency of compassion which is an internal matter, and refusal may occur, likewise the rest. As for the second: separation by option of emancipation, separation by iyla, separation by apostasy, separation by difference of domiciles, separation by one spouse owning the other, and separation in a void marriage. These six do not depend on judicial process because they are based on a clear cause. Then Imam al-Mahbubi said in al-Tanqih: Every separation coming from the woman without a cause from the husband is a separation without divorce, like apostasy from the woman's side, option of puberty, option of emancipation, and lack of competence; and every separation coming from the husband is divorce, like iyla, impotence, and barrenness. And this does not require the husband's apostasy according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf because by apostasy ownership is negated, so the legality which is a consequent of ownership is negated; the separation occurs by contradiction and opposition, not by direct action from the husband, unlike refusal from the husband's side where it is divorce according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad because there is no contradiction, evidenced by ownership remaining with non-refusal; thus they differ. End. His statement: (And it is nullified by her silence if she knew, for a virgin; not by his silence unless he said 'I am satisfied,' even by indication) meaning: the option of puberty is nullified by the silence of the one who has attained puberty, considering this state like the state of the initial marriage; the silence of the virgin is consent initially, unlike the silence of the thayyib and the boy. By 'knowledge' he means knowledge of the basic marriage, because she cannot act without it and the guardian is independent with it, so she is excused; and knowledge that she has the option of puberty is not required because she devotes herself to learning the rulings of Shari'ah and it is a place of knowledge, so she is not excused by ignorance, unlike the emancipated slave woman because the slave girl does not devote herself to learning it, so she is excused by ignorance of the option's existence. It is understood from its nullification by her silence that it does not extend to the end of the sitting. On this basis, they said: It should be nullified with the sighting of blood; if she sees it at night, she seeks with her tongue and says: I annul my marriage, and testifies when morning comes and says: I saw the blood now. It was said to Muhammad: How can this be valid while it is a lie and she had attained puberty before this? He said: She is not truthful in attribution, so it is permissible for her to lie so that her right is not nullified. Then when she chooses and testifies and does not approach the qadi for a month or two months, it will come that it is a separation due to a cause not from the husband, so it cannot be made divorce, so it is made annulment. End. Its clarification is in its place. End. Ponder.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the rulings of khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) for a virgin girl. The author begins with a discussion of when the option is nullified. He refutes a view from al-Tabyin and al-Dhakhirah that asking about the husband's name or mahr or greeting nullifies the option, calling it arbitrary. The correct Hanafi position is that for a virgin, the option is nullified by mere silence (because her silence is considered consent due to shyness). However, asking about the husband's name or mahr does not nullify the option because it does not indicate consent; rather, it shows she is not satisfied until she knows the details. Greeting is also not a sign of consent, especially if her sole purpose was to annul. The passage then compares khiyar al-bulugh with khiyar al-shuf'a (pre-emption), noting that silence after knowledge nullifies in both cases. A key distinction is made between a virgin (bikr) and a thayyib (non-virgin) or boy; the latter require explicit verbal consent. The commentary also addresses the issue of timing: if the virgin stays silent but sees blood (sign of puberty), she still has the option until she either explicitly annuls or remains silent in knowledge of the contract. A permittance of lying in this context is discussed when she claims to have just seen the blood to preserve her option. Finally, the passage moves to a classification of separations (al-firaq). Thirteen types are listed: seven require qada (judicial decree) because they involve hidden causes (like impotence, deficiency, li'an), and six do not because they are based on clear causes (like iyla, apostasy, etc.). The author quotes al-Tanqih: separations from the woman's side are not divorce, while those from the husband's side are divorce, except apostasy of the husband according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (it is not divorce). This section provides a systematic overview of how different marital dissolutions are categorized in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option of the minor who is married by the guardian before puberty to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.
بكر (bikr): A virgin, a female who has not been previously married or had sexual intercourse.
ثيب (thayyib): A previously married woman (non-virgin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what is the effect of a virgin asking about the husband's name after reaching puberty?
A) It nullifies her option of puberty.
B) It does not nullify her option; she must still choose explicitly.
C) It confirms her consent and marriage becomes binding.
D) It delays the option until she knows the name.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's khiyar al-bulugh is nullified only by silence with knowledge of the marriage, not by questioning or greeting.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Classification of Marital Separations in Hanafi Fiqh
Systematic classification of thirteen types of marital separations into those requiring judicial process and those not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify marital separations into those requiring judicial process (al-qada') and those that do not, based on the clarity of the cause.

Core Idea
Thirteen types of marital separation are divided: seven with hidden causes need qada, six with clear causes do not.

This passage provides a systematic classification of all forms of marital dissolution (firaq) in Hanafi fiqh. The author lists thirteen types, distinguishing seven that require a judicial decree because their underlying cause is hidden (e.g., option of puberty, impotence), and six that do not because the cause is evident (e.g., iyla, apostasy, difference of domicile). He also quotes al-Tanqih to clarify which separations are considered divorce and which are simply annulments from the woman's side.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thirteen separation types
List all thirteen as given: seven in the first group, six in the second. Note the names of each separation.
The author provides a numbered list.
2
Separate into two groups: require qada vs not
The first seven require judicial process because the cause is hidden (khafiyy). The latter six do not because the cause is evident (jaliyy).
The hidden causes include: jabb (impotence), 'unnah (barrenness), khiyar al-bulugh, 'adam (lack of maintenance), nuqsan al-mahr (deficiency of mahr), iba' al-zawj 'an al-Islam (husband's refusal to embrace Islam), and li'an (mutual curse).
3
Understand why hidden causes require qada
These causes are not apparent to the senses; e.g., deficiency of mahr requires expert assessment, and khiyar al-bulugh depends on internal lack of compassion.
A judge verifies these conditions.
4
Note the clear cause group
The six clear separations are based on observable events: khiyar al-'atq (option of emancipation), iyla (oath of abstinence), riddah (apostasy), tabayun al-darayn (difference of domiciles), milk ahad al-zawjayn (one spouse owning the other), and nikah fasid (void marriage).
These are evident and do not need judicial verification.
5
Apply the rule from al-Tanqih
Imam al-Mahbubi states: separations from the wife's side without a cause from the husband are not divorce (e.g., apostasy, khiyar al-bulugh, lack of competence). Separations from the husband's side are divorce (e.g., iyla, impotence, barrenness). Exception: husband's apostasy according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is not divorce because ownership is negated.
This distinction affects legal consequences like iddah and maintenance.

فهي على خيارها كخيار العيب وما في التبيين من أنها لو بعثت خادمها حين حاضت للشهود فلم تقدر عليهم وهي في مكان منقطع لزمها ولم تعذر محمول على ما إذا لم تفسخ بلسانها حتى فعلت وما فيه أيضاً. وفي الذخيرة من أنها لو سألت عن اسم الزوج أو عن المهر أو سلمت على الشهود بطل خيارها، تعسف لا دليل عليه، وغاية الأمر كون هذه الحالة كحالة ابتداء النكاح. ولو سألت البكر عن اسم الزوج لا ينفذ عليها وكذا عن المهر وإن كان عدم ذكره لها لا يبطل كون سكوتها رضا على الخلاف فإن ذلك إذا لم تسأل عنه لظهور أنها راضية بكل مهر والسؤال يفيد نفي ظهوره في ذلك وإنما يتوقف رضاها على معرفة كميته، وكذا السلام على القادم لا يدل على الرضا، كيف وإنما أرسلت لغرض الإشهاد على الفسخ . في فتح القدير وفيه بحث، لأن بطلان هذا الخيار ليس متوقفاً على ما يدل على الرضا لأن ذلك إنما هو في حق الثيب والغلام وأما في حق البكر فيبطل بمجرد السكوت، ولا شك أن الاشتغال بالسلام فوق السكوت، وإذا اجتمع خيارا البلوغ والشفعة تقول أطلب الحقين ثم تبتدئ في التفسير بخيار البلوغ. وقيد بالبكر لأنها لو كانت ثيباً كما لو دخل بها الزوج قبل البلوغ أو كانت ثيباً وقت العقد فإنه لا يبطل بسكوتها فهي كالغلام لا بد من الرضا بالقول أو بفعل دال عليه . كذا ما لم تمكنه من نفسها كما صرح به في الذخيرة والظاهر أن الشهر والشهرين مثال لأحد مقدر إذ حقها تقرر بالإشهاد فلا يسقط بالتأخير كالشفعة تأمل قوله : ( ولا شك أن الاشتغال بالسلام فوق السكوت) قال في النهر : ممنوع فقد نقلوا في الشفعة أن سلامه على المشتري لا يبطلها لأنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم قال السلام» قبل الكلام (۱) ولا شك أن طلب المواثبة بعد العلم بالبيع يبطل بالسكوت كخيار البلوغ ولو كان فوقه لبطلت وقالوا: لو قال من اشتراها وبكم اشتراها لا تبطل شفعته كما في البزازية. وهذا يؤيد ما في فتح القدير، نعم ما وجه به في المهر إنما لم يخل أما إذا خلا بها خلوة صحيحة فالوقوف على كميته اشتغال بما لا يفيد لوجوبه بها، فإطلاق عدم سقوطه يتم إذا مما لا ينبغي ا هـ. وفي الرمز بعد نقل بحث المؤلف : والجواب أن الرضا لا بد منه لكنه تارة يكون صريحاً وتارة يكون دلالة في الثيب والبكر لكن مجرد السكوت من البكر جعل رضاً شرعاً وقام مقام القول لعلة الحياء. وأقول : ينبغي أن يقال إن سألت عن اسم الزوج مع علمها به أو سلمت معنى بأن قالت مرحباً للشهود ونحو ذلك يلزمها لكون ذلك مستغنى عنه، أما إذا ردت سلامهم أو كانت جاهلة بالزوج فالسؤال عنه لا يكون كالسكوت والحاصل أن اشتغالها بما لا يفيد يقوم مقام السكوت فيلزمها لا ما تحتاج إليه في هذا المقصود قوله : ( وإذا اجتمع خيار البلوغ والشفعة الخ) قال (۱) رواه الترمذي في كتاب الاستئذان باب ۱۱. ويبطل بسكوتها أن علمت بكر إلا بسكوته ما لم يقل رضيت ولو دلالة وتوارثا قبل الكفاءة، المهر بل يجب نصفه، فالحق أن لا يجعل لهذه المسألة ضابط بل يحكم في كل فرد بما أفاده الدليل . ثم اعلم أن الفرقة ثلاث عشرة فرقة : سبع منها تحتاج إلى القضاء، وست لا تحتاج . أما الأولى فالفرقة بالجب، والفرقة بالعنة والفرقة بخيار البلوغ، والفرقة بعدم والفرقة بنقصان المهر، والفرقة بإباء الزوج عن الإسلام والفرقة باللعان. وإنما توقفت على القضاء لأنها تنبني على سبب خفي لأن الكفاءة شيء لا يعرف بالحس وأسبابها مختلفة وكذا بنقصان مهر المثل، وخيار البلوغ مبني على قصور الشفقة وهو أمر باطن والإباء ربما يوجد وكذا البقية . وأما الثانية فالفرقة بخيار العتق والفرقة بالإيلاء، والفرقة بالرد، والفرقة بتباين الدارين والفرقة بملك أحد الزوجين صاحبه، والفرقة في النكاح الفاسد وإنما لم تتوقف هذه الست على القضاء لأنها تبتني على سبب جلي. ثم قال الإمام المحبوبي في التنقيح : كل فرقة جاءت من قبل المرأة لا بسبب من قبل الزوج فهي فرقة بغير طلاق كالردة من جهة المرأة وخيار البلوغ وخيار العتاقة وعدم الكفاءة، وكل فرقة جاءت من قبل الزوج فهي طلاق كالإيلاء والجب والعنة ولا يلزم على هذا ردة الزوج على قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لأن بالردة ينتفي الملك فينتفي الحل الذي هو من لوازم الملك فإنما حصلت الفرقة بالتنافي والتضاد لا بوجود المباشرة من الزوج بخلاف الإباء من جهة الزوج حيث يكون طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأنه لا تنافي بدليل أن الملك يبقى بعدم الإباء فلهذا افترقا اهـ . قوله: (ويبطل بسكوتها إن علمت بكراً لا بسكوته ما لم يقل رضيت ولو دلالة) أي ويبطل خيار البلوغ بسكوت من بلغت إلى آخره اعتباراً لهذه الحالة بحالة ابتداء النكاح، وسكوت البكر في الابتداء إذن بخلاف سكوت الثيب والغلام وأراد بالعلم العلم بأصل النكاح لأنه لا تتمكن من التصرف إلا به والولي ينفرد به فعذرت ولا يشترط العلم بأن لها خيار البلوغ لأنها تتفرغ لمعرفة أحكام الشرع والدار دار العلم فلم تعذر بالجهل بخلاف المعتقة لأن الأمة لا تتفرغ لمعرفتها فتعذر بالجهل بثبوت الخيار واستفيد من بطلانه بسكوتها أنه لا يمتد إلى آخر المجلس، وعلى هذا قالوا: ينبغي أن يبطل مع رؤية الدم فإن رأته ليلاً تطلب بلسانها فتقول فسخت نكاحي وتشهد إذا أصبحت وتقول رأيت الدم الآن. وقيل لمحمد : كيف يصح وهو كذب وإنما أدركت قبل هذا؟ فقال : لا تصدق في الإسناد فجاز لها أن تكذب كيلا يبطل حقها . ثم إذا اختارت وأشهدت ولم تتقدم إلى القاضي الشهر والشهرين وسيأتي لأنها فرقة حصلت بسبب لا من قبل الزوج فلا يمكن أن تجعل طلاقاً فتجعل فسخاً اهـ. إيضاحه في محله ا هـ فتأمل .
She is upon her option like the option of defect. What is in al-Tabyin that if she sent her servant when she menstruated to the witnesses and could not find them while she was in a remote place, it becomes binding upon her and she is not excused—this is carried on the case where she did not verbally annul until she acted. And what is also in it. In al-Dhakhirah that if she asked about the husband's name or about the mahr or greeted the witnesses, her option is nullified—this is arbitrary, no evidence for it. The utmost is that this state is like the state of the initial marriage. And if the virgin asked about the husband's name, it does not bind her, and likewise about the mahr, even though not mentioning it to her does not nullify that her silence is consent, contrary to the disagreement, for that is when she did not ask about it, because it is apparent that she is satisfied with any mahr, and the question negates its appearance in that; rather her consent depends on knowing its quantity. Likewise, greeting the arriving one does not indicate consent, how, and she only sent for the purpose of testifying to the annulment. In Fath al-Qadir: there is discussion, because the nullification of this option does not depend on what indicates consent, because that is only regarding the thayyib and the boy; as for the virgin, it is nullified by mere silence, and there is no doubt that engaging in greeting is above silence. When the options of puberty and pre-emption come together, she says: I seek both rights. Then he begins the explanation with the option of puberty. It is restricted to the virgin because if she were a thayyib, as if the husband consummated before puberty or she was a thayyib at the time of contract, then it is not nullified by her silence; she is like the boy: consent is necessary by word or by a gesture indicating it. Likewise, as long as she does not enable him over herself, as clarified in al-Dhakhirah, and it appears that a month or two months is an example of a specific extent, for her right is established by testifying, so it is not dropped by delay like pre-emption. Ponder his statement: (And there is no doubt that engaging in greeting is above silence). He said in al-Nahr: This is denied, for they transmitted in pre-emption that his greeting the buyer does not nullify it because the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said 'greeting before speech' (1). And there is no doubt that seeking the fastest after knowledge of the sale is nullified by silence like the option of puberty; if it were above it, it would be nullified. And they said: If the buyer said 'I bought it and for how much you bought it,' his pre-emption is not nullified as in al-Bazzaziyyah. This supports what is in Fath al-Qadir. Yes, what he faced regarding the mahr only was not left; as for when he was alone with her in a valid seclusion, stopping at its quantity is an engagement in what is not beneficial because it is obligatory upon her, so the absolute statement of its non-lapse is not appropriate. End. In al-Rimz after quoting the author's discussion: The answer is that consent is necessary, but sometimes it is explicit and sometimes it is by indication for the thayyib and the virgin, but mere silence of the virgin is considered consent legally and stands in place of speech due to the cause of shyness. And I say: It should be said that if she asked about the husband's name while knowing him, or greeted meaningfully by saying 'welcome' to the witnesses and the like, it binds her because that is dispensable; as for if she refused their greeting or was ignorant of the husband, then asking about it is not like silence. The conclusion is that engaging in what is not beneficial stands in place of silence and binds her, not what she needs for this purpose. His statement: (And when the options of puberty and pre-emption come together, etc.) said (1) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Isti'dhan, Bab 11. And it is nullified by her silence if she knew, for a virgin except by his silence unless he said 'I am satisfied,' even by indication, and they inherit beforehand regarding competency, the mahr, but half is obligatory; so the truth is that no rule should be made for this issue, but judgment is made in each individual case according to what the evidence indicates. Then know that the separations are thirteen groups: seven of them need judicial process, and six do not. As for the first: separation due to impotence, separation due to barrenness, separation by option of puberty, separation due to lack, separation due to deficiency of mahr, separation due to the husband's refusal to embrace Islam, and separation by li'an. They depend on judicial process because they are based on a hidden cause: because competency is not known by sense and its causes are various; likewise deficiency of mahr of the like, and the option of puberty is based on deficiency of compassion which is an internal matter, and refusal may occur, likewise the rest. As for the second: separation by option of emancipation, separation by iyla, separation by apostasy, separation by difference of domiciles, separation by one spouse owning the other, and separation in a void marriage. These six do not depend on judicial process because they are based on a clear cause. Then Imam al-Mahbubi said in al-Tanqih: Every separation coming from the woman without a cause from the husband is a separation without divorce, like apostasy from the woman's side, option of puberty, option of emancipation, and lack of competence; and every separation coming from the husband is divorce, like iyla, impotence, and barrenness. And this does not require the husband's apostasy according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf because by apostasy ownership is negated, so the legality which is a consequent of ownership is negated; the separation occurs by contradiction and opposition, not by direct action from the husband, unlike refusal from the husband's side where it is divorce according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad because there is no contradiction, evidenced by ownership remaining with non-refusal; thus they differ. End. His statement: (And it is nullified by her silence if she knew, for a virgin; not by his silence unless he said 'I am satisfied,' even by indication) meaning: the option of puberty is nullified by the silence of the one who has attained puberty, considering this state like the state of the initial marriage; the silence of the virgin is consent initially, unlike the silence of the thayyib and the boy. By 'knowledge' he means knowledge of the basic marriage, because she cannot act without it and the guardian is independent with it, so she is excused; and knowledge that she has the option of puberty is not required because she devotes herself to learning the rulings of Shari'ah and it is a place of knowledge, so she is not excused by ignorance, unlike the emancipated slave woman because the slave girl does not devote herself to learning it, so she is excused by ignorance of the option's existence. It is understood from its nullification by her silence that it does not extend to the end of the sitting. On this basis, they said: It should be nullified with the sighting of blood; if she sees it at night, she seeks with her tongue and says: I annul my marriage, and testifies when morning comes and says: I saw the blood now. It was said to Muhammad: How can this be valid while it is a lie and she had attained puberty before this? He said: She is not truthful in attribution, so it is permissible for her to lie so that her right is not nullified. Then when she chooses and testifies and does not approach the qadi for a month or two months, it will come that it is a separation due to a cause not from the husband, so it cannot be made divorce, so it is made annulment. End. Its clarification is in its place. End. Ponder.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the rulings of khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) for a virgin girl. The author begins with a discussion of when the option is nullified. He refutes a view from al-Tabyin and al-Dhakhirah that asking about the husband's name or mahr or greeting nullifies the option, calling it arbitrary. The correct Hanafi position is that for a virgin, the option is nullified by mere silence (because her silence is considered consent due to shyness). However, asking about the husband's name or mahr does not nullify the option because it does not indicate consent; rather, it shows she is not satisfied until she knows the details. Greeting is also not a sign of consent, especially if her sole purpose was to annul. The passage then compares khiyar al-bulugh with khiyar al-shuf'a (pre-emption), noting that silence after knowledge nullifies in both cases. A key distinction is made between a virgin (bikr) and a thayyib (non-virgin) or boy; the latter require explicit verbal consent. The commentary also addresses the issue of timing: if the virgin stays silent but sees blood (sign of puberty), she still has the option until she either explicitly annuls or remains silent in knowledge of the contract. A permittance of lying in this context is discussed when she claims to have just seen the blood to preserve her option. Finally, the passage moves to a classification of separations (al-firaq). Thirteen types are listed: seven require qada (judicial decree) because they involve hidden causes (like impotence, deficiency, li'an), and six do not because they are based on clear causes (like iyla, apostasy, etc.). The author quotes al-Tanqih: separations from the woman's side are not divorce, while those from the husband's side are divorce, except apostasy of the husband according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (it is not divorce). This section provides a systematic overview of how different marital dissolutions are categorized in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الفرقة (al-firqa): Marital separation or dissolution.
القضاء (al-qada'): Judicial process or decree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many types of marital separation in Hanafi fiqh require judicial process (qada)?
A) 13
B) 7
C) 6
D) 5

Key Takeaway
Marital separations are classified into seven with hidden causes (need qada) and six with clear causes (no qada); husband's apostasy is an exception.
