Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Classification of Marital Separations and Their Legal Nature
Classification of thirteen types of furqa with legal analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the thirteen types of marital separation in Hanafi fiqh into those requiring judicial decree and those not, and explain the legal distinction between talaq and faskh based on the cause of separation.

Core Idea
Not all marital separations are talaq; many are faskh (annulments) based on the cause and who initiates.

The passage from Bahr al-Raiq systematically lists thirteen types of separation (furqa) and divides them into two categories: seven that require a qadi's decree and six that do not. It then establishes a principle: separations originating from the wife without a cause from the husband are faskh, while those from the husband are talaq, with the exception of apostasy according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 13 types of furqa
List all thirteen separations mentioned in the passage under their two groups.
Memorize the names: jabb, 'inna, khiyar al-bulugh, lack of kafa'a, deficiency of mahr, refusal to embrace Islam, li'an (first seven); khiyar al-'itq, ila', apostasy, difference of abodes, one spouse owning the other, void marriage (last six).
2
Categorize by need for qada
Separate the first seven (require qada) from the last six (do not require qada). Understand why: hidden cause vs. manifest cause.
The cause is hidden (khafi) in the first seven, e.g., kafa'a is not known by senses; manifest (jali) in the last six, e.g., emancipation is obvious.
3
Apply the principle from al-Tanqih
State the principle: separations from the wife without husband's cause are faskh; from the husband are talaq. Apply to each type.
For example, khiyar al-bulugh (wife's option) is faskh; jabb (husband's condition) is talaq.
4
Analyze the exception: husband's apostasy
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say apostasy is faskh; Muhammad al-Shaybani may consider it talaq. Understand the reasoning: apostasy negates ownership (milk), so it is not a direct action of the husband.
Contrast with the husband's refusal to embrace Islam, which is talaq because ownership persists.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if talaq, the husband can revoke during 'iddah; if faskh, no revocation and the marriage ends immediately.
The mufta bihi view is that apostasy is faskh (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).

ثم اعلم أن الفرقة ثلاث عشرة فرقة : سبع منها تحتاج إلى القضاء، وست لا تحتاج . أما الأولى فالفرقة بالجب، والفرقة بالعنة والفرقة بخيار البلوغ، والفرقة بعدم والفرقة بنقصان المهر، والفرقة بإباء الزوج عن الإسلام والفرقة باللعان. وإنما توقفت على القضاء لأنها تنبني على سبب خفي لأن الكفاءة شيء لا يعرف بالحس وأسبابها مختلفة وكذا بنقصان مهر المثل، وخيار البلوغ مبني على قصور الشفقة وهو أمر باطن والإباء ربما يوجد يوجد وكذا البقية . وأما الثانية فالفرقة بخيار العتق والفرقة بالإيلاء، والفرقة بالرد، والفرقة بتباين الدارين والفرقة بملك أحد الزوجين صاحبه، والفرقة في النكاح الفاسد وإنما لم تتوقف هذه الست على القضاء لأنها تبتني على سبب جلي. ثم قال الإمام المحبوبي في التنقيح : كل فرقة جاءت من قبل المرأة لا بسبب من قبل الزوج فهي فرقة بغير طلاق كالردة من جهة المرأة وخيار البلوغ وخيار العتاقة وعدم الكفاءة، وكل فرقة جاءت من قبل الزوج فهي طلاق كالإيلاء والجب والعنة ولا يلزم على هذا ردة الزوج على قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لأن بالردة ينتفي الملك فينتفي الحل الذي هو من لوازم الملك فإنما حصلت الفرقة بالتنافي والتضاد لا بوجود المباشرة من الزوج بخلاف الإباء من جهة الزوج حيث يكون طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأنه لا تنافي بدليل أن الملك يبقى بعدم الإباء فلهذا افترقا اهـ .
Then know that the separation is thirteen types: seven of them require the judiciary, and six do not. As for the first: separation due to impotence (jabb), separation due to 'inna (chronic impotence), separation by option of puberty, separation due to lack [of kafa'a], separation due to deficiency of mahr, separation due to the husband's refusal to embrace Islam, and separation by li'an. They depend on judicial decree because they are based on a hidden cause, for kafa'a is something not known by sense and its causes vary, likewise deficiency of proper mahr, and option of puberty is based on deficiency of affection which is an inward matter, and refusal may occur, and similarly the rest. As for the second: separation by option of emancipation, separation by ila', separation by apostasy, separation by difference of abodes, separation by one spouse owning the other, and separation in a void marriage. These six do not depend on judicial decree because they are based on a manifest cause. Then Imam al-Mahbubi said in al-Tanqih: Every separation that comes from the woman without a cause from the husband is a separation without divorce, like apostasy from the woman's side, option of puberty, option of emancipation, and lack of kafa'a; and every separation that comes from the husband is a divorce, like ila', jabb, and 'inna. This is not contradicted by the husband's apostasy according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, because by apostasy ownership is negated, so the permissibility which is a necessary attribute of ownership is negated; thus the separation occurs by mutual negation and opposition, not by the husband's direct action, unlike the husband's refusal where it is a divorce according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad because there is no opposition, as evidenced by the fact that ownership remains despite refusal; hence they differed.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a classification of marital separations (firaq) and an explanation of their legal character. It divides thirteen types of separation into two categories: seven that require a judicial decree (qada) and six that do not. The reason for requiring qada is that the cause is hidden (khafi) and not ascertainable without investigation. For example, kafa'a (suitability in marriage) is a subtle matter; likewise deficiency of proper mahr, and the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) depends on an inward lack of affection. The second category of six separations has a manifest (jali) cause, so no judicial decree is needed. Then the principle from al-Tanqih is quoted: any separation initiated by the wife without a cause from the husband is a faskh (annulment), not a divorce; any separation initiated by the husband is a talaq (divorce). This principle is applied with an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the husband's apostasy is not a divorce because it negates ownership itself, making the separation a faskh. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the husband's refusal to embrace Islam is a divorce because ownership persists despite the refusal, creating a contradiction that the husband resolves by his refusal, thus it is his direct action.

Key Terms
فرقة (furqa): Separation between spouses dissolving the marriage bond.
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): Option of puberty: the right of a minor married by a guardian other than father/grandfather to annul the marriage upon attaining majority.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of marriage without the pronouncement of talaq, often by judicial decree or by operation of law.
كفاءة (kafa'a): Suitability in marriage, considering lineage, religion, profession, etc.
عنّة (‘inna): Chronic impotence, a defect that gives the wife a right to seek annulment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which of the following separations does NOT require a judicial decree (qada)?
A) Separation due to impotence (jabb)
B) Separation by li'an
C) Separation by option of emancipation (khiyar al-'itq)
D) Separation due to deficiency of mahr

Key Takeaway
Thirteen types of marital separation are classified by cause and legal nature; seven require qada due to hidden causes, and separations from the wife are faskh, while those from the husband are talaq, with apostasy being a key exception per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Nikah — Marriage

The Option of Puberty: When Silence Invalidates
Details khiyar al-bulugh and its abrogation by silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which khiyar al-bulugh is invalidated by silence and apply the Hanafi qiyas to distinguish between a virgin and a non-virgin.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence upon reaching puberty waives her option to annul — just as it grants consent to marry.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-bulugh, the right of a minor given in marriage by a non-father/grandfather guardian to annul upon puberty. The key point is that this option is not indefinite; it can be lost by silence if the woman is a virgin and knows of the marriage. The Hanafi school draws an analogy (qiyas) to the initial marriage contract, where a virgin's silence is taken as consent. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the conditions: who is considered a virgin, what knowledge is required, and how the option is timed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its exception
The passage states that the option of puberty is invalidated by the silence of a virgin who knows of the marriage. This is the general rule; note that the rule does not apply to a non-virgin or a male minor.
Non-virgin's silence does not invalidate; she must explicitly annul.
2
Understand the qiyas (analogy) to initiation of marriage
The Hanafi position is based on comparing this situation to the initial marriage contract, where a virgin's silence is considered permission (by hadith). The same effective cause (illa) — a virgin's shyness — applies in both contexts.
The hadith 'The virgin's silence is her permission' is the proof for the qiyas.
3
Determine the knowledge required
The passage clarifies that the virgin must know of the marriage itself, but she need not know that she has the option of puberty. Ignorance of the option is not excused because she is expected to learn religious rulings.
This distinguishes her from a slave woman, who may be excused due to lack of opportunity to learn.
4
Apply the timing: the option is immediate
The invalidation by silence shows that the option does not extend to the end of the sitting; it is immediate. If she sees blood indicating puberty at night, she must verbally annul immediately, and may even use a white lie in testimony to protect her right.
The permissibility of the white lie is specific to this context to avoid losing the right.
5
Distinguish between a virgin and others
The rule applies only to a virgin. The silence of a non-virgin or a male minor does not invalidate the option; they must express consent or annul explicitly.
The reasoning: a non-virgin is not overcome by shyness, so her silence does not imply consent.

أو بعضها فيه نظر، ففي البدائع : الفرقة الواقعة بملكه إياها أو شقصاً منها فرقة بغير طلاق
His saying: (And it is invalidated by her silence if she knows, [if] a virgin, not by his silence unless he says 'I am pleased' even by implication) meaning: the option of puberty is invalidated by the silence of the one who has attained puberty etc., considering this situation like the situation of initiating marriage, and the silence of a virgin in initiation is permission, unlike the silence of a non-virgin and a boy. He intended by 'knowledge' knowledge of the original marriage, because she cannot act except by it, and the guardian is alone in it, so she is excused. And knowledge that she has the option of puberty is not required because she is free to learn the rulings of Shari'ah and the abode is an abode of knowledge, so she is not excused by ignorance, unlike the emancipated slave woman because the slave woman is not free to know it, so she is excused by ignorance of the existence of the option. And it is derived from its invalidation by her silence that it does not extend to the end of the sitting. Based on this they said: It should be invalidated upon seeing blood. If she sees it at night, she should verbally seek it, saying 'I have annulled my marriage,' and testify when morning comes and say 'I saw the blood now.' It was said to Muhammad: How is this valid when it is a lie and she actually attained puberty before this? He said: She is not considered truthful in attribution, so it is permissible for her to lie so that her right is not invalidated. Then when she chooses and makes witnesses and does not approach the judge for a month or two months, it will come [later] because it is a separation that occurred due to a cause not from the husband, so it cannot be made a divorce, so it is made an annulment. Its clarification is in its place. Then his saying: (Then when she chooses and makes witnesses and does not approach the judge for a month or two months) al-Ramli said: meaning the 'iddah, and a woman observing 'iddah due to annulment is not subject to another divorce during the 'iddah. But in our specific issue, it was mentioned that it does occur. As for the ruling of mahr: if the separation occurred after consummation, even constructively, the full mahr is obligatory; if before consummation, then no mahr for her. If it is from her [side], it is apparent because it came from her; if it is from him [the husband], then its falling due is the benefit of the option for him, otherwise there is no benefit in establishing it for him since he possesses divorce. He said in al-Ikhtiyar: There is no separation that comes from the husband before consummation without mahr upon him except this one. But this exclusivity is incorrect because of what is in al-Dhakhira from the twenty-sixth chapter on miscellaneous matters before the Book of Nafaqat: A free man marries a kitabiyyah slave with her master's permission for a specific slave-girl, then the kitabiyyah slave does not take possession of the slave-girl until she marries her to her husband for one hundred dirhams; both marriages are valid. If the husband divorces the kitabiyyah first, then divorces the slave-girl, the divorce falls on the kitabiyyah but not on the slave-girl because by divorcing the kitabiyyah, the slave-girl becomes half (?) and half of her returns to the husband by the very divorce, so the marriage of the slave-girl becomes corrupted before the divorce reaches her, so her divorce is ineffective. And all mahr is void from the husband even though it is a separation from the husband before consummation, because if the separation is from the husband, it does not drop all mahr only if it is a divorce. But if the separation from him before consummation is an annulment in every respect, it necessitates dropping all dower, like the minor when he attains puberty. Also, if he purchases his married wife before consummation, all dower drops even though the separation comes from him, because the invalidity of the marriage is a ruling attached to ownership, and every ruling attached to ownership is referred to the acceptance of the buyer, not to the obligation of the seller. All dower drops because it is an annulment in every respect. This is his wording. This refutes the author of al-Dhakhira: if the husband apostatizes before consummation, the separation is an annulment in every respect, yet not all mahr drops, as per the case of the wife of an apostate husband because the prohibition due to apostasy is not perpetual since it can be removed by Islam, so his divorce falls on her during the 'iddah carrying its benefit of making her prohibited to him after three divorces, a prohibition ending by intercourse with another husband, unlike the prohibition of mahram which is perpetual, so the attachment of divorce brings no benefit. This was the point that occurrence here is more appropriate for one who reflects, except that it necessitates restricting the non-occurrence during 'iddah to cases where the separation is due to a cause that necessitates perpetual prohibition, like kissing and breastfeeding, and this contradicts the apparent wording of the scholars, as known by one who examines it. This is because they explicitly stated non-attachment in the 'iddah of the option of emancipation and puberty, and also non-kafa'a and deficiency of mahr, until al-Fath explicitly stated that at the beginning of the Book of Divorce: attachment applies when one of the spouses is captured or emigrates to us as a Muslim or dhimmi, or both come out as musta'mins and one of them embraces Islam or becomes dhimmi. He also explicitly stated there attachment of divorce when separation occurs due to the other's refusal and apostasy, and said: The separation due to his apostasy is annulment, contrary to Abu Yusuf, and if she is the apostate, it is annulment and his divorce falls on her during the 'iddah. He did not give the same reasoning as in the marriage book. And he explicitly stated non-attachment by agreement. His saying: (Also, if he purchases his married wife) al-Nahr said: Regarding the claim that the separation is from him when he owns her or part of her, there is consideration. In al-Bada'i': The separation occurring by his ownership of her or a share of her is a separation without divorce.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on rulings related to khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) and the consequences of separation on mahr. It begins by explaining that the option of puberty is invalidated by the silence of the virgin if she knows of the marriage, analogizing to the initial marriage where silence indicates consent. The knowledge required is knowledge of the marriage itself, not necessarily of the option. A virgin is not excused for ignorance of the option because she is expected to learn religious rulings, unlike a slave woman who may be excused. If she sees blood (indicating puberty) at night, she must verbally annul immediately and then testify in the morning; she may state she saw blood now even if it is a lie, because she is not considered truthful in attribution, and this protects her right. Then it discusses mahr: if separation occurs after consummation, full mahr is due; before consummation, no mahr. If separation is from the wife, no mahr; if from the husband, then the falling of mahr is the benefit of the option for him. The text then critiques a claim from al-Ikhtiyar that the only separation from the husband before consummation without mahr is this one, citing examples from al-Dhakhira where separation from the husband before consummation drops all mahr because it is an annulment (faskh) from every aspect. Examples include the case of a husband marrying a kitabiyyah slave and then divorcing in a specific sequence, and purchasing one's wife. Then it discusses the case of apostasy before consummation and the difference between perpetual and non-perpetual prohibitions affecting whether divorce can occur during 'iddah. The passage ends with citations from other sources about attachment/non-attachment of divorce during 'iddah in various separations.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The right of a person married as a minor (by a non-father/grandfather guardian) to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of marriage that is not a talaq; it nullifies the marriage from its inception or dissolves it without counting as a divorce.
مهر (mahr): Dower or bridal gift, obligatory upon the husband to pay to the wife upon marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the effect of a virgin's silence upon attaining puberty and knowing of her marriage?
A) It invalidates her option of puberty (the marriage remains valid).
B) It confirms her option to annul.
C) It has no effect; she must speak explicitly.
D) It invalidates the marriage entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school analogizes a virgin's silence in khiyar al-bulugh to her silence in initial marriage, both indicating consent, and thus her silence invalidates the option.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced

Mahr Upon Separation: When the Full Dower Drops Before Consummation
Details mahr consequences upon separation, especially faskh from husband before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when mahr is fully due, when it drops entirely before consummation, and distinguish between talaq and faskh in mahr obligations.

Core Idea
Before consummation, mahr drops entirely if the separation is a faskh from the husband — but not if it is a divorce.

This lesson examines the mahr implications when a marriage ends before consummation. The general rule is that if separation occurs after consummation, full mahr is due; before consummation, no mahr is due if the separation is from the wife, but if from the husband, the outcome depends on whether the separation is a talaq (divorce) or a faskh (annulment). In Hanafi fiqh, when the separation is a faskh from every aspect, the entire mahr drops. This lesson explores the unique case of khiyar al-bulugh exercised by a husband, critiques the claim that it is the only such case, and discusses the apostasy exception.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for mahr after separation
After consummation (even constructively), full mahr is obligatory. Before consummation, no mahr is due if the separation is from the wife; if from the husband, the rule is nuanced.
The default for husband-initiated separation before consummation is half mahr if it is a divorce.
2
Identify the exception: faskh from the husband drops all mahr
The passage presents the case of khiyar al-bulugh exercised by the husband (or where the separation is from him) as an example where before consummation, all mahr drops because it is an annulment (faskh) from every aspect.
This is the benefit of the option for the husband; otherwise, he would have to pay half mahr if he divorced.
3
Critique the claim of exclusivity
The text from al-Ikhtiyar claims that this is the only separation from the husband before consummation with no mahr. The author refutes this by citing examples from al-Dhakhira where other faskh cases (e.g., buying one's wife, or the complex slave-girl marriage scenario) also result in all mahr dropping.
The key is whether the separation is a faskh in every respect, not just a divorce.
4
Analyze the apostasy case as a counterexample
Apostasy before consummation is a faskh, yet mahr does not drop entirely because the prohibition is not perpetual; divorce can still occur during 'iddah, so it is not a complete faskh. This shows that not all faskh drops mahr.
This clarifies that the rule applies only to faskh that is absolute and perpetual.
5
Distinguish between perpetual and non-perpetual prohibitions
The passage explains that if the prohibition is perpetual (like mahram), the separation is a complete annulment and mahr drops. If the prohibition can be lifted (like apostasy by returning to Islam), divorce can still occur, so mahr does not drop.
This distinction affects whether divorce can be pronounced during 'iddah after a faskh.

مائة درهم جاز النكاحان، فإن طلق الزوج المكاتبة أولاً ثم طلق الأمة وقع الطلاق على المكاتبة ولا يقع على الأمة لأن بطلاق المكاتبة تتنصف الأمة وعاد نصفها إلى الزوج بنفس الطلاق فيفسد نكاح الأمة قبل ورود الطلاق عليها فلم يعمل طلاقها، ويبطل جميع مهر عن الزوج مع أنها فرقة جاءت من قبل الزوج قبل الدخول بها لأن الفرقة إذا كانت من قبل الزوج إنما لا تسقط كل المهر إذا كان طلاقاً، وأما إذا كانت الفرقة من قبله قبل الدخول وكانت فسخاً من كل وجه توجب سقوط كل الصداق كالصغير إذا بلغ . وأيضاً لو اشترى منكوحته قبل الدخول بها فإنه يسقط كل الصداق مع أن الفرقة جاءت من قبله لأن فساد النكاح حكم تعلق بالملك، وكل حكم تعلق بالملك فإنه يحال على قبول المشتري لا على إيجاب البائع، وإنما سقط كل الصداق لأنه فسخ من كل وجه .اهـ. بلفظه. ويرد على صاحب الذخيرة إذا ارتد الزوج قبل الدخول فإنها فرقة هي فسخ من كل وجه مع أنه لم يسقط كل الأمة من زوج المرتدة بأن الحرمة بالردة غير متأبدة لارتفاعها بالإسلام فيقع طلاقه عليها في العدة مستتبعاً فائدته من حرمتها عليه بعد الثلاث حرمة مغياة بوطء زوج آخر بخلاف حرمة المحرمية فإنها متأبدة فلا يفيد لحوق الطلاق فائدة ا.هـ. وكان هذا هو وجه كون الوقوع هنا أوجه لمن تأمل إلا أنه يقتضي قصر عدم الوقوع في العدة على ما إذا كانت الفرقة بما يوجب حرمة مؤبدة كالتقبيل وكالإرضاع وفيه مخالفة لظاهر كلامهم عرف ذلك من تصفحه ا هـ. وذلك أنهم صرحوا بعدم اللحاق في عدة خيار العتق والبلوغ، وكذا بعدم الكفاءة ونقصان المهر حتى صرح بذلك في الفتح أول كتاب الطلاق، اللحاق فيما إذا سبي أحد الزوجين أو هاجر إلينا مسلماً أو ذمياً أو خرجاً مستأمنين فأسلم أحدهما أو صار ذمياً. وصرح أ أيضاً هناك بلحاق الطلاق فيما إذا فرق بينهما بإباء الآخر وبالارتداد وقال : إن الفرقة بردته فسخ خلافاً لأبي يوسف ولو كانت هي المرتدة فهي فسخ ويقع طلاقه عليها في العدة ولم يعلل بما علل به في النكاح . وصرح أيضاً بعدم اللحاق اتفاقاً. قوله : (وأيضاً لو اشترى منكوحته الخ قال في النهر : في دعوى كون الفرقة من قبله فيما إذا ملكها أو بعضها فيه نظر، ففي البدائع : الفرقة الواقعة بملكه إياها أو شقصاً منها فرقة بغير طلاق
His saying: (And it is invalidated by her silence if she knows, [if] a virgin, not by his silence unless he says 'I am pleased' even by implication) meaning: the option of puberty is invalidated by the silence of the one who has attained puberty etc., considering this situation like the situation of initiating marriage, and the silence of a virgin in initiation is permission, unlike the silence of a non-virgin and a boy. He intended by 'knowledge' knowledge of the original marriage, because she cannot act except by it, and the guardian is alone in it, so she is excused. And knowledge that she has the option of puberty is not required because she is free to learn the rulings of Shari'ah and the abode is an abode of knowledge, so she is not excused by ignorance, unlike the emancipated slave woman because the slave woman is not free to know it, so she is excused by ignorance of the existence of the option. And it is derived from its invalidation by her silence that it does not extend to the end of the sitting. Based on this they said: It should be invalidated upon seeing blood. If she sees it at night, she should verbally seek it, saying 'I have annulled my marriage,' and testify when morning comes and say 'I saw the blood now.' It was said to Muhammad: How is this valid when it is a lie and she actually attained puberty before this? He said: She is not considered truthful in attribution, so it is permissible for her to lie so that her right is not invalidated. Then when she chooses and makes witnesses and does not approach the judge for a month or two months, it will come [later] because it is a separation that occurred due to a cause not from the husband, so it cannot be made a divorce, so it is made an annulment. Its clarification is in its place. Then his saying: (Then when she chooses and makes witnesses and does not approach the judge for a month or two months) al-Ramli said: meaning the 'iddah, and a woman observing 'iddah due to annulment is not subject to another divorce during the 'iddah. But in our specific issue, it was mentioned that it does occur. As for the ruling of mahr: if the separation occurred after consummation, even constructively, the full mahr is obligatory; if before consummation, then no mahr for her. If it is from her [side], it is apparent because it came from her; if it is from him [the husband], then its falling due is the benefit of the option for him, otherwise there is no benefit in establishing it for him since he possesses divorce. He said in al-Ikhtiyar: There is no separation that comes from the husband before consummation without mahr upon him except this one. But this exclusivity is incorrect because of what is in al-Dhakhira from the twenty-sixth chapter on miscellaneous matters before the Book of Nafaqat: A free man marries a kitabiyyah slave with her master's permission for a specific slave-girl, then the kitabiyyah slave does not take possession of the slave-girl until she marries her to her husband for one hundred dirhams; both marriages are valid. If the husband divorces the kitabiyyah first, then divorces the slave-girl, the divorce falls on the kitabiyyah but not on the slave-girl because by divorcing the kitabiyyah, the slave-girl becomes half (?) and half of her returns to the husband by the very divorce, so the marriage of the slave-girl becomes corrupted before the divorce reaches her, so her divorce is ineffective. And all mahr is void from the husband even though it is a separation from the husband before consummation, because if the separation is from the husband, it does not drop all mahr only if it is a divorce. But if the separation from him before consummation is an annulment in every respect, it necessitates dropping all dower, like the minor when he attains puberty. Also, if he purchases his married wife before consummation, all dower drops even though the separation comes from him, because the invalidity of the marriage is a ruling attached to ownership, and every ruling attached to ownership is referred to the acceptance of the buyer, not to the obligation of the seller. All dower drops because it is an annulment in every respect. This is his wording. This refutes the author of al-Dhakhira: if the husband apostatizes before consummation, the separation is an annulment in every respect, yet not all mahr drops, as per the case of the wife of an apostate husband because the prohibition due to apostasy is not perpetual since it can be removed by Islam, so his divorce falls on her during the 'iddah carrying its benefit of making her prohibited to him after three divorces, a prohibition ending by intercourse with another husband, unlike the prohibition of mahram which is perpetual, so the attachment of divorce brings no benefit. This was the point that occurrence here is more appropriate for one who reflects, except that it necessitates restricting the non-occurrence during 'iddah to cases where the separation is due to a cause that necessitates perpetual prohibition, like kissing and breastfeeding, and this contradicts the apparent wording of the scholars, as known by one who examines it. This is because they explicitly stated non-attachment in the 'iddah of the option of emancipation and puberty, and also non-kafa'a and deficiency of mahr, until al-Fath explicitly stated that at the beginning of the Book of Divorce: attachment applies when one of the spouses is captured or emigrates to us as a Muslim or dhimmi, or both come out as musta'mins and one of them embraces Islam or becomes dhimmi. He also explicitly stated there attachment of divorce when separation occurs due to the other's refusal and apostasy, and said: The separation due to his apostasy is annulment, contrary to Abu Yusuf, and if she is the apostate, it is annulment and his divorce falls on her during the 'iddah. He did not give the same reasoning as in the marriage book. And he explicitly stated non-attachment by agreement. His saying: (Also, if he purchases his married wife) al-Nahr said: Regarding the claim that the separation is from him when he owns her or part of her, there is consideration. In al-Bada'i': The separation occurring by his ownership of her or a share of her is a separation without divorce.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on rulings related to khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) and the consequences of separation on mahr. It begins by explaining that the option of puberty is invalidated by the silence of the virgin if she knows of the marriage, analogizing to the initial marriage where silence indicates consent. The knowledge required is knowledge of the marriage itself, not necessarily of the option. A virgin is not excused for ignorance of the option because she is expected to learn religious rulings, unlike a slave woman who may be excused. If she sees blood (indicating puberty) at night, she must verbally annul immediately and then testify in the morning; she may state she saw blood now even if it is a lie, because she is not considered truthful in attribution, and this protects her right. Then it discusses mahr: if separation occurs after consummation, full mahr is due; before consummation, no mahr. If separation is from the wife, no mahr; if from the husband, then the falling of mahr is the benefit of the option for him. The text then critiques a claim from al-Ikhtiyar that the only separation from the husband before consummation without mahr is this one, citing examples from al-Dhakhira where separation from the husband before consummation drops all mahr because it is an annulment (faskh) from every aspect. Examples include the case of a husband marrying a kitabiyyah slave and then divorcing in a specific sequence, and purchasing one's wife. Then it discusses the case of apostasy before consummation and the difference between perpetual and non-perpetual prohibitions affecting whether divorce can occur during 'iddah. The passage ends with citations from other sources about attachment/non-attachment of divorce during 'iddah in various separations.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The right of a person married as a minor (by a non-father/grandfather guardian) to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of marriage that is not a talaq; it nullifies the marriage from its inception or dissolves it without counting as a divorce.
مهر (mahr): Dower or bridal gift, obligatory upon the husband to pay to the wife upon marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, which of the following separations before consummation results in no mahr being due?
A) Husband divorces his wife.
B) Husband apostatizes from Islam.
C) Husband exercises khiyar al-bulugh (having been a minor at marriage).
D) Wife apostatizes from Islam.

Key Takeaway
Before consummation, mahr drops entirely when the separation is a faskh from every aspect; the classic example is khiyar al-bulugh exercised by the husband.

Divorce in 'Iddah after Faskh: Exception Cases
Determining whether divorce occurs in 'iddah after annulment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between types of faskh that permit or prevent divorce during 'iddah and identify the underlying principle of benefit.

Core Idea
Divorce can occur in 'iddah after faskh only if the prohibition is temporary, not perpetual.

This passage examines an exception to the general Hanafi rule that divorce does not take effect during the 'iddah of an annulled marriage. While normally a woman in 'iddat al-faskh cannot be divorced, the author discusses a specific case—apostasy—where divorce does occur. The key distinction lies in whether the prohibition that caused the annulment is permanent (like mahram) or temporary (like apostasy, which ends with repentance). Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of talaq in various marital breakdown scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The author first states the general rule: divorce does not occur during 'iddah after faskh, whereas it does occur during 'iddah after talaq. Note this baseline.
2
Locate the exception
The author mentions that in the specific case under discussion, divorce does occur. This is the exception being analyzed.
The exact case is apostasy of the husband.
3
Examine the dower implications
The passage then discusses whether the full dower is due. If separation is before consummation, the dower is cancelled only if the separation is a faskh from every aspect, like a minor's option or purchase of spouse.
This is used to contrast with apostasy where dower is not cancelled.
4
Understand the underlying reason (ta'lil)
The author explains that the difference hinges on whether the prohibition is perpetual. Apostasy is not perpetual—it can be removed by Islam—so divorce can achieve its purpose (making her unlawful until she marries another). Mahram prohibition is perpetual, so divorce serves no benefit and does not attach.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Use the principle of benefit: if divorce would achieve a lasting effect, it occurs; if not, it does not. This resolves the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتدة بعدة الطلاق يلحقها طلاق آخر في العدة، والمعتدة بعدة الفسخ لا يلحقها طلاق آخر في العدة. وذكر في خصوص مسألتنا أنه يقع، وأما حكم المهر فإن كانت الفرقة بعد الدخول ولو حكماً وجب تمامه، وإن كانت قبله فلا مهر لها، فإن كانت منها فظاهر لأنها جاءت من قبلها وإن كانت منه فسقوطه هو فائدة الخيار له وإلا فلا فائدة في إثباته له إذ هو مالك للطلاق. قال في الاختيار : وليس لنا فرقة جاءت من قبل الزوج قبل الدخول ولا مهر عليه إلا في هذه اهـ. وهذا الحصر غير صحيح لما في الذخيرة من الفصل السادس والعشرين في المتفرقات قبيل كتاب النفقات : حر تزوج مكاتبة بإذن سيدها على جارية بعينها فلم تقبض المكاتبة الجارية حتى زوجتها من زوجها على مائة درهم جاز النكاحان، فإن طلق الزوج المكاتبة أولاً ثم طلق الأمة وقع الطلاق على المكاتبة ولا يقع على الأمة لأن بطلاق المكاتبة تتنصف الأمة وعاد نصفها إلى الزوج بنفس الطلاق فيفسد نكاح الأمة قبل ورود الطلاق عليها فلم يعمل طلاقها، ويبطل جميع مهر عن الزوج مع أنها فرقة جاءت من قبل الزوج قبل الدخول بها لأن الفرقة إذا كانت من قبل الزوج إنما لا تسقط كل المهر إذا كان طلاقاً، وأما إذا كانت الفرقة من قبله قبل الدخول وكانت فسخاً من كل وجه توجب سقوط كل الصداق كالصغير إذا بلغ . وأيضاً لو اشترى منكوحته قبل الدخول بها فإنه يسقط كل الصداق مع أن الفرقة جاءت من قبله لأن فساد النكاح حكم تعلق بالملك، وكل حكم تعلق بالملك فإنه يحال على قبول المشتري لا على إيجاب البائع، وإنما سقط كل الصداق لأنه فسخ من كل وجه .اهـ. بلفظه. ويرد على صاحب الذخيرة إذا ارتد الزوج قبل الدخول فإنها فرقة هي فسخ من كل وجه مع أنه لم يسقط كل المهر. من زوج المرتدة بأن الحرمة بالردة غير متأبدة لارتفاعها بالإسلام فيقع طلاقه عليها في العدة مستتبعاً فائدته من حرمتها عليه بعد الثلاث حرمة مغياة بوطء زوج آخر بخلاف حرمة المحرمية فإنها متأبدة فلا يفيد لحوق الطلاق فائدة ا.هـ.
The woman in 'iddah from divorce is subject to another divorce in that 'iddah, while the woman in 'iddah from annulment (faskh) is not subject to another divorce in that 'iddah. And it was mentioned regarding our specific issue that it does occur. As for the ruling of the dower: if the separation occurred after consummation, even constructively, the full dower is obligatory; if before consummation, she has no dower. If the separation is from her side, it is obvious because she brought it about; if from his side, the falling away of the dower is the benefit of the option for him, otherwise there is no benefit in establishing the option for him since he has the power of divorce. Al-Ikhtiyar said: We do not have any separation coming from the husband before consummation where no dower is due except in this case. This enumeration is incorrect, as found in al-Dhakhirah from the twenty-sixth section on miscellaneous topics before the Book of Nafaqat: A free man married a mukatabah with her master's permission for a specific slave girl, but the mukatabah did not take possession of the slave girl until she married her to her husband for one hundred dirhams; both marriages are valid. If the husband divorces the mukatabah first, then divorces the slave girl, the divorce takes effect on the mukatabah but not on the slave girl, because by divorcing the mukatabah the slave girl becomes half-owned and half of her returns to the husband by the very divorce, so the marriage of the slave girl is corrupted before the divorce occurs on her, so her divorce does not operate, and all the dower is annulled for the husband, even though this separation came from the husband before consummation, because when the separation is from the husband before consummation, the entire dower is not cancelled only if it is a divorce; but if the separation before consummation is an annulment (faskh) from every aspect, it causes the cancellation of the entire dower, like when a minor reaches puberty. Also, if he buys his wife before consummation, the entire dower is cancelled even though the separation came from him, because the corruption of the marriage is a ruling connected to ownership, and every ruling connected to ownership is attributed to the acceptance of the buyer, not to the offer of the seller; and the entire dower is cancelled because it is an annulment from every aspect. End quote. It is objected to the author of al-Dhakhirah: if the husband apostatizes before consummation, the separation is an annulment from every aspect, yet the entire dower is not cancelled... from the husband of the apostate woman: that the prohibition due to apostasy is not perpetual, because it is removed by Islam, so his divorce of her occurs in her 'iddah, carrying its benefit of making her unlawful to him after three divorces until she marries another husband, unlike the prohibition of mahram relationship, which is perpetual, so the attachment of divorce does not bring benefit. End quote.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with analysis about whether a woman in 'iddah from an annulment (faskh) can be subject to divorce. The author begins by stating the general rule: a woman in 'iddah from divorce can be divorced again, but a woman in 'iddah from faskh cannot. However, he notes that in the specific case under discussion, divorce does occur. He then discusses the dower implications. He quotes from al-Ikhtiyar that the only case where a husband's separation before consummation exempts him from the full dower is the one at hand, but he rejects this limitation with examples from al-Dhakhirah. The key distinction is that some types of faskh, like apostasy, are not perpetual prohibitions, so divorce can have effect. The author clarifies that the underlying reason is benefit: if the divorce would achieve a lasting purpose (like making her unlawful until she marries another), it attaches; if the prohibition is perpetual (like mahram), it does not. This is a nuanced Hanafi discussion on the intersection of faskh and talaq, especially during 'iddah.

Key Terms
عدة الفسخ ('iddat al-faskh): The waiting period after the annulment (faskh) of a marriage, during which the woman cannot remarry.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of a marriage contract from its foundation, as opposed to divorce (talaq) which dissolves a valid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a woman is in 'iddah after an annulment (faskh) due to apostasy, can her husband divorce her during that 'iddah?
A) No, because faskh always prevents divorce.
B) Yes, because the prohibition of apostasy is not perpetual.
C) Only if the divorce is pronounced before the apostasy.
D) It depends on whether the wife accepts Islam.

Key Takeaway
Divorce can occur in 'iddat al-faskh only when the prohibition that caused the annulment is temporary (like apostasy), not permanent (like mahram).

When Does Divorce Attach After Annulment? An Intra-Hanafi Refinement
Critical refinement on when divorce attaches after faskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on whether a divorce pronounced during the 'iddah of a faskh takes effect, and distinguish between temporary and perpetual prohibitions.

Core Idea
Divorce after faskh attaches only when the prohibition is temporary; perpetual prohibitions negate its benefit.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq refines an earlier view that all faskh prevent divorce during 'iddah. The commentator al-Nahr objects to a claim that separation by purchase is not from the husband, and the author responds by pointing to the distinction between perpetual and temporary prohibitions. This leads to a more nuanced rule: the attachment of divorce depends on whether the prohibition that caused the faskh can be lifted, allowing the benefit of divorce to be realized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being refuted
What is the claim from al-Dhakhirah that triggers the discussion? The claim is that separation by purchase (ownership of wife) is not considered from the husband, implying that divorce does not attach in such cases.
This is the starting point of the critique.
2
Recognize the distinction between perpetual and temporary prohibitions
The author argues that the key factor is whether the prohibition is permanent (like mahram) or temporary (like apostasy). The benefit of divorce (making her unlawful until remarriage) is only achievable if the prohibition can be removed later.
This is the 'illah behind the rule.
3
Apply the rule to specific cases from the text
Work through the examples: apostasy (temporary → divorce attaches), fosterage (perpetual → divorce does not attach), options of emancipation/puberty (apparent contradiction). The text notes that earlier scholars said divorce does not attach in option cases, even though those are not perpetual prohibitions.
The author refines the rule but acknowledges an exception.
4
Evaluate the refinement and its limitations
Determine the mufta bihi position: divorce attaches except for perpetual prohibitions. However, this seems to conflict with the cases of options and incompatibility. The author suggests this refinement is most appropriate, but there is a divergence from apparent wording.
This intra-Hanafi dispute shows ongoing scholarly refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأيضاً لو اشترى منكوحته الخ قال في النهر : في دعوى كون الفرقة من قبله فيما إذا ملكها أو بعضها فيه نظر، ففي البدائع : الفرقة الواقعة بملكه إياها أو شقصاً منها فرقة بغير طلاق. ويرد على صاحب الذخيرة إذا ارتد الزوج قبل الدخول فإنها فرقة هي فسخ من كل وجه مع أنه لم يسقط كل المهر. من زوج المرتدة بأن الحرمة بالردة غير متأبدة لارتفاعها بالإسلام فيقع طلاقه عليها في العدة مستتبعاً فائدته من حرمتها عليه بعد الثلاث حرمة مغياة بوطء زوج آخر بخلاف حرمة المحرمية فإنها متأبدة فلا يفيد لحوق الطلاق فائدة ا.هـ. وكان هذا هو وجه كون الوقوع هنا أوجه لمن تأمل إلا أنه يقتضي قصر عدم الوقوع في العدة على ما إذا كانت الفرقة بما يوجب حرمة مؤبدة كالتقبيل وكالإرضاع وفيه مخالفة لظاهر كلامهم عرف ذلك من تصفحه ا هـ. وذلك أنهم صرحوا بعدم اللحاق في عدة خيار العتق والبلوغ، وكذا بعدم الكفاءة ونقصان المهر حتى صرح بذلك في الفتح أول كتاب الطلاق، اللحاق فيما إذا سبي أحد الزوجين أو هاجر إلينا مسلماً أو ذمياً أو خرجاً مستأمنين فأسلم أحدهما أو صار ذمياً. وصرح أ أيضاً هناك بلحاق الطلاق فيما إذا فرق بينهما بإباء الآخر وبالارتداد وقال : إن الفرقة بردته فسخ خلافاً لأبي يوسف ولو كانت هي المرتدة فهي فسخ ويقع طلاقه عليها في العدة ولم يعلل بما علل به في النكاح . وصرح اتفاقاً أيضاً بعدم. قوله : (وأيضاً لو اشترى منكوحته الخ قال في النهر : في دعوى كون الفرقة من قبله فيما إذا ملكها أو بعضها فيه نظر، ففي البدائع : الفرقة الواقعة بملكه إياها أو شقصاً منها فرقة بغير طلاق
His saying: (Also, if he bought his wife, etc.) Al-Nahr said: Regarding the claim that the separation is from him when he comes to own her or a portion of her, there is consideration. In al-Bada'i': The separation occurring by his ownership of her or a share of her is a separation without divorce. It is refuted against the author of al-Dhakhirah: if the husband apostatizes before consummation, the separation is an annulment from every aspect, yet the entire dower is not cancelled. From the husband of the apostate woman: that the prohibition due to apostasy is not perpetual, because it is removed by Islam, so his divorce of her occurs in her 'iddah, carrying its benefit of making her unlawful to him after three divorces until she marries another husband, unlike the prohibition of mahram relationship, which is perpetual, so the attachment of divorce does not bring benefit. End quote. This was the reason why occurrence here is more appropriate for one who reflects, except that it necessitates confining non-occurrence in 'iddah to cases where the separation is due to a perpetual prohibition, like kissing and breastfeeding, and this contradicts the apparent of their words, as understood by one who examines it. That is because they explicitly stated the non-attachment in the 'iddah of the option of emancipation and puberty, and also in incompatibility and deficiency of dower, as explicitly stated in al-Fath at the beginning of the Book of Divorce: attachment in the case where one of the spouses is captured or immigrates to us as a Muslim or a dhimmi, or they go out as musta'mins and one becomes Muslim or dhimmi. And he also explicitly stated there the attachment of divorce in the case where they are separated due to the other's refusal and due to apostasy, and said: The separation by his apostasy is an annulment contrary to Abu Yusuf, and if she is the apostate, it is an annulment and his divorce of her occurs in the 'iddah, and he did not give the reasoning given in Nikah. And he explicitly stated also by consensus the non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah-style) where the commentator al-Nahr objects to a claim in al-Dhakhirah that when a husband buys his wife, the separation is not considered from him. Al-Nahr points out that in al-Bada'i', the separation by ownership is without divorce. The author of al-Bahr al-Rā'iq then rebuts the objection by emphasizing that the key is whether the prohibition is perpetual. He notes that the earlier statement in al-Fath about apostasy shows that divorce can attach because the prohibition is not perpetual. This leads to a general rule: non-occurrence of divorce in 'iddah after faskh is limited to cases involving perpetual prohibitions (like mahram). However, the author acknowledges that this contradicts the apparent wording of earlier texts that include option of emancipation and puberty as cases where divorce does not attach, even though those are not perpetual prohibitions. The discussion highlights the internal Hanafi dispute on the exact scope of the rule.

Key Terms
حرمة مؤبدة (hurmah mu'abbadah): A permanent prohibition that makes the spouses perpetually unlawful to each other, such as suckling or becoming mahram.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of marriage, as opposed to divorce (talaq); it severs the marital bond without a pronouncement of divorce.
حرمة مغياة (hurmah mughayyah): A time-bound prohibition, such as the prohibition after three divorces which ends when the woman marries another husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, in which type of faskh does a husband's divorce during the wife's 'iddah take effect?
A) Faskh due to a perpetual prohibition (e.g., fosterage).
B) Faskh due to a temporary prohibition (e.g., apostasy).
C) Faskh due to purchase of the wife by the husband.
D) Faskh due to option of puberty.

Key Takeaway
The attachment of divorce after faskh depends on whether the prohibition is temporary (allows divorce) or perpetual (prevents divorce).

Nature of Khiyar al-Bulugh and Khiyar al-'Itq: Faskh vs Talaq
Definition-ruling on faskh for marital options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itq are faskh (annulment) not talaq (divorce), and explain why this distinction matters for divorce count.

Core Idea
The option of puberty and emancipation is faskh, not talaq, and does not count toward the three divorces.

When a minor married by a guardian other than the father or grandfather reaches puberty, or when a slave is emancipated after marriage, they have the option to annul the marriage. The Hanafi school emphasizes that this separation is faskh, a retroactive annulment, not a talaq. This distinction affects the number of divorces remaining and the rules of 'iddah and maintenance. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq and clarify the scope of these options, including exceptions and scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and guardians
Determine who the guardian was at the time of marriage. The option of puberty exists only if the guardian was someone other than the father or grandfather, because those have binding authority. The option of emancipation applies to a female slave who was married while enslaved and then freed.
Male slaves do not have the option of emancipation in the Hanafi school.
2
Classify the separation as faskh or talaq
Understand that exercising the option results in faskh, not talaq. The text explicitly states that the author used the term 'faskh' to indicate that it is not talaq, so it does not reduce the divorce count. This is because the option belongs to the female, who cannot pronounce talaq.
Faskh cancels the contract from its inception in some respects, whereas talaq dissolves a valid marriage.
3
Apply exceptions and understand the 'binding' distinction
Note that faskh is possible only before the marriage becomes binding (lāzim). Marriage by a non-father guardian is valid but not binding until consummation or the wife's consent. Some cases like apostasy are faskh even after binding, by consensus. This refutes the claim that marriage does not admit faskh after completion.
Reference to rejection of a flawed argument: marriage does admit faskh when a legal cause exists.
4
Analyze the dispute about divorce during 'iddah
Consider the question: if faskh occurs after consummation, can a divorce be pronounced during the resulting 'iddah? The passage reports two views; the chosen view (or the one from al-Muhit) is that it does not occur, because the woman is in the 'iddah of faskh, not talaq.
This dispute shows the practical implications of the faskh/talaq distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد أن الكلام في الحر لأن ولاية الأب إنما هي عليه وأما الصغير والصغيرة المرقوقان إذا زوجهما المولى ثم أعتقهما ثم بلغا فإنه لا يثبت لهما خيار البلوغ لكمال ولاية المولى فهو أقوى من الأب والجد ولأن خيار العتق يغني عنه حتى لو أعتق أمته الصغيرة أولاً ثم زوجها ثم بلغت فإن لها خيار البلوغ كما ذكره الأسبيجابي، وهو داخل في غير الأب والجد. فلو قال المصنف وللمولى عليه خيار الفسخ بالبلوغ في غير الأب والجد والابن والمولى» لكان أولى وأشمل. ويدخل تحت غير الأب والجد الأم والقاضي على الأصح لأن ولايتهما متأخرة عن ولاية الأخ والعم فإذا ثبت الخيار في الحاجب ففي المحجوب أولى. وإنما عبر بالفسخ ليفيد أن هذه الفرقة فسخ لاطلاق فلا ينقص عدده لأنه يصح من الأنثى ولا طلاق إليها، وكذا بخيار العتق لما بيناه، وكذا الفرقة بعدم الكفاءة أو نقصان المهر فسخ بخلاف خيار المخيرة لأن الزوج هو الذي ملكها وهو مالك للطلاق وفي التبيين ولا يقال النكاح لا يحتمل الفسخ فلا يستقيم جعله فسخاً لأنا نقول : المعنى بقولنا لا يحتمل الفسخ بعد التمام وهو النكاح الصحيح النافذ اللازم، وأما قبل التمام فيحتمل الفسخ وتزويج الأخ والعم صحيح نافذ لكنه غير لازم فيقبل الفسخ .اهـ. ويرد عليه ارتداد أحدهما فإنه فسخ اتفاقاً وهو بعد التمام وكذا إباؤها عن الإسلام بعد إسلامه فإنه فسخ اتفاقاً وهو بعد التمام وكذا ملك أحد الزوجين صاحبه فالحق أن يقبل الفسخ مطلقاً إذا وجد ما يقتضيه شرعاً. وفي فتح القدير: وهل يقع الطلاق في العدة إذا كانت هذه الفرقة بعد الدخول أي الصريح أو لا؟ لكل وجه والأوجه الوقوع .اهـ. والظاهر عدم الوقوع لما في النهاية من باب نكاح أهل الشرك معزياً إلى المحيط الأصل أن المعتدة بعدة الطلاق يلحقها طلاق آخر في
And it conveyed that the discussion is about the free person because the guardianship of the father is only over him. As for the male and female minor slaves, if the master marries them, then emancipates them, then they reach puberty, they do not have the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) because the master's guardianship is complete; it is stronger than the father and grandfather, and because the option of emancipation (khiyar al-'itq) suffices instead. So much so that if he first emancipates his female minor slave, then marries her, then she reaches puberty, she has the option of puberty, as al-Isbijabi mentioned, and it falls under 'other than the father and grandfather'. If the author had said: 'And for the one under guardianship is the option to annul upon puberty in cases other than the father, grandfather, son, and master', it would be more appropriate and comprehensive. Under 'other than the father and grandfather' enters the mother and the judge according to the correct view, because their guardianship is subsequent to that of the brother and paternal uncle; so if the option is established for the one who excludes, it is more so for the one excluded. He only expressed it with 'annulment' (faskh) to indicate that this separation is faskh, not talaq, so it does not reduce the count [of divorces], because it is valid from the female and she has no divorce. Similarly for the option of emancipation as we explained, and similarly separation due to lack of compatibility (kafa'ah) or deficiency of dower is faskh, unlike the option of the woman given choice (mukhayyarah) because the husband is the one who gave her that right and he has the power of divorce. In al-Tabyin: It is not said that marriage does not admit faskh, so making it faskh is not correct; for we say: The meaning of our statement 'it does not admit faskh' is after completion, i.e., the valid, effective, and binding marriage; but before completion, it admits faskh. The marriage contracted by the brother and paternal uncle is valid and effective but not binding, so it admits faskh. End quote. It is objected to this by the apostasy of one spouse, which is faskh by consensus after completion; similarly, her refusal to accept Islam after his acceptance of Islam is faskh by consensus after completion; similarly, the ownership of one spouse over the other; so the truth is that faskh is acceptable absolutely when there is a legal cause. And in Fath al-Qadir: Does divorce occur in the 'iddah if this separation after consummation is explicit or not? Each view has weight, and the most appropriate is occurrence. End quote. And the apparent is non-occurrence, as in al-Nihayah from the chapter on marriage of polytheists, attributed to al-Muhit: the principle is that the woman in 'iddah from divorce is subject to another divorce in her 'iddah.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling and expansion probe on the nature of khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) and khiyar al-'itq (option of emancipation). The key point is that when a minor or slave exercises the option to annul a marriage, it is a faskh (annulment), not a talaq (divorce), and therefore does not reduce the count of divorces. The text discusses who has this option: the free minor married by a guardian other than father or grandfather, and the slave who has been emancipated before marriage. It also addresses the objection that marriage after consummation does not admit faskh, responding that after the marriage becomes binding, it does not admit faskh, but until it is binding (like when contracted by a non-father guardian), it does. The passage then discusses exceptions like apostasy, which is faskh by consensus even after completion. Finally, it raises the question of whether a divorce pronounced during the 'iddah after such faskh occurs, referencing differing views. This is a detailed Hanafi analysis of the categories of option and their legal effects.

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option for a minor married by a guardian to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.
خيار العتق (khiyar al-'itq): The option for a slave to annul marriage upon emancipation, if the marriage was contracted while enslaved.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of a marriage contract from its basis, as opposed to talaq which is dissolution of a valid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a minor exercises the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh), what is the nature of the separation?
A) Talaq (divorce), which counts toward the three divorces.
B) Faskh (annulment), which does not count as a divorce.
C) It depends on whether the guardian is the father.
D) It is considered a mutual divorce (khula).

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itq are faskh, not talaq, preserving the woman's divorce count.

Khayar al-Faskh upon Majority for Minors Married by Non-Father Guardians
Commentary on the option to annul marriage upon majority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the option to annul upon puberty for minors married by a non-father guardian, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifah/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the condition of a judge's decree.

Core Idea
Minors married by a brother or uncle have the option to annul upon puberty, but only with a judge's decree.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a nuanced ruling within the Hanafi school: when a minor is married by a guardian other than the father or grandfather, the marriage is valid but not binding. Upon reaching puberty, the minor has the option to annul the marriage, but only through a judge's decree. This introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute, as Abu Yusuf argued that the analogy to the father's binding marriage should extend to all guardians. We will unpack the reasoning behind the majority view and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The core ruling: minors married by a guardian other than the father or grandfather have the option (khayar) to annul upon puberty, but only with a judge's decree. Note that this applies to both male and female minors, and includes dhimmis and Muslims.
The marriage is valid but not binding (lazim) until the minor reaches puberty.
2
Understand the key distinction between faskh and talaq
Faskh (annulment) does not reduce the count of divorces and can be initiated by the female, unlike talaq. This is important because the marriage is annulled, not divorced, so it does not affect the number of permissible wives.
The text emphasizes that this separation is faskh, not talaq.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifah and Muhammad hold that the option exists with the condition of qada. Abu Yusuf denies the option entirely, analogizing to the father's binding marriage. The majority argue that the kinship of a brother/uncle is deficient, indicating insufficient compassion, hence the minor needs protection after puberty.
The disagreement is about whether the analogy to the father's authority is valid.
4
Understand why a judge's decree is necessary
The annulment is to ward off 'hidden harm' (ضرر خفي) – the possibility that the guardian's deficient compassion led to a defective marriage. A judge is needed to discern this hidden harm. In contrast, the option of emancipation (khayar al-‘itq) wards off clear harm (ضرر جلي) and does not require a judge.
Hidden harm requires judicial determination; clear harm is self-evident.
5
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Consider a minor girl married by her uncle. At puberty, she may petition a judge to annul. If the judge decrees the annulment, it is effective. If not, the marriage remains binding. Also consider the case where a minor married herself and the guardian authorized it – she still has the option upon puberty.
The option is absolute for all minors in these circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كتاب النكاح باب الأولياء والأكفاء ٢١١
العصبة بترتيب الإرث ولهما خيار الفسخ بالبلوغ في غير الأب والجد بشرط القضاء
كفء ثم قال يسترد اهـ . الثمن بالصغيرة؛ فقيل لا يدخل بها ما لم تبلغ وقيل يدخل بها إذا بلغت تسع سنين، وقيل إن كانت سمينة جسيمة تطيق الجماع يدخل بها وإلا فلا وكذا اختلفوا في وقت ختان الصبي على الأقوال الثلاثة، وقيل يختن إذا بلغ عشراً .اهـ. وفي الخلاصة وأكثر المشايخ على أنه لا اعتبار للسن فيهما وإنما المعتبر الطاقة. وفي الظهيرية صغيرة زوجها وليها من لست أنا بولي لا يصدق ولكن ينظر إن كانت ولايته ظاهرة جاز النكاح وإلا فلا اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : صغيرة زوجت فذهبت إلى بيت زوجها بدون أخذ المهر فلمن هو أحق بإمساكها قبل التزويج أن يمنعها حتى يأخذ له أخذ من حق جميع المهر، وغير الأب إذا زوج الصغيرة وسلمها إلى الزوج قبل قبض جميع الصداق فالتسليم فاسد وترد إلى بيتها. قال رحمه الله : هذا في عرفهم، أما في مما زماننا فتسليم جميع الصداق ليس بلازم والأب إذا سلم البنت إليه قبل القبض له أن يمنعها بخلاف ما لو باع مال الصغير وسلم قبل قبض الثمن فإنه والفرق أن حقوق النقد في الأموال راجعة إليه بخلاف النكاح ولذا ملك الإبراء عن ويضمن ولا يصح الإبراء عن المهر من الولي. قوله : ( ولهما خيار الفسخ بالبلوغ في غير الأب والجد بشرط القضاء) أي للصغير والصغيرة إذا بلغا وقد زوجا أن يفسخا عقد النكاح الصادر من ولي غير أب ولا جد بشرط قضاء القاضي بالفرقة. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله. وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : لا خيار لهما اعتباراً بالأب والجد ولهما أن قرابة الأخ ناقصة والنقصان يشعر بقصور الشفقة فيتطرق الخلل إلى المقاصد والتدارك يعلم بخيار الإدراك بخلاف ما إذا زوجهما الأب والجد فإنه لا خيار لهما بعد بلوغهما لأنهما كاملا الرأي وافرا الشفقة فيلزم العقد بمباشرتهما كما إذا باشراه برضاهما بعد البلوغ. وإنما شرط فيه القضاء بخلاف خيار العتق لأن الفسخ ههنا لدفع ضرر خفي وهو تمكن الخلل ولهذا يشمل الذكر والأنثى فجعل إلزاماً في الآخر فيفتقر إلى القضاء، وخيار العتق لدفع ضرر جلي وهو زيادة الملك عليها ولهذا يختص بالأنثى فاعتبر دفعاً والدفع لا يفتقر إلى القضاء. أطلق الخيار لهما فشمل الذميين والمسلمين كما في المحيط، وشمل ما إذا زوجت الصغيرة نفسها فأجاز الولي فإن لها الخيار إذا بلغت لأن الجواز ثبت بإجازة الولي فالتحق بنكاح باشره الولي. كذا في المحيط.
And He (the author) only expressed it as annulment (faskh) to indicate that this separation is an annulment, not a divorce, so it does not reduce the count (of divorces) because it is valid from the female and she has no divorce right. Similarly, (the separation) by the option of emancipation (khayar al-‘itq) as we explained, and likewise the separation due to lack of suitability (kafa’ah) or deficiency of the dowry is annulment, contrary to the option of the choice (khayar al-mukhayyarah) because the husband is the one who owns her and he is the owner of divorce. In al-Tabyin: And it is not said that marriage does not admit annulment, so making it annulment is not correct, for we say: The meaning of our saying 'does not admit annulment' is after completion (tamam), i.e., a valid, effective, and binding marriage. However, before completion, it does admit annulment. The marriage contracted by a brother or paternal uncle is valid and effective but not binding, so it admits annulment. End quote. It is objected to it (the statement) by the apostasy of one of them, for it is annulment by consensus and it is after completion; likewise her refusal of Islam after his Islam, for it is annulment by consensus and after completion; likewise the ownership of one spouse over the other. The correct view is that annulment is accepted absolutely when something that necessitates it is found legally. In Fath al-Qadir: And does divorce occur during the ‘iddah if this separation is after intercourse (i.e., explicit) or not? There are two views, and the most likely is occurrence. End quote. And the apparent view is non-occurrence, because in al-Nihayah from the chapter on marriage of polytheists, attributed to al-Muhit: The principle is that a woman observing ‘iddah due to divorce is affected by another divorce in her ‘iddah... Her brother married her, then he regained sanity, so he has the option. End quote. His statement: (And because the option of emancipation suffices for it) this is with respect to the female. As for the male, he has no option of emancipation; rather it is only for her, as will be stated shortly before his saying: 'They inherit from each other before annulment.' And the restriction to the minor female does not have a concept, for the major female similarly has the option of emancipation as stated by the author in the chapter on marriage of slaves. But because it was suspected that the minor female has the option of puberty, he limited the explanation to her, as stated by some virtuous scholars. His statement: (Even if he emancipated his minor female slave) singling out the female by mention does not have a concept, because the male similarly has the option of puberty as will be stated there as well. His statement: (And it is objected to it by the apostasy of one of them) etc. It may be said: He meant by annulment what is intended independently by itself, and in the cases he mentioned it is not like that, for it is subsidiary and necessary to something else, namely apostasy, refusal, and ownership. Similar to it is annulment by kissing the son of the husband, captivity of one of them, and his migration to us. Consider. Then after that I saw that some virtuous scholars answered that it is dissolution (insikhakh), not annulment (faskh). End quote. And that is the import of what we said. His statement: (The principle that the woman observing ‘iddah due to divorce...) He said in al-Nahr: I say: This principle is refuted by the case where she refuses Islam and they separate, then he divorces her during the ‘iddah, it occurs even though it is annulment; and by the occurrence of divorce of the apostate even though the separation by his apostasy is annulment, and there is no difference that this divorce of his occurs upon her during the ‘iddah. Thus in al-Fath. And in al-Nikah, it is argued that divorce occurs even though separation by her apostasy is annulment, and with... Book of Marriage, Chapter on Guardians and Suitable Matches 211. The 'asabah (residuary heirs) are in the order of inheritance, and both (the minor male and female) have the option to annul upon puberty in cases other than the father and grandfather, with the condition of a judge's decree. Suitable, then he said: He recovers the price of the minor; it was said: He should not consummate with her until she reaches puberty; and it was said: He may consummate with her if she reaches nine years of age; and it was said: If she is plump and bulky and capable of intercourse, he may consummate, otherwise not. Similarly, they differed on the time of circumcision of a boy according to three statements; and it was said: He should be circumcised when he reaches ten years. End quote. In al-Khulasah: Most of the mashayikh hold that age is not considered in these two, rather the capability is considered. In al-Zahiriyyah: A minor girl was married by her guardian from one who is not a guardian? I am not truthful, but it is to be seen: if his guardianship is apparent, the marriage is valid, otherwise not. End quote. In al-Khulasah: A minor girl was married and went to her husband's house without taking the dowry; then whoever has more right to retain her before the marriage may prevent her until he takes what is due of the entire dowry. And someone other than the father, if he marries the minor girl and hands her over to the husband before receiving the entire dowry, the delivery is invalid and she is returned to her house. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: This is according to their custom. As for our time, delivery of the entire dowry is not necessary. And the father, if he hands over the daughter to him before receiving (the dowry), he has the right to prevent her, unlike if he sold the property of the minor and handed over before receiving the price, for... and the difference is that rights of payment in properties revert to him, unlike marriage; therefore he has the right to grant a discharge from (the dowry) and he is guarantor, and discharge from the dowry by the guardian is not valid. His statement: (and both have the option to annul upon puberty in cases other than the father and grandfather, with the condition of a judge's decree) means: for the minor male and female, when they reach puberty and have been married, they may annul the marriage contract issued by a guardian other than father or grandfather, with the condition that the judge separates them. This is according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them. And Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: They have no option, by analogy with the father and grandfather. And to them (Abu Hanifah and Muhammad) that the kinship of a brother is deficient, and deficiency indicates lack of compassion, so defect enters the objectives, and remedy is known through the option of discernment. Unlike if the father and grandfather married them, for they have no option after puberty because the former are complete in opinion and abundant in compassion, so the contract is binding by their direct action just as if (the minors) had directly acted with their consent after puberty. And the condition of a judge is required, contrary to the option of emancipation, because the annulment here is to ward off hidden harm, namely the possibility of defect, and therefore it includes both male and female, so it is made binding in the end, thus it needs a judge. And the option of emancipation is to ward off clear harm, namely the increase of ownership over her, and therefore it is specific to the female, and it is considered as warding off, and warding off does not require a judge. He made the option absolute for both, so it includes dhimmis and Muslims as in al-Muhit. And it includes the case where a minor girl married herself and the guardian authorized it, for she has the option when she reaches puberty because the authorization is established by the guardian's permission, so it joins the marriage that the guardian directly contracted. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discussing the option (khayar) of annulment upon reaching puberty for minors who were married by a guardian other than their father or grandfather. The author first explains why this separation is called annulment (faskh) rather than divorce (talaq), emphasizing that it does not affect the count of divorces and is valid from the female side. He clarifies that marriage before completion (tamam) can admit annulment, and the marriage by a brother or uncle is valid but not binding, thus allowing annulment. Objections are raised regarding cases like apostasy, refusal of Islam, or ownership, but the author maintains that annulment can occur whenever there is a legal cause. He then discusses whether divorce can occur during the 'iddah after such annulment, citing different views. The commentary then addresses the option of emancipation distinguishing between male and female, and clarifies that the option of puberty applies to both genders. The core ruling is then presented: both the minor male and female have the option to annul the marriage upon puberty if contracted by a guardian other than father or grandfather, but only with a judge's decree. This is the position of Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf disagrees, analogizing to the father and grandfather. The reason for the condition of a judge is that this annulment is to ward off hidden harm, unlike the option of emancipation which wards off clear harm. The passage concludes by stating that this option applies to all, including dhimmis and Muslims, and even if the minor married herself with the guardian's permission.

Key Terms
خيار الفسخ (khayar al-faskh): The option to annul a marriage contract before it becomes binding (lazim), exercised by the minor upon reaching puberty.
البلوغ (al-bulugh): Reaching legal majority, typically by age or physical signs, which triggers the option to annul.
ولي (wali): A guardian authorized to contract marriage; includes father, grandfather, brother, uncle, etc., but the father and grandfather have priority and make binding contracts.
قضاء (qada): A judge's decree ordering the annulment; required for the annulment to be effective in this case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a judge's decree required for the annulment upon puberty when the guardian is a brother or uncle, but not for the option of emancipation?
A) Because the option of emancipation is based on clear harm, while this annulment is based on hidden harm.
B) Because the option of emancipation is only for females, while this annulment is for both males and females.
C) Because a judge's decree is always required for any annulment in marriage.
D) Because the brother and uncle have less authority than the father.

Key Takeaway
Minors married by a brother or uncle have the option to annul upon puberty, but only with a judge's decree, due to the possibility of hidden harm from deficient guardian compassion.

Nikah — Marriage
