Silence of a Minor Virgin After Puberty Nullifies Her Option
Lists additional case of silence as consent: minor virgin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a minor virgin's silence for one hour after reaching puberty nullifies her option to reject a marriage made by a non-guardian, and identify the conditions for this ruling.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence after puberty is consent; her option to reject is lost after one hour.

In Hanafi marriage law, a minor girl married by someone other than her father or grandfather retains the option (khiyar al-bulugh) upon reaching puberty to affirm or reject the marriage. The text adds a specific case: if she is a virgin and remains silent for an hour after learning of the marriage, her option is nullified. This illustrates the broader principle that silence can indicate consent when the occasion calls for speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Locate the case: a minor girl married by a person who is neither her father nor her grandfather (a non-guardian).
This differs from marriage by a father or grandfather, which is binding without option.
2
Check the conditions
Note that the girl must reach puberty, be a virgin, and learn of the marriage. Then she must remain silent for one hour.
If she is not a virgin (thayyib), explicit consent is required.
3
Apply the legal effect
Understand that her silence for one hour after knowledge nullifies her option (khiyar al-bulugh). The marriage becomes binding.
If she speaks to reject within the hour, the marriage is nullified.
4
Recognize the rationale
Silence is taken as consent because in this context, the occasion demands speech; the lack of objection implies approval.
This is a specific application of the maxim: 'Silence in a context requiring speech is consent.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشهورة لا المحصورة .اهـ. ولذا زدت عليه مسألة الوقف ويزاد أيضاً الصغيرة إذا زوجها غير الأب والجد فبلغت بكراً فسكتت ساعة بطل خيارها وهي العشرون، وهي في المجتبى. ويزاد أيضاً ما في المحيط رجل زوج رجلاً بغير أمره فهناه القوم وقبل التهنئة فهو رضا لأن قبول التهنئة دليل الإجازة وهي الحادية والعشرون . قوله : (ويزاد أيضاً الصغيرة) ظاهره أنه لم يذكرها في الفتح مع أنه ذكرها نظماً مع الثمانية عشر السابقة حيث قال : [poem] ... أي ففيه مسألتان، وحينئذ فالمزيد مسألة الوقف ومسألة التهنئة عند تزوج الفضولي قال في الرمز : وزدت عليه والوقف والتفويض أو حلف للعبد لا يعطى له إذن وشريك من قال اشتريت كذا لي كالوكيل لنفسه يعنو اهـ. فقد نظم مسألة الوقف التي زادها المؤلف وزاد عليه أربعة أخر مذكورة في الأشباه : إحداها سكوت المفوض إليه قبول للتفويض وله رده الثانية لو حلف المولى لا يأذن له
Well-known, not confined. Therefore, I added to it the issue of waqf, and also added the minor girl: if someone other than the father or grandfather marries her, then she reaches puberty as a virgin and remains silent for an hour, her option is nullified — this is the twentieth case, and it is in al-Mujtaba. Also added is what is in al-Muhit: a man married a man without his order, then the people congratulated him and he accepted the congratulation; that is consent because acceptance of congratulation is evidence of approval — this is the twenty-first case. His saying: 'Also added the minor girl'... apparently he did not mention it in al-Fath, although he mentioned it in verse with the previous eighteen cases where he said: [poem] ... That is, it contains two cases. So the added cases are the issue of waqf and the issue of congratulation at the marriage of a fuduli. He said in al-Ramz: 'I added to it the waqf and the delegation...' He thus versified the waqf issue that the author added, and added four others mentioned in al-Ashbah: first, the silence of the delegate is acceptance of delegation and he may revoke it; second, if the master swore not to permit him...
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage provides additional cases where silence is taken as consent. The author adds the case of a minor girl married by someone other than her father or grandfather. When she reaches puberty and learns of the marriage, her silence for an hour nullifies her option to reject. Another case is from al-Muhit: if a man marries someone without his permission, and then the people congratulate him and he accepts the congratulation, that indicates consent. The commentator then discusses that the author's statement about adding the minor girl implies that it was not in al-Fath, although it was included in a verse. The verse lists many cases of silence being treated as consent. Ultimately, the added cases are the waqf issue and the congratulation case. Additionally, from al-Ashbah, four more cases are mentioned: the silence of a delegate, the case of the master's oath, and others. This shows how the Hanafi school uses silence as an indicator of consent in various contexts.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts without authorization, e.g., marries someone without their permission.
خيار البلوغ (khiyār al-bulūgh): The option of puberty; the right of a minor married before puberty to affirm or reject the marriage upon reaching puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Accepting Congratulations After a Fuduli Marriage Indicates Consent
Lists additional case of silence as consent: acceptance of congratulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that accepting congratulations after a fuduli marriage is legally considered consent, and identify the conditions for this ruling.

Core Idea
Accepting congratulations after a marriage by an unauthorized person is evidence of consent.

In Hanafi law, if someone marries another without his permission (a fuduli contract), the marriage is suspended. However, if the person whose permission was needed later receives congratulations from others and accepts them, that acceptance is taken as an indication of his approval. This is a practical way of showing consent without explicit verbal statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A fuduli marries a man to a woman without his prior order. The man then receives congratulations from the community and accepts them.
The marriage itself is performed by a fuduli; the groom's consent was missing initially.
2
Understand the legal effect
The acceptance of congratulations is considered evidence (dalil) of approval. This turns the suspended marriage into a valid, binding contract.
The text says 'قبول التهنئة دليل الإجازة' (acceptance of congratulations is evidence of approval).
3
Note the condition
The congratulations must be specifically about the marriage, and the person must knowingly accept them. Mere silence after congratulations may not suffice; acceptance is key.
This distinguishes from the case where someone remains silent without being congratulated.
4
Contrast with other forms of consent
This is one of several ways consent can be inferred: explicit statement, silence in specific contexts, or actions like acceptance of congratulations.
The list in the text is non-exhaustive.

Nineteen Cases Where Silence Equals Consent
Enumerates nineteen legal scenarios where silence is deemed equivalent to verbal consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the nineteen cases where Hanafi fiqh treats silence as consent and explain the legal effect in each case.

Core Idea
Silence, when indicative of consent, is legally equivalent to speech in Hanafi fiqh.

This passage presents a comprehensive enumeration from the Hanafi tradition of scenarios where silence is considered as explicit consent. Covering marriage, sales, agency, slavery, preemption, deposits, and more, the list illustrates the school's nuanced approach to inferring legal intent without verbal expression. As the author notes, the list is not exhaustive, but it provides essential guidance for muftis and judges.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overarching principle
The author states that when silence indicates consent, it stands in place of speech. This is a contextual assessment – the surrounding circumstances make silence probative.
Silence alone is not always consent; it requires strong contextual indications.
2
Categorize the cases by fiqh chapter
The 19 cases span marriage (1-3), gifts (4), sales (5-9), paternity (10), torts (11), oaths (12), fictitious sales (13), deposits (14), preemption (15), slavery (16), agency (17), property claims (18), and waqf (19). Grouping them aids memory and understanding.
Recognize that the same principle applies across diverse areas.
3
Analyze each case for context and legal effect
For each case, identify the specific situation (who is silent, under what circumstances) and what legal consequence follows (validity, lapse of right, liability, etc.). Ask: Why does silence here reliably indicate consent?
For example, in case 5, the buyer's possession in the seller's presence with silence indicates acceptance of the sale.
4
Note the non-exhaustive nature
The author concludes with 'induction indicates it is not confined,' meaning the list is illustrative, not exhaustive. Other scenarios may be analogized.
This invites qiyas (analogical reasoning) to extend the principle to new cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أن السكوت إذا دل على الرضا فإنه يقوم مقام القول وقد ذكروا مسائل أقيم فيها السكوت مقام التصريح : الأولى سكوت البكر عند الاستثمار. الثانية سكوتها عند بلوغها الخبر الثالثة سكوتها عند قبض الأب أو الجد المهر. كذا قالوا، ولا ينبغي إدخاله فيما نحن فيه لأن له أن يقبض المهر في غيبتها حتى لو ردت عند بلوغها الخبر بقبضه لا تملك ذلك، نعم لها نهيه عنه قبل القبض كما قدمناه. الرابعة سكوت المالك عند قبض الموهوب له أو المتصدق عليه العين بحضرته الخامسة في البيع ولو فاسداً إذا قبضه المشتري بمرأى من البائع فسكت صح وسقط حق الحبس بالثمن. السادسة إذا اشترى العبد بحضرة مولاه فسكت كان إذناً في غير الأول السابعة الصبي إذا اشترى أو باع بمرأى من وليه فسكت فهو إذن له الثامنة المشتري بالخيار إذا رأى العبد يبيع ويشتري فسکت سقط خياره التاسعة سيد العبد المأسور إذا رآه يباع فسكت بطل . أخذه في بالقيمة العاشرة إذا سكت الأب ولم ينف الولد مدة التهنئة لزمه فلا ينتقي بعد الحادية عشرة السكوت عقيب شق رجل زقه حتى سال ما فيه يضمن الشاق ما سال. الثانية عشرة سكوته عقب حلفه على أن لا أسكن فلاناً وفلان ساكن فيحنث. الثالثة عشرة السكوت عقيب قول رجل واضع غيره على أن يظهرا بيع تلجئة ثم قال بدا لي جعله بيعاً بمسمع من الآخر ثم عقدا كان نافذاً. الرابعة عشرة يصير مودعاً بسكوته عقيب وضع رجل متاعه عنده وهو ينظر الخامسة عشر الشفيع إذا بلغه البيع فسكت كان تسليماً. السادسة عشر مجهول النسب إذا بيع فسكت كان إقراراً بالرق السابعة عشر يكون وكيلاً بسكوته عقب الأمر ببيع المتاع. الثامنة عشر إذا رأى ملكاً له يباع ولو عقاراً فسكت حتى قبضه المشتري سقط دعواه فيه لكن شرط في فتح القدير لسقوط دعواه أن يقبض المشتري ويتصرف فيه أزماناً وهو ساكت بخلاف السكوت عند مجرد البيع. التاسعة عشر في الوقف على فلان إذا سكت جاز وإن رده بطل كذا في الخلاصة من الإقرار وفيه خلاف ذكره في التبيين من آخر الكتاب أيضاً. وفي فتح القدير والاستقراء يفيد عدم الحصر وهذه
The author indicated that when silence indicates consent, it stands in place of speech. They have mentioned cases where silence is set in place of explicit expression: First: the silence of a virgin at the time of seeking permission. Second: her silence when she is informed of the marriage. Third: her silence when the father or grandfather takes the dowry. That is what they said, but it should not be included here because he may take the dowry in her absence, and even if she rejects after learning of his taking it, she cannot reclaim it; though she can forbid him before taking as we mentioned earlier. Fourth: the silence of the owner when the donee or the recipient of charity takes the object in his presence. Fifth: in a sale, even if invalid, if the buyer takes possession in the sight of the seller and he remains silent, the sale is valid and the right to retain the price is dropped. Sixth: if a slave buys in the presence of his master and he remains silent, it is permission in the first purchase. Seventh: if a minor buys or sells in the sight of his guardian and he remains silent, it is permission for him. Eighth: the buyer with an option if he sees the slave selling and buying and remains silent, his option lapses. Ninth: if the master of a captive slave sees him being sold and remains silent, his right to take him by value is forfeited. Tenth: if the father remains silent and does not deny the child during the period of congratulation, it is established and cannot be repudiated afterwards. Eleventh: silence after a man cuts someone's wineskin so that its contents flow out — the cutter guarantees what flows out. Twelfth: his silence after he swears that he will not house so-and-so and that person is living in his house — he is guilty of perjury. Thirteenth: silence after a man places another to pretend a fictitious sale, then says 'I have decided to make it a real sale' in the hearing of the other, and then they contract — it is valid. Fourteenth: he becomes a depositary by his silence after a man places his belongings with him while he watches. Fifteenth: if the preemptor is informed of the sale and remains silent, it is surrender. Sixteenth: if a person of unknown lineage is sold and he remains silent, it is an admission of slavery. Seventeenth: he becomes an agent by his silence after being ordered to sell goods. Eighteenth: if he sees his property being sold, even real estate, and remains silent until the buyer takes possession, his claim to it is dismissed. But in Fath al-Qadir, a condition is set: the buyer must take possession and deal with it for some time while he is silent, unlike silence at the mere sale. Nineteenth: in a waqf upon someone, if he remains silent, it is valid; if he rejects, it is invalid. This is in al-Khulasa from al-Iqrar, and there is a difference of opinion mentioned in al-Tabyin at the end of the book. In Fath al-Qadir, induction indicates that it is not confined, and these...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is an important enumeration in Hanafi fiqh showing that silence can be legally treated as consent in various contexts. The author first mentions that when silence indicates consent, it stands in place of verbal expression. Then he lists nineteen specific cases drawn from prior texts. Some of these relate to marriage (cases 1-3), others to sales (5-8), agency (17), slavery (16), preemption (15), deposits (14), waqf (19), and so on. The list demonstrates the breadth of the principle. The author also notes that the list is not exhaustive (عدم الحصر). This is a crucial resource for understanding how the Hanafi school uses contextual indications to infer consent without explicit speech. Students should study each case to understand the specific reasoning behind treating silence as consent.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukūt): Silence that legally indicates consent in specific contexts.
استثمار (istithmār): Seeking permission, particularly from a virgin for marriage.

Silence After Knowledge: When Consent Is (Not) Effective
Ruling on woman married by father without consent, husband dies: effect of silence on inheritance and 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a woman's silence to count as valid consent (ijazah) in a marriage contract and apply it to determine inheritance and 'iddah rights.

Core Idea
Silence after the husband's death can never ratify a marriage contract.

This lesson examines a subtle but critical rule in Hanafi marriage law: the timing of a woman's consent when a marriage was conducted without her prior permission. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that silence can only constitute acceptance if the news reaches her while the husband is still alive. After his death, the contract is void and cannot be revived by any subsequent consent. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly allocating inheritance and 'iddah obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a woman's silence after learning of a marriage contract. The ruling: it can be considered valid consent only if the husband is alive at that time.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage uses an analogy: like a cat's circumambulation is invalid, silence after death is invalid. It also cites al-Khaniyya for a detailed case.
The analogy shows that the contract must be capable of ratification; death extinguishes it.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The condition is derived from the principle that a contract must be in existence to be ratified. The analogy with tawaf reinforces that death nullifies the contract.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
No explicit ikhtilaf within Hanafi, but the Shafi'i school requires explicit verbal consent for a virgin, creating a practical difference.
5
Apply to the case
Analyze the three scenarios from al-Khaniyya: heirs deny knowledge (no inheritance), woman claims prior order (inheritance), woman claims later consent (no inheritance).
Focus on the timing of consent relative to husband's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب الأولياء والأكفاء
وعدماً كالطواف في الهرة ولذا كان سؤر الهرة الوحشية نجساً لفقد الطواف كما عرف في الأصول. ولا بد أن يكون سكوتها بعد بلوغها الخبر في حياة الزوج وإلا فليس بإجازة لأن شرطها قيام العقد وقد بطل بموته كما في الفتاوى وذكر في الخانية: رجل زوج ابنته البالغة ولم يعلم الرضا والرد حتى مات زوجها فقالت ورثته إنها زوجت بغير أمرها ولم تعلم بالنكاح ولم ترض فلا ميراث لها وإن قالت هي زوجني أبي بأمري كان القول قولها ولها الميراث وعليها العدة، وإن قالت زوجني أبي بغير أمري فبلغني الخبر فرضيت فلا مهر لها ولا ميراث لأنها أقرت أن العقد وقع غير تام فإذا ادعت النفاذ بعد ذلك لا يقبل قولها لمكان التهمة اهـ.
The chapter of marriage, section on guardians and compatibility. And [silence] is not [valid] like the circumambulation of a cat; therefore the leftover water of a wild cat is impure due to lack of circumambulation as known in Usul. It is necessary that her silence be after the news reaches her during the husband's lifetime; otherwise it is not an acceptance because its condition is the existence of the contract, and it has become void by his death as in al-Fatawa. It is mentioned in al-Khaniyya: A man married his adult daughter and did not know her consent or rejection until her husband died. Her heirs said: She was married without her command, she did not know of the marriage, and she did not consent — then there is no inheritance for her. If she says: My father married me by my command — her word is accepted, she has inheritance, and upon her is the 'iddah. If she says: My father married me without my command, then the news reached me and I consented — then there is no mahr for her and no inheritance, because she confessed that the contract occurred incomplete; if she claims enforcement after that, her statement is not accepted due to suspicion.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage begins a ruling on the effect of a woman's silence after learning of a marriage contract conducted without her prior consent. The author starts with an analogy: just as a cat's circumambulation is not valid for purification, silence after the husband's death cannot count as acceptance because the contract's prerequisite (the husband's life) is gone. The core condition: silence only operates as consent if the news reaches her while the husband is alive. If he dies first, the contract is void and cannot be ratified. The text then cites al-Khaniyya for a detailed case: a man marries his adult daughter without her knowledge, and the husband dies. If the heirs deny her knowledge, she gets no inheritance. If she claims she ordered the marriage, her word is accepted, she inherits, and must observe 'iddah. If she admits she had no prior order but later consented after the news, she still gets no mahr or inheritance because she acknowledges the contract was initially incomplete; her later claim of enforcement is rejected due to self-interest.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukūt): Silence after reaching the news of the marriage; considered indicative of consent under specific conditions.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification or acceptance of a contract after the fact; here specifically the woman's subsequent consent to a marriage conducted without her prior permission.
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband or dissolution of marriage, during which she cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a woman's silence after learning of her marriage be considered valid consent (ijazah)?
A) At any time, even after the husband's death
B) Only if she explicitly says 'I consent'
C) Only if the news reaches her during the husband's lifetime
D) Only if she never speaks at all

Key Takeaway
Silence can only ratify a marriage contract if the news reaches the woman while the husband is still alive; otherwise the contract is void and cannot be inherited from.

Silence of a Virgin Married to a Non-Compatible: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on silence in non-compatible marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on a virgin's silence after being married to a non-compatible, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Silence of a virgin married to a non-compatible: Abu Hanifa deems it consent if by father/grandfather; Sahibayn reject it entirely.

In this passage from Bahr al-Raiq, al-Marghinani presents a dispute within the Hanafi school about a specific scenario: a guardian marries an adult virgin to a man who is not her kuf' (compatible), and she remains silent upon learning the news. Does this silence constitute consent? The answer impacts the validity of the marriage and highlights the subtle difference between explicit and implicit consent in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
First, note the precise case: an adult virgin (not a minor) is married off by a guardian to a man who is not her equal in lineage, profession, or piety. She learns of the marriage and remains silent.
The passage specifies that the dispute only arises when the husband is ghayr kuf'; if compatible, silence generally indicates consent.
2
Contrast the two positions
State the Sahibayn's view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): her silence is not consent, so the marriage is invalid unless she explicitly agrees. Then state Abu Hanifa's view: her silence is consent, but only if the marrying party is her father or grandfather.
The difference hinges on the authority of the father/grandfather versus other guardians.
3
Trace the legal basis
Understand the reasoning: Abu Hanifa analogizes from the case of a minor girl married to a non-compatible, where silence is not consent. For an adult, the father's authority strengthens the implication. The Sahibayn argue that silence is ambiguous when the marriage is to a non-compatible because the woman would naturally object.
The source of this analogy is mentioned in the text: 'akhidhan min mas'alat al-saghirah al-muzawwajah min ghayr kuf''.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Apply the two views: if a father marries his adult virgin daughter to a non-compatible and she does not object, the marriage is valid according to Abu Hanifa but invalid according to the Sahibayn. This affects the woman's right to contest the marriage later.
The text does not give a definitive tarjih, but later commentaries often prefer the more cautious view (Sahibayn).

لا يكون رضا لأن ابن العم كان أصيلاً في نفسه فضولياً في جانب المرأة فلم في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد فلا يعمل الرضا، ولو استأمرها في التزويج من فسكتت ثم زوجها من نفسه جاز إجماعاً. كذا في الخانية. وأطلق في البكر فشمل ما إذا كانت تزوجت قبل ذلك وطلقت قبل زوال البكارة ولذا قال في الظهيرية : وإذا فرق القاضي بين امرأة العنين وبين العنين وجبت عليها العدة وتزوج كما تزوج الأبكار. نص عليه في الأصل. وشمل ما إذا ...by it she was silent for her in a manner that knowledge occurs for her as we have presented in the asking for permission. As for specifying the mahr, it is according to the preceding disagreement. And a branch in al-Tabyin on the condition of non-specification: if he specifies it, it must be ample, i.e., mahr al-mithl, so that silence is not consent without it. And they disagreed regarding when he marries her to a non-compatible and she learns and is silent: the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is not consent. And it is said in the view of Abu Hanifa that it is consent if the marrying party is the father or grandfather, and if other than them, then not, as in al-Khaniyya, taken from the case of the minor girl married to a non-compatible. And the author did not mention the case when she laughs after learning the news, even though it is like her laughter at the time of asking permission, as in Ghayat al-Bayan, contenting himself with mentioning it earlier. And if the author had said 'and if the guardian or her husband asked permission and she knew or laughed, then it is permission', it would be better. And weeping at marriage is like weeping at asking permission. And he gave a general statement regarding her silence after learning the news, including the case where he asked her permission for a specific person and she refused, then he married her to that person and she was silent – it is an acceptance according to the sound view, unlike the case where the contract reached her and she refused, then said 'I consent' – it is not valid because the contract was voided by refusal. Hence they recommended renewal at the wedding feast when the marriage took place before asking permission, since most women show aversion at the sudden hearing. And in Fath al-Qadir, the more correct view is that it is not valid because that explicit refusal does not fail to weaken that silence as an indication of consent. And if she had said 'I had said I do not want him' and added nothing more, the marriage is not valid because it informs that she is in a state of refusal. End. The contract is completed by itself. And the author indicated by 'silence upon learning the news' that if she allows him access to herself or demands the mahr and maintenance, it is consent because indication acts like explicit statement, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he restricted with his statement 'or her husband' because if the guardian marries her, like a cousin marrying his adult virgin cousin without her permission, then she learns and is silent, it is not consent, because the cousin is principal in himself and a volunteer in regard to the woman; so according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, consent does not operate. And if he asked her permission to marry from [someone] and she was silent, then he married her to himself, it is valid by consensus. As in al-Khaniyya. And he gave a general statement regarding the virgin, including the case where she had been married before and divorced before loss of virginity – hence al-Zahiriyya said: when the judge separates between an impotent man and his wife, upon her is the 'iddah and she marries as virgins marry. Textually stated in al-Asl. And it includes the case where she disputes with husbands over mahr, and there is disagreement. Al-Zahiriyya said: A virgin, if she disputes with husbands over the mahr beforehand, she is not asked to speak; and it is said she is asked to speak because the reason for not speaking is shyness, and shyness is no longer present. End. And the first view should be preferred because the criterion in the textually stated [ruling] is for the text itself, not for its meaning; and she is a virgin, so her silence suffices, even if she has no shyness, like the virgins of our time – most of them lack shyness. And it may be answered that it is a textually stated cause, not inferred; the ruling depends on its existence. Rejection; and if with silence it is consent, that is the more correct view and the fatwa is upon it. End. His statement: 'And in Fath al-Qadir the more correct is validity' is in contrast to his statement 'it is acceptance according to the sound view' – reflect. Not.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and additional rulings on the theme of silence in marriage. It begins with a continuation: the silence of the virgin after proper notification. The first sub-topic is the specification of the mahr: if the guardian names a mahr, it must be fair (mahr al-mithl) because otherwise silence would not indicate consent to an inadequate amount. The core dispute concerns marriage to a non-compatible (ghayr kuf'). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that her silence does not constitute consent. According to one report from Abu Hanifa, however, it does constitute consent if the person conducting the marriage is her father or grandfather; if it is someone else, then not. This is derived from the case of a minor girl married to a non-compatible – her silence is not consent. Next, laughter: if she laughs after learning of the marriage, it is equivalent to explicit consent, just as laughter at the time of isti'dhan (asking permission) is consent. Weeping, by contrast, is like weeping at the time of asking – it indicates aversion. The passage then addresses a scenario: if the guardian asked her permission for a specific man, she refused, but then he married her to that man and she remained silent, this silence is valid consent (ijazah) according to the sahih view. But if the contract was concluded without prior consultation, she refused upon learning, and later said 'I consent', it is not valid because the contract was voided by the explicit refusal. The author notes a recommended practice: when a marriage is done without prior permission, the guardians should renew the contract at the wedding feast because women often show initial reluctance. Fath al-Qadir deems the latter (silence after refusal) invalid because the explicit refusal weakens any subsequent indication of consent. The text then clarifies that if the woman, after learning of the marriage, allows the husband access or demands mahr and maintenance, that is consent because action is as strong as speech. A restriction is added: if the person who married her was a cousin (who is both a guardian and personally interested), her silence does not count because he acts as an 'asil (principal) for himself and a fuduli (volunteer) for her. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad agree that consent does not operate here. However, if the cousin first asked her permission to marry her (to someone else) and she was silent, then he married her to himself, it is valid by consensus. The passage also extends the ruling to a virgin who had been previously married but divorced before loss of virginity – she is treated as a virgin regarding consent. Similarly, a woman separated from an impotent husband observes 'iddah and then marries as a virgin. Finally, a dispute about a virgin who disputes over mahr: the silent view says she is not even asked to speak (her silence suffices); another view says she must speak because the rationale (shyness) is gone. The author prefers the first view because the ruling is text-based, not reasoning-based. The last line comments on a discrepancy in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The fair or customary mahr for a woman of her status; used as the standard when the actual mahr is not specified or is inadequate.
كفء (kufʾ): Compatibility between spouses in terms of lineage, profession, freedom, and piety; a marriage to a non-compatible may be challenged.
فُضولي (fuḍūlī): A person who acts on behalf of another without prior authorization; such acts can be ratified later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is always considered consent
B) It is not considered consent according to all Hanafi scholars
C) Abu Hanifa holds it is consent; the Sahibayn hold it is not
D) It is consent only if the mahr is specified

Key Takeaway
When a virgin is married to a non-compatible, her silence is not automatically valid consent; the Sahibayn require explicit consent, and this is the more cautious and relied-upon view.

The General Rule and Exceptions: A Virgin's Silence as Consent
Commentary on the ruling of a virgin's silence as consent and its exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a virgin's silence upon learning of marriage is considered consent, and identify the key exceptions including the dispute over non-competent guardians and the effect of prior explicit refusal.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence is consent — unless the guardian is non-competent or there was prior explicit refusal.

In Hanafi fiqh, the consent of a bride is a foundational requirement for marriage. For a virgin, the school has established that her silence when she learns of the marriage is treated as consent. However, this is not absolute. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores the conditions and exceptions to this rule, including a significant intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the role of a non-competent guardian. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply these rules accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The text states that a virgin's silence upon learning of marriage is considered consent (idhn). This is based on the hadith that 'the virgin's consent is her silence.'
This rule applies only if she has been informed in a manner that gives her knowledge.
2
Analyze the Exception for Non-Competent Guardian
There is a dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that silence is consent only if the guardian is the father or grandfather; otherwise it is not. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say silence is never consent when the guardian is not competent (kuf').
This dispute is derived from the case of a minor married to a non-kuf'.
3
Examine the Case of Prior Explicit Refusal
If the virgin explicitly refuses a specific suitor, then the guardian marries her anyway, and she remains silent, that silence is considered ratification (ijazah) according to the correct view. However, if she refuses and later says she is pleased, the marriage is invalid because the initial refusal nullified the contract.
The text contrasts two scenarios: silence after refusal (valid) vs. verbal consent after refusal (invalid).
4
Consider Self-Marriage by the Guardian
If the guardian marries her to himself (e.g., a cousin marrying his cousin without her explicit permission), her silence is not considered consent because he is both principal and intermediary.
This is a specific exception to the general rule.
5
Recognize Other Indications of Consent
Actions such as allowing consummation (tamkin) or demanding the dower and maintenance are treated as explicit consent, equivalent to silence being consent.
The text says 'indication acts as explicit statement.'

لأن ذلك الرد الصريح لا ينزل عن تضعيف كون ذلك السكوت دلالة الرضا ولو كانت قالت قد كنت قلت لا أريده ولم تزد على هذا لا يجوز النكاح للإخبار بأنها على امتناعها اهـ. يتم العقد نفسه. وأشار المصنف بالسكوت البالغة بغير إذنها فبلغها الخبر فسكتت لا يكون رضا لأن ابن العم كان أصيلاً في نفسه فضولياً في جانب المرأة فلم في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد فلا يعمل الرضا، ولو استأمرها في التزويج من فسكتت ثم زوجها من نفسه كالأجنبي لا يفعل شيئاً إلا برضاها فقد اختلف الترجيح فيها والمذهب الأول لما في الذخيرة أن إشارة كتب محمد تدل عليه.
Book of Marriage, Chapter on Guardians and Competence. By it: she was silent in a manner that gives her knowledge, as we presented in seeking permission. Regarding the naming of the dowry, it follows the previous disagreement. And they disagreed about when he marries her to someone not compatible and she learns and is silent: they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is not consent. And it is said in the opinion of Abu Hanifa it is consent if the one marrying is the father or grandfather, but if other than them then no, as in al-Khaniyah, derived from the case of a minor married to someone not compatible. And the author did not mention the case when she laughs after learning the news, even though it is like her laughter when seeking permission, as in Ghayat al-Bayan, relying on his earlier mention. And if the author had said 'and if the guardian seeks her permission or marries her and she learns or laughs then it is permission' it would be better. And in Fath al-Qadir: the most plausible is lack of validity because that explicit refusal does not reduce to weakening the silence as an indication of consent. And if she said 'I had said I do not want him' and said no more, the marriage is not valid because it informs that she is still refraining. End. The contract itself is completed. And the author indicated by 'silence at learning the news' that if she allows him to have sexual access or demands the dowry and maintenance, it is consent because indication acts as explicit statement, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he restricted with 'or marries her' because if the guardian marries her, like the cousin marrying his adult virgin cousin without her permission, and she learns the news and is silent, it is not consent because the cousin was acting as principal for himself and as intermediary for the woman, so according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the consent does not work. But if he sought her permission for marriage and she was silent, then he married her to himself, it is permissible by consensus, as in al-Khaniyah. And he made it absolute for the virgin, so it includes one who was married before and divorced before loss of virginity; hence it is said in al-Zahiriya: when the judge separates between a man with impotence and the woman, the waiting period is obligatory and she marries like virgins, as stated in al-Asl. And it includes the case where she disputes with husbands about the dowry, and there is disagreement. In al-Zahiriya: the virgin who disputes with her husband about the dowry is not questioned, and it is said she is questioned because the reason for removing speech is modesty and modesty is gone from her. End. The first is preferable because the consideration is for the text itself not its meaning, and she is a virgin so her silence suffices, even if she has no modesty like the virgins of our time, as they generally lack modesty. And it can be answered that it is a stated cause, not deduced, and the stated cause is related to the ruling in its existence. Rejection, but if accompanied by silence it is consent, and that is the most plausible and the fatwa is upon it. End. He said: (In Fath al-Qadir the most plausible is validity) in contrast to his saying 'it is ratification according to the correct view' — reflect. [continues on next page]
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the ruling on a virgin's silence when informed of her marriage. The general principle is that silence is consent, but there are exceptions. The author discusses the case where the guardian is not competent (non-kuf): Abu Hanifa holds that if the guardian is the father or grandfather, silence indicates consent; if the guardian is someone else, it does not. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it never indicates consent. The commentary also covers the scenario where she explicitly refused beforehand and then the guardian marries her anyway — if she is silent after learning, it is considered ratification as per the correct view, but if she refused outright and later says she is pleased, the marriage is invalid because the first refusal nullified the contract. The text also mentions that if she allows consummation or demands the dowry, it is consent. Additionally, if the guardian marries her to himself (e.g., a cousin), her silence is not consent because he is both principal and intermediary. The passage ends with a discussion of the father vs. grandfather distinction in a note.

Key Terms
كفء (kuf'): Compatibility in marriage, primarily in lineage and status; a non-competent guardian is one who does not match the bride in social standing.
بكر (bikr): A virgin woman who has never been married or whose virginity has been restored after divorce.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or consent after the fact; here it refers to a virgin's silence or other indication that validates a marriage contract made without prior permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a girl explicitly refuses a suitor, but the father later marries her to him and she remains silent upon hearing the news?
A) The marriage is invalid because her refusal nullified the contract.
B) The marriage is valid because her silence indicates consent.
C) The marriage is valid only if she laughs afterward.
D) The marriage is invalid because the father is not a competent guardian.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence upon learning of marriage is generally considered consent, but this presumption can be rebutted in cases of explicit prior refusal or when the guardian is non-competent, and the school has differing views on the guardian's role.

Ruling on a Virgin's Crying and Seeking Permission
Ruling on crying and permission for a virgin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a virgin's crying as consent and explain the sunnah of seeking permission.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence is consent; crying without sound is silence; seeking permission is sunnah, not wajib.

In Hanafi marriage law, a virgin's consent is crucial. This passage resolves a dispute about whether her crying counts as rejection. The correct view distinguishes crying with sound from silent tears. It also explains the sunnah of seeking her permission before the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute about crying
The author notes disagreement about whether a virgin's crying indicates rejection or consent. Two narrations from Abu Yusuf exist.
One narration says crying is rejection; the other says it is consent.
2
Reconcile the narrations
The reconciling view: crying without sound (silent tears) is consent due to natural grief; crying with sound (wailing) is rejection as it shows anger.
This is the mufta bihi position, supported by later scholars.
3
Understand the sunnah of isti'dhan
The hadith of Muslim says a virgin's permission is her silence. The Hanafi school interprets this as sunnah, not a condition of validity.
If she explicitly consents later, the marriage is valid.
4
Recognize the flexibility in consent
Explicit verbal consent after the contract cures any deficiency in the initial permission procedure.
Silence at the time of proposal, followed by later explicit consent, is sufficient.

And the author did not mention crying due to disagreement about it. The correct choice for fatwa is that if she cries without sound it is permission because of grief at leaving her family, but if with sound it is not permission because it indicates anger and dislike generally. However, in al-Mi'raj: crying, though it indicates anger, is not a rejection, so if she is pleased afterward the contract is valid, but if she says 'I am not pleased' then later is pleased, the marriage is not valid. With this it becomes clear that the statement of al-Wiqayah 'crying without sound is permission and with sound is rejection' is not correct unless interpreted to mean with sound is not permission because it indicates anger. And if he marries her without seeking permission, he has erred from the sunnah and it depends on her consent. End. And this is the interpretation of the prohibition in the hadith of Muslim: 'Do not marry a previously married woman until she is consulted, and do not marry a virgin until her permission is sought.' And that only happens at the time of ratification. So his statement 'This is if her crying has a sound' means it is a rejection, as indicated by the opposite. And this is supported by the fact that the original disagreement is about whether crying is a rejection or not, because of the statement of Qadikhan in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'And if she cries, it is a rejection in one narration from Abu Yusuf, and from him in another narration it is consent.' They said: If the crying is with sound and wailing, it is not consent; if it is without sound, it is consent. End. Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the consent of a virgin. It focuses on crying (buka'). The correct position for fatwa is that crying without sound (no wailing or screaming) indicates consent because it is natural grief at leaving her family. Crying with sound indicates displeasure and is not consent. There is a dispute, however, because Imam Abu Yusuf has two narrations: one that crying is a rejection (radd), the other that it is consent. The text reconciles them by distinguishing sound from silence. The author also addresses a conflict between two authoritative texts, al-Wiqayah and al-Mi'raj, about the ruling. Fath al-Qadir advises to consider circumstances. The passage then moves to the sunnah of seeking permission (isti'dhan) before the marriage contract, quoting the hadith of Muslim that a virgin's permission is her silence. It explains that the hadith indicates the preferred practice, not an absolute condition, because if she explicitly consents later, the marriage is valid. The text also discusses how knowledge of the marriage is established for the virgin, requiring a credible informant. The passage ends with a critical refinement comparing the weight of al-Wiqayah and al-Mi'raj, concluding that the actual disagreement is resolved by considering the nature of the crying.

Key Terms
بكاء (buka'): Crying; the text distinguishes between crying with sound (صوت or ويل) and crying without sound (سكوت or دمع بدون صوت).
استئذان (isti'dhan): Seeking permission from a virgin before marriage; the sunnah is to inform her and wait for her silence.
سكوت (sukut): Silence; the hadith says a virgin's permission is her silence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is not considered consent because it indicates displeasure.
C) It is considered a rejection, but if she later agrees the marriage is valid.
D) The marriage is automatically invalid.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence is her consent; crying without sound counts as silence, and the guardian's duty to seek permission is a sunnah.

Conditions of Valid Permission: Naming the Husband and Dower
Defining the requirements for a valid istidhan from the bride

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid permission from the bride and distinguish between required and recommended elements.

Core Idea
Permission requires naming the husband; silence counts only if it is silence from rejection, not absolute silence.

The bride's consent is crucial in marriage. Hanafi scholars detail how her silence or words are interpreted when the guardian seeks permission. This lesson unpacks the conditions for a valid istidhan, focusing on the necessity of naming the husband and the differing views on mentioning the dower. Understanding these details ensures that the marriage contract is based on genuine consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify valid silence
Determine what constitutes consent through silence. The passage specifies it must be silence from rejection, not absolute silence. If she speaks unrelated words, it is still considered silence.
e.g., saying 'Alhamdulillah' after being informed is not rejection.
2
Analyze the requirement to name the husband
Examine the ruling that the guardian must name the specific suitor. If he says 'I marry you to a man' without naming, silence is not consent. If he names one or more, silence grants him choice.
Naming a countable group (e.g., 'my cousins') is sufficient; an uncountable group (e.g., 'Banu Tamim') is not.
3
Distinguish laughter as consent
Laughter, even a smile, is normally consent. But mocking laughter does not indicate pleasure and is not consent.
The ruling is based on the indication of pleasure.
4
Examine the dower mention dispute
Three positions: (1) not required (al-Hidaya), (2) required (later scholars), (3) not required for father/grandfather. The mufta bihi is the later scholars' view.
The difference affects validity if dower is not mentioned in the permission.
5
Apply delegation scenarios
If the bride delegates (e.g., 'do what you wish'), a general statement suffices. The guardian cannot marry her to a man she previously rejected.
Similar to agency rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إنما جعل إذناً لدلالته على الرضا فإذا لم يدل على الرضا لم يكن إذناً. وأطلق في الاستئذان فانصرف إلى الكامل وهو بأن يسمى لها الزوج على وجه يقع لها به لها المهر؛ أما الأول فلا بد منه لتظهر رغبتها فيه من الظهيرية. وأما الثاني ففيه ثلاثة أقوال مصححة : قيل لا يشترط ذكر المهر في الاستئذان لأن للنكاح صحة بدونه وصححه في الهداية، وقيل يشترط ذكره لأن رغبتها تختلف باختلاف الصداق في القلة والكثرة وهو قول المتأخرين من مشايخنا كما في الذخيرة. وفي فتح القدير: إنه الأوجه وتفرع عليه أنه لو لم يذكر المهر لها قالوا: إن وهبها من رجل نفذ نكاحه لأنها رضت بنكاح لا تسمية فيه والنكاح بلفظ الهبة يوجب مهر المثل. وإن زوجها لا بمهر مسمى ينعقد نكاح الولي لأنها ما رضيت بتسمية الولي فلا ينعقد نكاح الولي إلا بإجازة مستقلة. كذا في الخانية وغيرها وهو مشكل، لأن مقتضى الاشتراط أن لا يصح الاستئذان إذا لم يذكره فلم يصح قولهما أنها رضيت بنكاح لا تسمية فيه فسكوتها إنما هو لعلمها بعدم صحة الاستئذان وقيل إن كان المزوج أباً أو جداً لا يشترط ذكر المهر عند سمى وقوعه فيمنع من إيقاعه لعدم تحقق الإذن فيه قوله : قالوا إن وهبها من رجل) قال في الفتح: يعني فوضها اهـ. وعزا المسألة إلى التجنيس معللة بأنه إذا وهبها فتمام العقد بالزوج والمرأة عالمة به، وإذا مهراً فتمامه به أيضاً. ثم قال : وهو فرع اشتراط التسمية في كون السكوت الرضى، ويجب كون الجواب في المسألة الأولى مقيداً بما إذا علمت بالتفويض تفريعاً على القول الآخر. قال في النهر : وبه اندفع إشكال البحر قوله : (وهو مشكل لأن مقتضى الاشتراط الخ) قال في الرمز : والجواب أن الذي رضيت به لم يوجد وما وجد إن لم ترض به أولاً فإجازتها كافية في نفاذه .
Then he said as in Fath al-Qadir. And he intended by silence: silence from rejection, not absolute silence, because if the news reaches her and she speaks foreign words, it is considered silence here, thus it is acceptance. If she says 'Praise to Allah, I choose myself' or says 'It is tanning, I do not want it', this is speech, so it is rejection. Similarly in al-Zahiriyya. And he applied it absolutely to laughter, so it includes a smile, and that is correct as in al-Qadir. And it is not refuted by the case where she laughs mockingly, for that is not permission, and upon that is the fatwa. Mocking laughter is not hidden from those present, because laughter is made permission due to its indication of pleasure; if it does not indicate pleasure, it is not permission. And he applied it absolutely to seeking permission, so it applies to the complete form, which is that he names the husband for her in a manner that the dower is mentioned; as for the first, it is necessary so that her desire for him becomes apparent as opposed to her desire away from him. If he says 'I marry you to a man' and she is silent, it is not permission. If he names someone or another and she is silent, he may marry her to whichever he wishes. Similarly, if he names a group in general, if they are countable, it is consent, such as 'from my neighbors' or 'my cousins' and they are such; if they are not countable, such as 'from Banu Tamim', it is not consent, as in al-Muhit. All this is if she did not delegate the matter to him. But if she says 'I am satisfied with what you do' after he says 'Some people are proposing to you' or 'Marry me to whomever you choose' etc., it is a valid permission as in al-Zahiriyya. And he does not have by this statement the right to marry her to a man whose marriage she had previously rejected, because the intended meaning of this generality is other than that, like an agency to marry a woman; the agent may not marry her to her divorced husband if the husband had complained about her to the agent and informed him of her divorce, as in al-Zahiriyya. As for the second, there are three corrected sayings: Some say the mention of the dower is not a condition in permission because marriage is valid without it, and this was corrected in al-Hidaya. Some say it is a condition because her desire differs with the difference of the dowry in smallness and largeness, and this is the statement of the later scholars among our shaykhs as in al-Dhakhira. In Fath al-Qadir: It is the most appropriate. And it branched from that that if he did not mention the dower to her, they said: If he gives her in marriage to a man as a gift, his marriage is executed because she consented to a marriage without a named dower, and marriage with the word 'gift' requires a dower of the like. And if he marries her without a named dower, the guardian's marriage is not concluded because she was not satisfied with the guardian's naming, so the guardian's marriage is not concluded except with an independent acceptance. Similarly in al-Khaniyya and others, and it is problematic, because the requirement of the condition is that the permission is not valid if he does not mention it, so their statement that she consented to a marriage without a named dower is not valid; her silence is only due to her knowledge that the permission is not valid. And it is said that if the marriage is contracted by the father or grandfather, mentioning the dower is not a condition when he names its occurrence, it prevents its execution due to the lack of verified permission in it. He said: They said: If he gives her in marriage to a man as a gift, he said in al-Fath: meaning he delegated her. And he attributed the issue to al-Tajnis, reasoned that if he gives her as a gift, the contract is completed by the husband and the woman is aware of it; if a dower, it is also completed by it. Then he said: It is a branch of the condition of naming in the silence being consent, and the answer in the first issue must be restricted to when she knows of the delegation, as a branch of the other saying. He said in al-Nahr: And by this the difficulty of al-Bahr is removed. He said: (It is problematic because the requirement of the condition...) He said in al-Ramz: The answer is that what she consented to did not exist, and what existed, if she did not initially consent to it, her acceptance is sufficient for its execution.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the conditions for a valid permission (istidhan) from the bride. It first clarifies that silence must be silence from rejection, not absolute silence; if she speaks unrelated words, it is still considered silence (and thus consent). If she explicitly rejects or makes a statement indicating lack of interest, it is rejection. The section on laughter states that even a smile is considered consent unless it is mocking laughter, which is not consent because it does not indicate pleasure. Then it discusses the complete form of seeking permission: the guardian must name the husband for her; if he says 'I marry you to a man' without naming, silence is not permission. If he names one or more men, silence gives him the choice. If he names a group, it is consent only if the group is countable and specific. If the bride has delegated the matter to the guardian, a general statement like 'do what you wish' suffices. Regarding the dower, there are three views: (1) mentioning the dower is not required (preferred in al-Hidaya), (2) it is required because the bride’s desire varies with the dower amount (preferred by later scholars), (3) a distinction: if the guardian is the father or grandfather, it is not required. The passage then discusses a problematic branch: if the guardian marries her without mentioning a dower (e.g., using the word 'gift'), the marriage is valid because she consented to a marriage without a specified dower, and the mahr al-mithl applies. But if the guardian tries to specify a dower later without her consent, it is invalid. The solution is that her subsequent acceptance can cure the defect. The text ends with a reconciliation of these issues, emphasizing that the silence is only valid if she knows the delegation.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukūt): Silence after being informed of the marriage; considered consent unless it is clear rejection.
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife; its mention in the permission is disputed as a requirement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guardian says to a virgin 'I marry you to a man' without naming the suitor, and she remains silent, what is the ruling?
A) The marriage is valid because silence implies consent.
B) The marriage is invalid because the permission must name the suitor.
C) The marriage is valid only if the dower is mentioned.
D) The silence is considered permission only if she smiles.

Key Takeaway
Valid permission requires naming the husband; silence counts only if it is silence from rejection.

Nikah — Marriage

Silence as Consent in Marriage: Ruling and Conditions
Ruling on silence as consent with conditions and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a woman's silence constitutes valid consent (idhn) for marriage and identify the conditions that invalidate it.

Core Idea
Silence is consent only when voluntary and in response to a complete, explicit proposal.

In Hanafi fiqh, a woman's silence after a guardian's proposal is generally considered acceptance. But this rule has crucial exceptions: the silence must be free from compulsion, and the proposal must be sufficiently specific. This lesson unpacks the conditions that make silence legally effective as consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the woman's silence after a marriage proposal. The ruling is that silence is considered consent (idhn), making the marriage valid if other conditions are met.
2
Determine the required completeness of the proposal
The passage states that the proposal must name the husband and, according to the preferred view, mention the mahr. If the guardian says 'I marry you to a man' without specification, silence is not consent.
This step involves distinguishing between complete and incomplete isti'dhan.
3
Recognize the condition of voluntariness
Silence must be by choice; if it is due to a sneeze, cough, or any compulsion, it is not valid. Similarly, laughter is considered consent only if it indicates pleasure, not mockery.
4
Identify exceptions and clarifications
The passage also covers: silence counts even if the woman is ignorant of the ruling; the guardian's identity (father/grandfather may have relaxed rules for mahr mention); and that phrases like 'that is up to you' are explicit permission.

It is known and called the Book of Marriage, Chapter on Guardians and Suitors. So if he said, as in Fath al-Qadir: 'And he intended by silence the silence of not rejecting, not absolute silence.' Because if the news reached her and she spoke a foreign speech, that is considered silence here and it is acceptance. If she said 'Praise be to God, I choose myself' or said 'It is a tanning treatment, I do not want it', this is one speech and is rejection. Similarly in al-Zahiriyyah. And he was absolute regarding laughter, so it includes smiling, which is the correct view as in al-Qadir. And what if she laughed mockingly is not an objection here, for it is not permission, and the fatwa is according to that. Mocking laughter is not hidden from whoever is present, because laughter is made permission only because it indicates consent; if it does not indicate consent, it is not permission. And he was absolute regarding seeking permission, so it refers to the complete form, which is that he names the husband in a way that the mahr becomes due to her. As for the first, it is necessary to show her desire for him from her lack of desire. If he says 'I marry you to a man' and she remains silent, it is not permission. If he named Zayd or Amr and she was silent, he may marry her to whichever he wishes. Similarly, if he named a group collectively, if they are countable, it is consent, like 'from my neighbors' or 'from my paternal cousins' if they are such. If they are not countable, like 'from Banu Tamim', it is not consent, as in al-Muhit. All this is if she did not delegate the matter to him. If she said 'I am pleased with what you do' after his words 'Indeed, a group seeks your hand' or 'Marry me to whomever you choose' and the like, it is a valid isti'dhan, as in al-Zahiriyyah. And he cannot by this statement marry her to a man whose marriage she first rejected, because the intended meaning of this generality is others, like the agency to marry a woman: the agent cannot marry her to her former husband if the husband had complained to the agent and informed him of the divorce, as in al-Zahiriyyah. As for the second, there are three corrected statements: it is said that mentioning the mahr is not a condition in isti'dhan because the marriage is valid without it, and this was corrected in al-Hidayah. It is said that mentioning it is a condition because her desire differs with the variation of the dower in its smallness and greatness, and this is the statement of the later scholars from our mashayikh, as in al-Dhakhirah. In Fath al-Qadir, it is the most appropriate. And it branches from this that if he did not mention the mahr to her, they said: if he gave her in marriage by hibah to a man, her marriage becomes effective because she consented to a marriage without specification, and marriage by the word hibah requires mahr al-mithl. And if he married her without a specified mahr, the marriage of the guardian is not valid because she did not consent to the guardian's specification, so the guardian's marriage is not valid except with an independent acceptance. Similarly in al-Khaniyyah and others, and it is problematic because the requirement of the condition is that the isti'dhan is not valid if he does not mention it, so their statement that she consented to a marriage without specification is not valid; her silence is only due to her knowledge of the invalidity of the isti'dhan. And it is said that if the one marrying is a father or grandfather, mentioning the mahr is not a condition when he names. Its occurrence prevents its execution due to lack of verification of permission in it. He said: 'They said: if he gives her in marriage to a man (by hibah)' – in al-Fath, he said: i.e., he delegates her. He attributed the issue to al-Tajnis, explaining that if he gives her by hibah, the completion of the contract is by the husband and the woman is aware of him, and if by specifying a mahr, its completion is also by him. Then he said: it is a branch of the condition of specification in silence being consent, and the answer in the first issue must be restricted to when she knows of the delegation, as a branch on the other statement. He said in al-Nahr: and by this the problem of al-Bahr is repelled. He said: (It is problematic because the requirement of the condition etc.) He said in al-Ramz: and the answer is that what she consented to did not occur, and what occurred, if she did not consent to it initially, then her acceptance is sufficient for its effectiveness. He is nearer because then he has the mentioned guardianship. If he seeks permission from one other than him who is nearer than him, her silence is not permission, and speech is necessary because the more distant with the nearer is like a stranger, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. For this subtlety, he used the term 'guardian' instead of 'relative'. Under 'guardian' enters the judge because he has the guardianship of recommendation in her marriage, hence he said in al-Khaniyyah: the judge in the absence of guardians is in the position of the guardian in that. So her silence suffices. Also enters the master in the marriage of a freed slave woman if she is an adult virgin, as in al-Qunyah. And if two equal guardians marry her, each to a man, and she accepts both simultaneously, they are void due to lack of priority. If she remains silent, they remain suspended until she accepts one by speech or action, and that is the apparent answer, as in al-Bada'i'. And the ruling of the messenger of the guardian is like that of the guardian because he stands in his place, so her silence suffices. And most of the later scholars chose this, as in al-Dhakhirah. And what is meant by silence is that which is by choice. As in al-Khaniyyah: if a sneeze or cough overtook her when she was informed, then when it passed she said 'I am not pleased', her rejection is valid. Similarly, if his mouth was taken and then released, and she said 'I am not pleased', because that silence was due to compulsion. And it was absolute to include whether she knows the ruling or is ignorant, and it includes when he seeks permission for himself. As in al-Jawami': if he seeks permission from his paternal cousin for himself while she is an adult and she is silent, then he marries her to himself, it is permissible because he became an agent by her silence. And it was restricted to silence because if she refused him, he returns. And her saying 'I do not want the husband' or 'I do not want so-and-so' is the same in that it is rejection, whether before or after the marriage. And that is the chosen view, as in al-Dhakhirah. If she said after the consultation 'someone else is more suitable than him', it is not permission; it is an acceptance after the contract, as in it also. And they distinguished between them: because it may be permission or not. Therefore, before the marriage, the marriage did not occur, so it is not valid with doubt. After the marriage, it occurred, so it is not invalidated by doubt. Similarly in al-Zahiriyyah, and it is problematic because it is not a marriage except after validity, and it is after permission, so it appears that it is not permission in both. And her saying 'that is up to you' is permission absolutely, unlike her saying 'you know better' or 'you are more informed of the benefit' or 'better knowing' because for the father, he may take the husband for the mahr except with an agency from her. So it contradicts what is here. Consider his saying: (it is permissible because he became an agent by her silence; if he married her to himself and the news reached her and she was silent, it is not permissible, as will come later.) His saying: (as in it also) – the pronoun refers to al-Dhakhirah. Then he mentioned his saying 'and they distinguished between them' up to his words 'similarly in al-Zahiriyyah' after his words 'as in it also', then his subsequent statement 'and it is problematic etc.' as in this copy is better than in other copies, where he mentioned later his words 'as in it also' and 'and he intended by silence' up to his words 'similarly in al-Zahiriyyah', then his words 'and her saying that is up to you' up to his words 'as in Fath al-Qadir', then his words 'and they distinguished between them', then his words 'and it is problematic'. His saying: (and her saying that is up to you is permission because it is only mentioned for delegation, unlike what comes after because it may be mentioned for hinting at lack of benefit in it. Similarly in al-Fath. His saying: (and it is problematic because it is not a marriage etc.) – the source of the problem is for the author of al-Fath, and he answered it in al-Ramz by saying: the answer is that if the contract occurred and after it comes something that may be considered affirmation or rejection, due to its occurrence, the likelihood of affirmation outweighs. And if it comes before it, something that may be permission or not, rejection outweighs due to lack of... (text breaks)
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, discusses the legal ruling of silence (sukut) as consent in marriage, specifically in the context of isti'dhan (seeking permission from the woman). It belongs to the chapter on guardians and suitors. The passage is an expansion of the matn, clarifying nuances and providing justifications. The core ruling: a virgin's silence after a proper proposal is legally considered acceptance (idhn). However, this is conditional: first, the silence must be in response to an explicit isti'dhan that clearly names the husband and, according to many scholars, mentions the mahr. Second, the silence must be voluntary — laughter, sneezing, coughing, or any compulsion invalidates it. The passage also addresses whether silence counts if the woman is ignorant of the ruling (yes, according to the text). For laughter, the text distinguishes between normal laughter (indicating consent) and mocking laughter (not consent). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether mentioning the mahr is required: one view (from al-Hidayah) says no, because marriage is valid without it; another (later scholars) says yes, because desire varies with the dower. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) per Fath al-Qadir is that mentioning the mahr is the most appropriate. The passage then discusses the effect of the guardian's identity: if the one seeking permission is a father or grandfather, there is a relaxation on mentioning the mahr. It also covers rules for equal guardians, the messenger of the guardian, and the distinction between phrases like 'that is up to you' (permission) vs. 'you know better' (not permission). Throughout, the commentary follows the author's order, explaining each sub-rule with legal reasoning drawn from Hanafi sources.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence in response to a proposal; legally considered consent if voluntary and after proper isti'dhan.
إذن (idhn): Permission or consent given by a woman for her marriage.
استئذان (isti'dhan): The act of the guardian seeking permission from the woman to marry her.
مهر (mahr): The dower or bride-price that the husband must give to the wife.
ولي (wali): Guardian who has the authority to contract the woman's marriage; includes father, grandfather, judge, and master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following would NOT be considered valid consent (idhn) through silence?
A) The woman remains silent after the guardian says 'I marry you to Zayd with a mahr of 500 dirhams.'
B) The woman sneezes just as the guardian mentions the proposal, then remains silent.
C) The woman says 'That is up to you' to the guardian.
D) The woman laughs normally after the guardian's proposal and remains silent.

Key Takeaway
A woman's silence is valid consent only when it is voluntary and in response to a proposal that clearly identifies the suitor.

The Condition of Mentioning Mahr in Isti'dhan: Hanafi Internal Dispute
Explaining the internal Hanafi dispute on mahr in isti'dhan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on mentioning mahr in isti'dhan, explain the evidence for each, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Mentioning the mahr in isti'dhan is required according to later Hanafi scholars; omitting it makes silence ambiguous.

While the Hanafi school accepts silence as consent, there is an important internal dispute about whether the guardian must mention the mahr when seeking permission. The earlier view (from al-Hidayah) says it is not necessary, but later scholars require it because the amount of dower affects the woman's inclination. This lesson examines the arguments and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
First position: mentioning mahr is not required because marriage is valid without it (mufawwid). Second position: it is required because desire varies with the dower amount.
2
Examine the evidence for each
For non-requirement: analogy with a marriage without mahr (fasid but can be valid). For requirement: the woman's consent is contingent on the dower; silence cannot be taken as consent to an unknown amount.
3
Identify the mufta bihi and its basis
The later scholars' view (required) is considered the most appropriate (al-awjah) by Fath al-Qadir and is the operative position for fatwa.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a guardian marries without mentioning mahr, according to the first view the marriage is valid with her silence; according to the second, it is not valid unless she explicitly accepts afterwards.
