المعرفة ويسمى كتاب النكاح باب الأولياء والأكفاء فلو قال كما في فتح القدير. وأراد بالسكوت السكوت عن الرد لا مطلق السكوت لأنه لو بلغها الخبر فتكلمت بكلام أجنبي فهو سكوت هنا فيكون إجازة، فلو قالت الحمد لله اخترت نفسي أو قالت هو دباغ لا أريده فهذا كلام واحد فكان رداً. كذا في الظهيرية . وأطلق في الضحك فشمل التبسم وهو الصحيح كما في القدير. ولا يرد عليه ما إذا ضحكت مستهزئة فإنه لا يكون إذناً وعليه الفتوى وضحك الاستهزاء لا يخفى على من يحضره لأن الضحك إنما جعل إذناً لدلالته على الرضا فإذا لم يدل على الرضا لم يكن إذناً. وأطلق في الاستئذان فانصرف إلى الكامل وهو بأن يسمى لها الزوج على وجه يقع لها به لها المهر؛ أما الأول فلا بد منه لتظهر رغبتها فيه من رغبتها عنه، أزوجك من رجل فسكتت لا يكون إذناً، فلو سمى فلاناً أو فلاناً فسكتت فله أن يزوجها من أيهما شاء، وكذا لو سمى جماعة مجملاً فإن كانوا يحصون فهو رضا نحو من جيراني أو بني عمي وهم كذلك، وإن كانوا لا يحصون نحو من بني تميم فليس برضا كما في المحيط. وهذا كله إذا لم تفوض الأمر إليه، أما إذا قالت أنا راضية بما تفعله أنت بعد قوله إن أقواماً يخطبونك أو زوجني ممن تختاره ونحوه فهو استئذان صحيح كما في الظهيرية، وليس له بهذه المقالة أن يزوجها من رجل ردت نكاحه أولاً لأن المراد بهذا العموم غيره كالتوكيل بتزويج امرأة ليس للوكيل أن يزوجه مطلقته إذا كان الزوج قد شكا منها للوكيل وأعلمه بطلاقها كما في الظهيرية. وأما الثاني ففيه ثلاثة أقوال مصححة : قيل لا يشترط ذكر المهر في الاستئذان لأن للنكاح صحة بدونه وصححه في الهداية، وقيل يشترط ذكره لأن رغبتها تختلف باختلاف الصداق في القلة والكثرة وهو قول المتأخرين من مشايخنا كما في الذخيرة. وفي فتح القدير: إنه الأوجه وتفرع عليه أنه لو لم يذكر المهر لها قالوا: إن وهبها من رجل نفذ نكاحها لأنها رضت بنكاح لا تسمية فيه والنكاح بلفظ الهبة يوجب مهر المثل. وإن زوجها لا بمهر مسمى ينعقد نكاح الولي لأنها ما رضيت بتسمية الولي فلا ينعقد نكاح الولي إلا بإجازة مستقلة. كذا في الخانية وغيرها وهو مشكل، لأن مقتضى الاشتراط أن لا يصح الاستئذان إذا لم يذكره فلم يصح قولهما أنها رضيت بنكاح لا تسمية فيه فسكوتها إنما هو لعلمها بعدم صحة الاستئذان وقيل إن كان المزوج أباً أو جداً لا يشترط ذكر المهر عند سمى وقوعه فيمنع من إيقاعه لعدم تحقق الإذن فيه قوله : قالوا إن وهبها من رجل) قال في الفتح: يعني فوضها اهـ. وعزا المسألة إلى التجنيس معللة بأنه إذا وهبها فتمام العقد بالزوج والمرأة عالمة به، وإذا مهراً فتمامه به أيضاً. ثم قال : وهو فرع اشتراط التسمية في كون السكوت الرضى، ويجب كون الجواب في المسألة الأولى مقيداً بما إذا علمت بالتفويض تفريعاً على القول الآخر. قال في النهر : وبه اندفع إشكال البحر قوله : (وهو مشكل لأن مقتضى الاشتراط الخ) قال في الرمز : والجواب أن الذي رضيت به لم يوجد وما وجد إن لم ترض به أولاً فإجازتها كافية في نفاذه . أقرب منه لأنه حينئذ له الولاية المذكورة، فلو استأذنها من غيره أقرب منه فلا يكون سكوتها إذناً ، ولا بد من النطق لأن الأبعد مع الأقرب كالأجنبي كما ذكره الأسبيجابي، ولهذه النكتة عبر بالولي دون القريب. ودخل تحت الولي القاضي لأن له ولاية الاستحباب في نكاحها ولذا قال في الخانية والقاضي عند عدم الأولياء بمنزلة الولي في ذلك اهـ. فيكفي سكوتها . ودخل أيضاً المولى في نكاح المعتقة إذا كانت بكراً بالغة كما في القنية: ولو زوجها وليان متساويان كل واحد منهما من رجل فأجازتهما معاً بطلا لعدم الأولوية، وإن سكتت بقيا موقوفين حتى تجيز أحدهما بالقول أو بالفعل وهو ظاهر الجواب كما في البدائع. وحكم رسول الولي كالولي لأنه قائم مقامه فيكفي سكوتها. واختاره أكثر المتأخرين كما في الذخيرة. والمراد بالسكوت ما كان عن اختيار لما في الخانية لو أخذها العطاس أو السعال حين أخبرت فلما ذهب العطاس أو السعال قالت لا أرضى صح ردها، وكذا لو أخذ فمها ثم ترك فقالت لا أرضى لأن ذلك السكوت كان عن اضطرار. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت عالمة بحكمه أو جاهلة، وشمل ما إذا استأذنها لنفسه لما في الجوامع : لو استأذن بنت عمه لنفسه وهي بالغة فسكتت فزوجها من نفسه جاز لأنه صار وكيلاً بسكوتها ا.هـ. وقيد بالسكوت لأنها لو ردته ارتد، وقولها لا أريد الزوج أو لا أريد فلاناً سواء في أنه رد، سواء كان قبل التزويج أو بعده، وهو المختار كما في الذخيرة ولو قالت بعد الاستثمار غيره أولى منه فليس بإذن وهو إجازة بعد العقد كما فيها أيضاً وفرقوا بينهما بأنه يحتمل الإذن وعدمه فقبل النكاح لم يكن النكاح فلا يجوز بالشك، وبعد النكاح كان فلا يبطل بالشك. كذا في الظهيرية وهو مشكل، لأنه لا يكون نكاحاً إلا بعد الصحة وهو بعد الإذن فالظاهر أنه ليس بإذن فيهما، وقولها ذلك إليك إذن مطلقاً بخلاف قولها أنت أعلم أو أنت بالمصلحة أخبر وبالأحسن أعلم بكر للأب أن يأخذ الزوج بالمهر إلا بوكالة منها .اهـ. فهو مخالف لما هنا تأمل قوله : (جاز لأنه صار وكيلاً بسكوتها أما لو زوجها لنفسه فبلغها الخبر فسكتت فإنه لا يجوز كما سيأتي بعد ورقة قوله : (كما فيها أيضاً) الضمير راجع إلى الذخيرة. ثم إن ذكر قوله وفرقوا بينهما إلى قوله «كذا في الظهيرية» عقب قوله «كما فيها أيضاً ثم إعقابه بقوله وهو مشكل الخ» كما في هذه النسخة أحسن مما في عامة النسخ حيث ذكر فيما بعد قوله كما فيها أيضاً وأراد بالسكوت إلى قوله «كذا في الظهيرية» ثم قوله «وقولها ذلك إليك» إلى قوله كما في فتح القدير ثم قوله وفرقوا بينهما» ثم قوله «وهو مشكل» قوله : (وقولها ذلك إليك إذن لأنه إنما يذكر للتوكيل بخلاف ما بعده لأنه قد يذكر للتعريض بعدم المصلحة فيه. كذا في الفتح قوله : (وهو) مشكل لأنه لا يكون نكاحاً (الخ) أصل الإشكال لصاحب الفتح وقد أجاب عنه في الرمز بقوله : ويجاب بأن العقد إذا وقع وورد بعده ما يحتمل كونه تقديراً له وكونه رداً ترجح بوقوعه احتمال التقرير، وإذا ورد قبله ما يحتمل الإذن وعدمه ترجح الرد لعدم
It is known and called the Book of Marriage, Chapter on Guardians and Suitors. So if he said, as in Fath al-Qadir: 'And he intended by silence the silence of not rejecting, not absolute silence.' Because if the news reached her and she spoke a foreign speech, that is considered silence here and it is acceptance. If she said 'Praise be to God, I choose myself' or said 'It is a tanning treatment, I do not want it', this is one speech and is rejection. Similarly in al-Zahiriyyah. And he was absolute regarding laughter, so it includes smiling, which is the correct view as in al-Qadir. And what if she laughed mockingly is not an objection here, for it is not permission, and the fatwa is according to that. Mocking laughter is not hidden from whoever is present, because laughter is made permission only because it indicates consent; if it does not indicate consent, it is not permission. And he was absolute regarding seeking permission, so it refers to the complete form, which is that he names the husband in a way that the mahr becomes due to her. As for the first, it is necessary to show her desire for him from her lack of desire. If he says 'I marry you to a man' and she remains silent, it is not permission. If he named Zayd or Amr and she was silent, he may marry her to whichever he wishes. Similarly, if he named a group collectively, if they are countable, it is consent, like 'from my neighbors' or 'from my paternal cousins' if they are such. If they are not countable, like 'from Banu Tamim', it is not consent, as in al-Muhit. All this is if she did not delegate the matter to him. If she said 'I am pleased with what you do' after his words 'Indeed, a group seeks your hand' or 'Marry me to whomever you choose' and the like, it is a valid isti'dhan, as in al-Zahiriyyah. And he cannot by this statement marry her to a man whose marriage she first rejected, because the intended meaning of this generality is others, like the agency to marry a woman: the agent cannot marry her to her former husband if the husband had complained to the agent and informed him of the divorce, as in al-Zahiriyyah. As for the second, there are three corrected statements: it is said that mentioning the mahr is not a condition in isti'dhan because the marriage is valid without it, and this was corrected in al-Hidayah. It is said that mentioning it is a condition because her desire differs with the variation of the dower in its smallness and greatness, and this is the statement of the later scholars from our mashayikh, as in al-Dhakhirah. In Fath al-Qadir, it is the most appropriate. And it branches from this that if he did not mention the mahr to her, they said: if he gave her in marriage by hibah to a man, her marriage becomes effective because she consented to a marriage without specification, and marriage by the word hibah requires mahr al-mithl. And if he married her without a specified mahr, the marriage of the guardian is not valid because she did not consent to the guardian's specification, so the guardian's marriage is not valid except with an independent acceptance. Similarly in al-Khaniyyah and others, and it is problematic because the requirement of the condition is that the isti'dhan is not valid if he does not mention it, so their statement that she consented to a marriage without specification is not valid; her silence is only due to her knowledge of the invalidity of the isti'dhan. And it is said that if the one marrying is a father or grandfather, mentioning the mahr is not a condition when he names. Its occurrence prevents its execution due to lack of verification of permission in it. He said: 'They said: if he gives her in marriage to a man (by hibah)' – in al-Fath, he said: i.e., he delegates her. He attributed the issue to al-Tajnis, explaining that if he gives her by hibah, the completion of the contract is by the husband and the woman is aware of him, and if by specifying a mahr, its completion is also by him. Then he said: it is a branch of the condition of specification in silence being consent, and the answer in the first issue must be restricted to when she knows of the delegation, as a branch on the other statement. He said in al-Nahr: and by this the problem of al-Bahr is repelled. He said: (It is problematic because the requirement of the condition etc.) He said in al-Ramz: and the answer is that what she consented to did not occur, and what occurred, if she did not consent to it initially, then her acceptance is sufficient for its effectiveness. He is nearer because then he has the mentioned guardianship. If he seeks permission from one other than him who is nearer than him, her silence is not permission, and speech is necessary because the more distant with the nearer is like a stranger, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. For this subtlety, he used the term 'guardian' instead of 'relative'. Under 'guardian' enters the judge because he has the guardianship of recommendation in her marriage, hence he said in al-Khaniyyah: the judge in the absence of guardians is in the position of the guardian in that. So her silence suffices. Also enters the master in the marriage of a freed slave woman if she is an adult virgin, as in al-Qunyah. And if two equal guardians marry her, each to a man, and she accepts both simultaneously, they are void due to lack of priority. If she remains silent, they remain suspended until she accepts one by speech or action, and that is the apparent answer, as in al-Bada'i'. And the ruling of the messenger of the guardian is like that of the guardian because he stands in his place, so her silence suffices. And most of the later scholars chose this, as in al-Dhakhirah. And what is meant by silence is that which is by choice. As in al-Khaniyyah: if a sneeze or cough overtook her when she was informed, then when it passed she said 'I am not pleased', her rejection is valid. Similarly, if his mouth was taken and then released, and she said 'I am not pleased', because that silence was due to compulsion. And it was absolute to include whether she knows the ruling or is ignorant, and it includes when he seeks permission for himself. As in al-Jawami': if he seeks permission from his paternal cousin for himself while she is an adult and she is silent, then he marries her to himself, it is permissible because he became an agent by her silence. And it was restricted to silence because if she refused him, he returns. And her saying 'I do not want the husband' or 'I do not want so-and-so' is the same in that it is rejection, whether before or after the marriage. And that is the chosen view, as in al-Dhakhirah. If she said after the consultation 'someone else is more suitable than him', it is not permission; it is an acceptance after the contract, as in it also. And they distinguished between them: because it may be permission or not. Therefore, before the marriage, the marriage did not occur, so it is not valid with doubt. After the marriage, it occurred, so it is not invalidated by doubt. Similarly in al-Zahiriyyah, and it is problematic because it is not a marriage except after validity, and it is after permission, so it appears that it is not permission in both. And her saying 'that is up to you' is permission absolutely, unlike her saying 'you know better' or 'you are more informed of the benefit' or 'better knowing' because for the father, he may take the husband for the mahr except with an agency from her. So it contradicts what is here. Consider his saying: (it is permissible because he became an agent by her silence; if he married her to himself and the news reached her and she was silent, it is not permissible, as will come later.) His saying: (as in it also) – the pronoun refers to al-Dhakhirah. Then he mentioned his saying 'and they distinguished between them' up to his words 'similarly in al-Zahiriyyah' after his words 'as in it also', then his subsequent statement 'and it is problematic etc.' as in this copy is better than in other copies, where he mentioned later his words 'as in it also' and 'and he intended by silence' up to his words 'similarly in al-Zahiriyyah', then his words 'and her saying that is up to you' up to his words 'as in Fath al-Qadir', then his words 'and they distinguished between them', then his words 'and it is problematic'. His saying: (and her saying that is up to you is permission because it is only mentioned for delegation, unlike what comes after because it may be mentioned for hinting at lack of benefit in it. Similarly in al-Fath. His saying: (and it is problematic because it is not a marriage etc.) – the source of the problem is for the author of al-Fath, and he answered it in al-Ramz by saying: the answer is that if the contract occurred and after it comes something that may be considered affirmation or rejection, due to its occurrence, the likelihood of affirmation outweighs. And if it comes before it, something that may be permission or not, rejection outweighs due to lack of... (text breaks)

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, discusses the legal ruling of silence (sukut) as consent in marriage, specifically in the context of isti'dhan (seeking permission from the woman). It belongs to the chapter on guardians and suitors. The passage is an expansion of the matn, clarifying nuances and providing justifications. The core ruling: a virgin's silence after a proper proposal is legally considered acceptance (idhn). However, this is conditional: first, the silence must be in response to an explicit isti'dhan that clearly names the husband and, according to many scholars, mentions the mahr. Second, the silence must be voluntary — laughter, sneezing, coughing, or any compulsion invalidates it. The passage also addresses whether silence counts if the woman is ignorant of the ruling (yes, according to the text). For laughter, the text distinguishes between normal laughter (indicating consent) and mocking laughter (not consent). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether mentioning the mahr is required: one view (from al-Hidayah) says no, because marriage is valid without it; another (later scholars) says yes, because desire varies with the dower. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) per Fath al-Qadir is that mentioning the mahr is the most appropriate. The passage then discusses the effect of the guardian's identity: if the one seeking permission is a father or grandfather, there is a relaxation on mentioning the mahr. It also covers rules for equal guardians, the messenger of the guardian, and the distinction between phrases like 'that is up to you' (permission) vs. 'you know better' (not permission). Throughout, the commentary follows the author's order, explaining each sub-rule with legal reasoning drawn from Hanafi sources.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower specified in the marriage contract; its mention in isti'dhan is disputed.
الاستئذان الكامل (al-isti'dhan al-kamil): Complete seeking of permission that includes naming the husband and (per mufta bihi) mentioning the mahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the passage, what is the ruling if a guardian marries his daughter without mentioning the mahr, and she remains silent?
A) The marriage is valid because silence is consent.
B) The marriage is not valid because the isti'dhan was incomplete.
C) The marriage is valid only if the guardian is the father.
D) The marriage is valid but the mahr is set to mahr al-mithl.

Key Takeaway
Mentioning the mahr in the isti'dhan is required for silence to be considered valid consent; otherwise, the marriage must be explicitly accepted.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Silence of a Virgin Bride as Consent
Commentary on the ruling that a virgin's silence is consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a virgin's silence constitutes consent, identify the conditions under which it applies, and explain the distinction between agency and ratification.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence is consent when asked by her guardian, based on the hadith and her modesty.

In Hanafi marriage law, the consent of a virgin bride is presumed from her silence when asked by her guardian. This ruling is rooted in a well-known hadith and the recognition that modesty may prevent a virgin from verbally expressing consent. However, this principle has specific conditions and exceptions that must be carefully understood, particularly regarding the identity of the guardian and the voluntariness of the silence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the guardian seeking permission from a virgin for marriage. The ruling is that her silence counts as consent, making the marriage valid.
This is based on the hadith: 'The virgin is asked for permission; if she remains silent, she has consented.'
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The text also mentions that laughter is even stronger evidence of consent.
The hadith is classified as ahad (single narration) but is widely accepted.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'if she remains silent, she has consented' directly indicates that silence is a valid form of consent. The Hanafi school interprets this as a concession due to a virgin's shyness.
The imperative 'tusta'mar' (she is asked) indicates recommendation, not obligation.
4
Identify conditions and exceptions
Silence is only valid if the guardian is the closest male relative (else explicit speech needed), if the silence is voluntary (not due to sneezing, coughing, or duress), and if the woman is adult and sane.
Distinction: silence before the contract is agency (tawkeel); silence after the contract is ratification (ijazah).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr
The passage discusses a dispute with Zufr regarding the father's right to receive the mahr without the daughter's presence. The relied-upon position (Abu Yusuf later, al-Khassaf) allows it.
The father may also litigate for the mahr, but if the daughter forbids him, he cannot receive it.

كتاب النكاح باب الأولياء والأكفاء
أقرب منه لأنه حينئذ له الولاية المذكورة، فلو استأذنها من غيره أقرب منه فلا يكون سكوتها إذناً، ولا بد من النطق لأن الأبعد مع الأقرب كالأجنبي كما ذكره الأسبيجابي، ولهذه النكتة عبر بالولي دون القريب. ودخل تحت الولي القاضي لأن له ولاية الاستحباب في نكاحها ولذا قال في الخانية والقاضي عند عدم الأولياء بمنزلة الولي في ذلك اهـ. فيكفي سكوتها. ودخل أيضاً المولى في نكاح المعتقة إذا كانت بكراً بالغة كما في القنية: ولو زوجها وليان متساويان كل واحد منهما من رجل فأجازتهما معاً بطلا لعدم الأولوية، وإن سكتت بقيا موقوفين حتى تجيز أحدهما بالقول أو بالفعل وهو ظاهر الجواب كما في البدائع. وحكم رسول الولي كالولي لأنه قائم مقامه فيكفي سكوتها. واختاره أكثر المتأخرين كما في الذخيرة. والمراد بالسكوت ما كان عن اختيار لما في الخانية لو أخذها العطاس أو السعال حين أخبرت فلما ذهب العطاس أو السعال قالت لا أرضى صح ردها، وكذا لو أخذ فمها ثم ترك فقالت لا أرضى لأن ذلك السكوت كان عن اضطرار. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت عالمة بحكمه أو جاهلة، وشمل ما إذا استأذنها لنفسه لما في الجوامع: لو استأذن بنت عمه لنفسه وهي بالغة فسكتت فزوجها من نفسه جاز لأنه صار وكيلاً بسكوتها ا.هـ. وقيد بالسكوت لأنها لو ردته ارتد، وقولها لا أريد الزوج أو لا أريد فلاناً سواء في أنه رد، سواء كان قبل التزويج أو بعده، وهو المختار كما في الذخيرة ولو قالت بعد الاستثمار غيره أولى منه فليس بإذن وهو إجازة بعد العقد كما فيها أيضاً وفرقوا بينهما بأنه يحتمل الإذن وعدمه فقبل النكاح لم يكن النكاح فلا يجوز بالشك، وبعد النكاح كان فلا يبطل بالشك. كذا في الظهيرية وهو مشكل، لأنه لا يكون نكاحاً إلا بعد الصحة وهو بعد الإذن فالظاهر أنه ليس بإذن فيهما، وقولها ذلك إليك إذن مطلقاً بخلاف قولها أنت أعلم أو أنت بالمصلحة أخبر وبالأحسن أعلم
للأب أن يأخذ الزوج بالمهر إلا بوكالة منها .اهـ. فهو مخالف لما هنا تأمل قوله: (جاز لأنه صار وكيلاً بسكوتها أما لو زوجها لنفسه فبلغها الخبر فسكتت فإنه لا يجوز كما سيأتي بعد ورقة قوله: (كما فيها أيضاً) الضمير راجع إلى الذخيرة. ثم إن ذكر قوله وفرقوا بينهما إلى قوله «كذا في الظهيرية» عقب قوله «كما فيها أيضاً» ثم إعقابه بقوله وهو مشكل الخ» كما في هذه النسخة أحسن مما في عامة النسخ حيث ذكر فيما بعد قوله كما فيها أيضاً وأراد بالسكوت إلى قوله «كذا في الظهيرية» ثم قوله «وقولها ذلك إليك» إلى قوله كما في فتح القدير ثم قوله وفرقوا بينهما» ثم قوله «وهو مشكل» قوله: (وقولها ذلك إليك إذن لأنه إنما يذكر للتوكيل بخلاف ما بعده لأنه قد يذكر للتعريض بعدم المصلحة فيه. كذا في الفتح قوله: (وهو) مشكل لأنه لا يكون نكاحاً (الخ) أصل الإشكال لصاحب الفتح وقد أجاب عنه في الرمز بقوله: ويجاب بأن العقد إذا وقع وورد بعده ما يحتمل كونه تقديراً له وكونه رداً ترجح بوقوعه احتمال التقرير، وإذا ورد قبله ما يحتمل الإذن وعدمه ترجح الرد لعدم
فسكتت أو ضحكت أو زوجها فبلغها الخبر فسكتت فهو إذن وإن استأذنها غير الولي
مر
صدق لأنه ليس له حق القبض فإذا قبض بأمر الزوج كان أمانة للزوج عنده فيصدق في رد الأمانة عليه كالمودع إذا قال رددت الوديعة ا هـ. وفي الذخيرة: للأب المخاصمة مع الزوج في مهر البكر البالغة كما له أن يقبضه ولا يشترط إحضار المرأة للاستيفاء عندنا خلافاً لزفر. فإن قال الزوج للقاضي الأب فليقبض المهر مني وليسلم الجارية إلي فإن القاضي يقول له اقبض المهر وادفعها إليه فإن امتنع الأب من ذلك ليس على الزوج دفعه إليه، ولو قال الأب ليست في منزلي ولا أعرف مكانها فليس على الزوج دفعه أيضاً، وإن قال الأب هي في منزلي وإنما أقبض المهر وأجهزها به وأسلمها إليه فالقاضي يأمر الزوج بالدفع إليه فإنه طلب الزوج كفيلاً بالمهر فالقاضي يأمر الأب بكفيل بالمهر، فإذا أتى بكفيل أمر الزوج بدفع المهر، فإن سلم البنت إليه بريء الكفيل، وإن عجز عن ذلك توصل الزوج إلى حقه بالكفيل فيعتدل النظر من الجانبين، وهكذا كان يقول أبو يوسف أولاً ثم رجع وقال: القاضي يأمر الأب أن يجعل المرأة مهيأة للتسليم ويحضرها ويأمر الزوج بدفع المهر والأب بتسليم البنت فيكون دفع الزوج المهر عند تسليمها نفسها إلى الزوج لأن النظر لا يحصل للزوج بالكفالة لأنه لا يصل إلى المرأة لا محالة بالكفالة، وإنما النظر في تسليم المهر بحضرتها. قال الخصاف: وهذا أحسن القولين ا وفي الخلاصة: الأب إذا جعل بعض مهر البنت آجلاً والبعض عاجلاً البعض كما هو المعهود ثم قال إن لم تجز البنت الهبة فقد ضمنت من مالي أن أؤدي قدر الهبة У يصح هذا الضمان اهـ. ووهب
قوله: (وإن استأذنها الولي فسكتت أو ضحكت أو زوجها فبلغها الخبر فسكتت فهو إذن) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام البكر تستأمر في نفسها فإن سكتت فقد رضيت» ولأن حيثية الرضا فيه راجحة لأنها تستحي عن إظهار الرغبة لا عن الرد، والضحك أدل على الرضا من السكوت والأصل أن سكوت البكر للاستثمار وكالة وللعقد إجازة كما ذكره الأسبيجابي، فالإذن في عبارة المختصر مشترك بين الوكالة والإجازة، ففي المسألة الأولى توكيل، وفي الثانية إجازة. ويتفرع على كونه توكيلاً أن الولي لو استأذنها في رجل معين فقالت يصلح أو سكتت ثم لما خرج قالت لا أرضى ولم يعلم الولي بعدم رضاها فزوجها فهو كما في الظهيرية، لأن الوكيل لا ينعزل حتى يعلم وليس السكوت إذناً حقيقياً لما صحيح في الخانية من الأيمان: إذا حلفت أن لا تأذن في تزويجها فسكتت عند الاستثمار لا تحنث والمراد بالولي من له ولاية استحباب لأن الكلام في البالغة العاقلة فيفيد أنه ليس لها ولي
اهـ.
القبض أما إذا انهته فلا يملكه ولا يبرأ الزوج منه. صرح بذلك كثير من علمائنا فاعلم ذلك اهـ. وقد ر التصريح به من المؤلف أيضاً قوله: (وفي الذخيرة للأب المخاصمة الخ قال الرملي: أي بغير وكالة منها كما في المضمرات. وفي مجمع الفتاوى: رجل تزوج امرأة بكراً ودفع المهر إلى الأب برئ وليس له القبض أما إذا انهته فلا يملكه ولا يبرأ الزوج منه. صرح بذلك كثير من علمائنا فاعلم ذلك اهـ.
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Guardians and Compatibility. Closer [guardian] because then he has the mentioned guardianship. If she is asked by someone other than a closer guardian, her silence is not permission, and speech is necessary because a distant relative with a closer one is like a stranger, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. For this reason, the author expressed 'wali' instead of 'relative'. The judge is included under wali because he has a recommended guardianship in her marriage; hence in al-Khaniyya: the judge in absence of guardians is like the guardian in that regard, so her silence suffices. Also included is the master in the marriage of an emancipated slave if she is a virgin adult, as in al-Quniyya. If two equal guardians each marry her to a man and she accepts both, both are void due to lack of preference; if she remains silent, they remain suspended until she accepts one by word or action, as in al-Bada'i. The ruling of the guardian's messenger is like the guardian because he stands in his place, so her silence suffices. Most later scholars chose this, as in al-Dhakhira. Intended by silence is that which is voluntary; in al-Khaniyya, if a sneeze or cough seized her when informed, and when it passed she said 'I do not consent', her rejection is valid; similarly if her mouth was covered then released and she said 'I do not consent', because that silence was by compulsion. It is general, including whether she knows the ruling or is ignorant, and includes when he asks her for himself; in al-Jawami': if he asks his cousin for himself while she is adult and she remains silent, then he marries her to himself, it is valid because he becomes an agent by her silence. It is conditioned by silence because if she rejects, she rejects; and her saying 'I do not want marriage' or 'I do not want so-and-so' is the same as a rejection, whether before marriage or after, which is the chosen opinion as in al-Dhakhira. If she says after consultation 'someone else is better than him', it is not permission but is consent after contract, as there as well; they distinguished between them because it admits possibility of permission and its opposite, so before marriage there is no marriage and it is not allowed due to doubt, and after marriage there is marriage and it is not invalidated by doubt. Thus in al-Zahiriyya, but it is problematic because there is no marriage except after validity, which is after permission, so apparently it is not permission in both. Her saying 'that is up to you' is permission absolutely, unlike her saying 'you know better' or 'you are more informed about benefit and better'. For the father to take the mahr from the husband except by her agency. So it is contrary to what is here. Consider his saying: (valid because he becomes an agent by her silence; but if he married her to himself and the news reached her and she stayed silent, it is not valid as will come after a leaf). His saying: (as also in it) the pronoun refers to al-Dhakhira. Then mentioning his saying 'and they distinguished...' up to 'thus in al-Zahiriyya' after his saying 'as also in it' then his afterword 'it is problematic' etc. as in this copy is better than in common copies where after his saying 'as also in it' he intended 'with silence' up to 'thus in al-Zahiriyya' then his saying 'and her saying that is up to you' up to 'as in Fath al-Qadir' then his saying 'and they distinguished' then his saying 'it is problematic'. His saying: (and her saying 'that is up to you' is permission because it is mentioned for agency, unlike what follows because it may be said to allude to lack of benefit in it). Thus in al-Fath. His saying: (it is) problematic because there is no marriage (etc.) the origin of the problem is from the author of al-Fath, and he answered it in al-Ramz with his saying: it is answered that when the contract occurs and after it comes what admits of being ratification or rejection, the probability of ratification is preponderated by its occurrence; and if before it comes what admits permission or its opposite, rejection is preponderated by absence of [???]. Then she remained silent or laughed, or he married her and the news reached her and she remained silent, it is permission. And if someone other than the guardian asks her, [???] he is truthful because he has no right to receive; if he receives by the husband's order, it is a trust with him, and he is truthful in returning the trust like a depositary if he says 'I returned the deposit'. In al-Dhakhira: the father may litigate with the husband over the mahr of an adult virgin, just as he may receive it; and presence of the woman is not required for collection according to us, contrary to Zufr. If the husband says to the judge: 'the father should take the mahr from me and hand over the girl to me', the judge says to him: 'take the mahr and give her to him'; if the father refuses that, the husband is not required to give it to him. If the father says: 'she is not in my house and I do not know her whereabouts', the husband is not required to give it either. If the father says: 'she is in my house, and I will take the mahr and equip her with it and hand her over to him', then the judge orders the husband to pay it. If he asks for a guarantor for the mahr, the judge orders the father to produce a guarantor for the mahr; when he brings a guarantor, the judge orders the husband to pay the mahr; if he hands over the daughter, the guarantor is discharged; if he is unable, the husband may pursue his right through the guarantor thus balancing interests. This is what Abu Yusuf initially said, then he retracted and said: the judge orders the father to make the woman ready for delivery and bring her, and orders the husband to pay the mahr and the father to hand over the daughter, so the husband pays the mahr at the time of her handing herself over to the husband, because the husband's interest is not achieved by guaranty since he cannot reach the woman inevitably by guaranty; the interest lies in delivering the mahr in her presence. Al-Khassaf said: this is the better of the two opinions. In al-Khulasa: if the father makes part of his daughter's mahr deferred and part immediate, as is customary, then says: 'if the daughter does not accept the gift, I guarantee from my wealth to pay the amount of the gift' — this guarantee is valid. And he gave [???]. His saying: (And if the guardian asks her and she remains silent or laughs, or he marries her and the news reaches her and she remains silent, it is permission) due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The virgin is asked for permission regarding herself; if she remains silent, she has consented.' And because the aspect of consent in it is preponderant since she is too shy to show desire, not to reject; laughter is even more indicative of consent than silence. The principle is that the silence of a virgin for consultation is agency, and for the contract is consent, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. Thus 'idhn' in the wording of al-Mukhtasar is shared between agency and consent; in the first case it is delegation, in the second it is ratification. A branch of its being delegation is that if the guardian asks her about a specific man and she says 'he is suitable' or remains silent, then later she says 'I do not consent' while the guardian is unaware of her lack of consent, and he marries her, it is as in al-Zahiriyya, because the agent is not dismissed until he knows; and silence is not real permission, as is sahih in al-Khaniyya from the chapter of oaths: if he swore that she would not permit her marriage, and she remained silent upon consultation, he does not break his oath. What is meant by wali is one who has recommended guardianship, because the discussion concerns an adult sane woman, so it indicates that she has no guardian. His saying: (reception) but if he is forbidden [by her?], he does not own it and the husband is not released. Many of our scholars have clarified this, so know it. His saying: (And in al-Dhakhira: the father may litigate...) al-Ramli said: i.e., without her agency, as in al-Mudmarat. In Majma' al-Fatawa: a man married a virgin and paid the mahr to the father, he is discharged and the father has no right to receive; but if he [the father] is forbidden [by her?], he does not own it and the husband is not released. Many of our scholars have clarified this, so know it.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on a line from Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the consent of a virgin bride. The main ruling is that when a guardian (wali) asks a mature virgin for permission to marry, her silence constitutes consent. This is based on the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'The virgin is asked for permission regarding herself; if she remains silent, she has consented.' The commentator explains the reasoning: a virgin's modesty makes it difficult for her to express desire openly, but she would readily reject if she objected. Laughter is even stronger evidence of consent. The ruling applies whether the guardian is asking for himself or for another. However, if the guardian is not the nearest one (e.g., a distant relative asking in the presence of a closer guardian), silence does not suffice; explicit verbal consent is required because the distant relative is treated like a stranger. The judge (qadi) is considered a guardian in the absence of blood relatives, and the master of an emancipated slave acts as guardian. If two equal guardians both marry her to different men and she accepts both, both marriages are void; if she remains silent, both are suspended until she explicitly accepts one. The messenger of the guardian (rasul al-wali) has the same authority as the guardian. Crucially, the silence must be voluntary; if it is due to a sneeze, cough, or physical obstruction, it is not valid consent and she may reject afterward. The passage also distinguishes between silence before the contract (which is delegation/agency) and silence after the contract (which is ratification). A branch ruling: if the guardian asks about a specific man and she says 'he is suitable' or remains silent, then later she objects before the guardian knows, the marriage is still valid because the agent (the guardian) is not dismissed until he learns of her objection. However, in oath cases, such silence is not considered real permission. The text then discusses the father's authority regarding the mahr: he may litigate and receive the mahr on behalf of his adult virgin daughter, but if she has forbidden him, he cannot. A dispute with Zufr is mentioned: Zufr required the woman's presence for collection of mahr, but the Hanafi position (Abu Yusuf later, supported by al-Khassaf) does not. The passage also covers the procedure if the father claims the daughter is in his house and wants to use the mahr to equip her, etc. The overall focus is the legal significance of a virgin's silence as a valid expression of consent in marriage, with detailed conditions and exceptions specific to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ولي (walī): Guardian who has the authority to contract marriage; in this passage, primarily the father or agnatic relative, or the judge in their absence.
بكر (bikr): A woman who has never been married (virgin).
سكوت (sukūt): Remaining silent when asked for permission; it is treated as consent if voluntary and in response to the guardian's question.
إذن (idhn): Permission; in this context, it can mean either agency (tawkeel) before the contract or ratification (ijazah) after the contract.
استئمار (isti’mār): The act of seeking permission from the bride; consultation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A guardian asks his adult virgin daughter for permission to marry her to a man. She remains silent. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) The marriage is invalid because she did not speak.
B) The marriage is valid and her silence counts as consent.
C) The marriage is suspended until she speaks.
D) The guardian may not proceed without a witness to her verbal consent.

Key Takeaway
A mature virgin's silence in response to her guardian's marriage proposal constitutes valid consent according to the Hanafi school, provided the guardian is the nearest and the silence is voluntary.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
Virgin's Silence as Consent in Nikah
Explains that a virgin's silence or laughter when asked for consent constitutes permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a virgin's silence as consent for marriage and explain the evidence from the hadith, including the distinction between agency and ratification.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence or laughter when asked for marriage consent is valid permission.

Marriage requires the bride's consent. For a virgin, modesty may prevent her from speaking openly. The Hanafi school, based on a clear Prophetic hadith, accepts her silence as consent. This lesson unpacks the evidence, the distinction between agency and ratification, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules that a virgin's silence or laughter when asked by her guardian constitutes valid permission (idhn) for marriage. Identify the act: seeking consent from a virgin. Identify the ruling: silence/laughter is considered consent.
This is a legal construct, not a separate hukm taklifi.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'The virgin is asked for her consent, and if she remains silent, she has agreed.' This is a sunnah ahad report from al-Bukhari and Muslim.
Thubut is zanni, dalala is zanni, but sufficient for establishing the ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'tusta'mar' (she is asked for consent) indicates that consent is required. The Prophet's statement 'if she remains silent, she has agreed' establishes that silence is a valid expression of consent. The context of modesty supports this.
The imperative form implies obligation, but here it is a condition for validity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Shafi'is require explicit verbal consent even for a virgin, arguing silence is ambiguous. The Hanafi response is that the hadith explicitly takes silence as consent, and custom supports a virgin's shyness.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the ummah.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is based on clear hadith and is mufta bihi. The hadith is authentic, and the ruling is practical for preserving modesty.
Thus, in Hanafi fatwa, silence is sufficient.

كتاب النكاح باب الأولياء والأكفاء
فسكتت أو ضحكت أو زوجها فبلغها الخبر فسكتت فهو إذن لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام البكر تستأمر في نفسها فإن سكتت فقد رضيت» ولأن حيثية الرضا فيه راجحة لأنها تستحي عن إظهار الرغبة لا عن الرد، والضحك أدل على الرضا من السكوت والأصل أن سكوت البكر للاستثمار وكالة وللعقد إجازة كما ذكره الأسبيجابي، فالإذن في عبارة المختصر مشترك بين الوكالة والإجازة، ففي المسألة الأولى توكيل، وفي الثانية إجازة. ويتفرع على كونه توكيلاً أن الولي لو استأذنها في رجل معين فقالت يصلح أو سكتت ثم لما خرج قالت لا أرضى ولم يعلم الولي بعدم رضاها فزوجها فهو كما في الظهيرية، لأن الوكيل لا ينعزل حتى يعلم وليس السكوت إذناً حقيقياً لما صحيح في الخانية من الأيمان : إذا حلفت أن لا تأذن في تزويجها فسكتت عند الاستثمار لا تحنث والمراد بالولي من له ولاية استحباب لأن الكلام في البالغة العاقلة فيفيد أنه ليس لها ولي
اهـ.
Chapter of Marriage, Section on Guardians and Compatible Matches. If [the guardian] sought permission from her and she remained silent, or laughed, or he married her and when the news reached her she remained silent, then that is permission. Due to his saying, peace be upon him: "The virgin is asked for her consent, and if she remains silent, she has agreed." And because the probability of consent is preponderant, as she is shy to show desire but not to reject. And laughter is more indicative of consent than silence. The principle is that the silence of a virgin for seeking permission is agency, and for the contract it is ratification, as mentioned by al-Asbajabi. So permission in the wording of al-Mukhtasar is shared between agency and ratification. In the first case it is appointment as agent, in the second it is ratification. And branching from its being agency: if the guardian sought permission regarding a specific man and she said "He is suitable" or remained silent, then later she said "I do not accept" while the guardian was unaware of her displeasure and he married her, then the ruling is as in al-Zahiriyya, because the agent is not dismissed until he knows. And silence is not actual permission, as is sound in al-Khaniyya from the chapter of oaths: if she swore that she would not give permission for her marriage, and she remained silent when seeking permission, she is not guilty of perjury. And by guardian is meant one who has recommended guardianship, because the discussion concerns an adult sane woman, thus it indicates that she has no guardian [with authority]. End.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Prophet's hadith. The matn states that if the guardian (wali) seeks the consent of a virgin and she remains silent, laughs, or the guardian marries her and she learns of it and remains silent, that constitutes permission. The commentator explains the hadith: the virgin's silence is taken as consent due to her natural modesty in expressing desire but not rejection. Laughter is even stronger evidence of consent. The commentator then clarifies that the word 'idhn' (permission) in the matn can refer either to agency (tawkil) before the contract or ratification (ijaza) after it. He derives a branch ruling: if the guardian seeks permission for a specific man and she indicates approval or is silent, but later changes her mind before the guardian knows, the marriage is still valid because the agent is not dismissed until he receives knowledge of revocation. This is based on the principle that silence is not considered a true revocation. The passage also cites a ruling from al-Khaniyya regarding oaths: if a woman swears she will not give permission for her marriage, her silence when asked does not break the oath. The commentator concludes that the 'guardian' here is one with recommended guardianship (wilayat istihbab), meaning the discussion applies to a mature, sane woman who has no compulsory guardian (i.e., she can act independently).

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission or consent, which can be either agency (tawkil) before the contract or ratification (ijaza) after it.
ولي (wali): Guardian, specifically one with recommended guardianship (wilayat istihbab) for an adult sane woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a virgin remains silent when asked for consent to marriage by her guardian, what is the ruling?
A) Her silence is considered refusal.
B) Her silence is considered consent.
C) Her silence is considered neither consent nor refusal; a verbal answer is required.
D) Her silence is considered consent only if the guardian is her father.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence or laughter when asked for consent constitutes valid permission in Hanafi fiqh.

Father's Authority to Accept Mahr for Adult Virgin Daughter
Ruling on father accepting mahr and role of custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father may validly accept mahr for his adult virgin daughter and apply the role of custom ('urf) in determining validity of property mahr.

Core Idea
Father's acceptance of mahr is valid unless daughter prohibits him; property mahr validity depends on local custom.

This lesson examines the scope of the father's guardianship (wilayah) in accepting mahr for his adult virgin daughter. The Hanafi school recognizes the father's right to accept mahr on her behalf, but this right is not absolute. The daughter's explicit prohibition nullifies it. Additionally, when the mahr consists of property rather than cash, the customary practice of the locality plays a decisive role. We will analyze the ruling and its proofs from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of validity
The father's acceptance of mahr for his adult virgin daughter is valid (sahih) as long as she does not explicitly prohibit him from doing so. If she forbids, his acceptance is void and he does not own the mahr.
This is the default ruling in the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the effect of prohibition
If the adult virgin daughter prohibits the father from accepting the mahr, he cannot own it, and the husband is not discharged from his obligation. The father's authority is cut off by her adulthood (bulugh) when she exercises her right to object.
The passage emphasizes this point explicitly.
3
Analyze the role of custom in property mahr
If the mahr is paid in property (e.g., land) rather than cash, its validity depends on local custom ('urf). If it is customary in that town to pay mahr in property, the acceptance is valid as mahr. If not, it is considered a separate sale, and since the daughter is adult, the father has no guardianship over her property sales, so it is invalid.
Custom transforms the transaction: either mahr acceptance or a sale.
4
Determine the ruling for minor daughter
If the daughter is a minor (saghira), the father's guardianship is broader. The same custom rule applies: if property mahr is customary, it is valid; otherwise not. The minor daughter cannot object.
The passage includes this for completeness.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from al-Dhakhira is that the father's acceptance of mahr is subject to the daughter's non-prohibition and, for property, local custom. This is the fatwa position.
Sources: al-Zahiriyya, al-Dhakhira.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القبض أما إذا انهته فلا يملكه ولا يبرأ الزوج منه. صرح بذلك كثير من علمائنا فاعلم ذلك اهـ. وقد ر التصريح به من المؤلف أيضاً قوله : (وفي الذخيرة للأب المخاصمة الخ قال الرملي : أي بغير وكالة منها كما في المضمرات. وفي مجمع الفتاوى : رجل تزوج امرأة بكراً ودفع المهر إلى الأب برئ وليس
كتاب النكاح باب الأولياء والأكفاء ١٩٥ قال في الظهيرية : رجل تزوج امرأة بكراً بالغة على مهر مسمى ودفع إلى أبيها مهرها ضيعة فلما بلغها الخبر قالت لا أرضى بما فعل الأب ينظر؛ إن كان في بلدة لم يجر التعارف بدفع الضيعة في المهر لم يجز لأن هذا شراء والبلوغ قاطع للولاية، وإن كان في بلدة جرى التعارف بذلك جاز لأن هذا قبض للمهر، وإن كانت البنت صغيرة فأخذ الأب مكان المهر ضيعة لا تساوي المهر، فإن كان في بلد جرى التعارف بذلك جاز وإلا فلا ا هـ . زاد في الذخيرة : وعليه الفتوى.
As for acceptance: if [the daughter] forbids him [from accepting], he does not own [the mahr] and the husband is not discharged. Many of our scholars have explicitly stated this, so know it. End. The author has also explicitly mentioned it, saying: (And in al-Dhakhira: the father may litigate with the husband regarding the mahr of the adult virgin...) al-Ramli said: that is without agency from her, as in al-Mudmarat. And in Majma' al-Fatawa: a man married a virgin woman and paid the mahr to the father; he is discharged and not... [page break] Chapter of Marriage, Section on Guardians and Compatible Matches. He said in al-Zahiriyya: A man married an adult virgin woman with a specified mahr and paid to her father the mahr as property (diya'). When the news reached her, she said: I do not accept what the father did. It is considered: if the town does not customarily pay property as mahr, it is not valid, because this is a sale and adulthood cuts off guardianship. But if the town customarily does so, it is valid, because this is acceptance of the mahr. If the daughter is minor and the father took property instead of mahr that does not equal the mahr, then if the town customarily does so, it is valid; otherwise not. End. He added in al-Dhakhira: and upon this is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the father's authority regarding the mahr. It begins by stating a key condition: if the adult virgin daughter explicitly prohibits her father from accepting the mahr, then he does not own it, and the husband is not discharged from his obligation. This is a settled ruling among Hanafi scholars. The commentary then cites al-Dhakhira, explaining that the father has the right to litigate with the husband over the mahr of his adult virgin daughter without needing her agency. This is because he has the right to accept the mahr. Next, a scenario from al-Zahiriyya is presented: if the father accepts a property (such as land) as mahr instead of cash, its validity depends on custom ('urf). If it is customary in that town to pay mahr in property, the acceptance is valid; otherwise, it is considered a separate sale and invalid because the daughter's adulthood terminates the father's compulsory guardianship. The same applies if the daughter is minor: if custom allows it, it is valid. The passage concludes with a fatwa from al-Dhakhira endorsing this ruling.

Key Terms
ضيعة (diya'): Property, land, or real estate given as mahr instead of cash.
تعارف (ta'aruf): Custom ('urf) of the locality regarding what is acceptable as mahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father accepts a piece of land as mahr for his adult virgin daughter, and it is not customary in that town to pay mahr with land, what is the ruling?
A) The acceptance is valid regardless of custom.
B) The acceptance is invalid because it is a sale, not mahr.
C) The acceptance is valid if the daughter consents.
D) The acceptance is invalid only if the daughter objects.

Key Takeaway
A father may accept mahr for his adult virgin daughter, but her explicit prohibition nullifies it, and for property mahr, local custom determines validity.

Father's Authority to Accept Gifts and Mahr
Rules on father's acceptance of gifts and mahr for daughters of various status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father may accept the mahr and gifts on behalf of his daughter in a Hanafi marriage contract.

Core Idea
Father's authority to accept mahr and gifts depends on the daughter's status: virgin/minor vs adult non-virgin.

In Hanafi fiqh, the father's role in accepting mahr and gifts from the husband is regulated by the daughter's marital and sexual status. This lesson clarifies who can accept what, and the key distinction between minors, adult virgins, and adult non-virgins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Determine what the father is accepting: a gift (hiba/hadiyya) from the husband or the mahr (dower).
2
Determine daughter's status
Check if the daughter is a minor (saghira), an adult virgin (bikr baligha), or an adult non-virgin (thayyib baligha). Also note if she has forbidden the father.
3
Apply the gift ruling
For gifts given to an adult virgin before consummation, the father cannot accept without her permission; else the husband may reclaim.
This applies to gifts, not mahr.
4
Apply the mahr ruling
For a minor's mahr, the father, grandfather, or executor may accept. For an adult non-virgin, no one may accept except herself.
For adult virgin, father may accept unless she forbids.
5
Note special cases
If there is a dispute about consummation, the father's word is taken. If the adult virgin prohibits the father, he loses authority.

Source Text

And in it also: The father does not have the right to accept what the husband gives as a gift or present to the adult virgin before consummation, even if he accepts it without her permission, the husband may reclaim it. End. As for accepting the mahr of a minor, it is for the father, grandfather, and executor (wasi) and not for other guardians, even if she is a mother. So if he pays it to her mother, if she is an executor she is discharged, otherwise after reaching majority she has the option to take it from him or from her. And he may recourse against the mother if the daughter took it from him, as in al-Muhit and others. The father and grandfather may demand it even if she is minor and cannot be enjoyed, unlike maintenance. The judge is like the father except if she is given in marriage (zuffat). And no one may accept the mahr of an adult non-virgin (thayyib). So if the father and husband differ regarding consummation, the father's word is taken, and he swears on denial of knowledge if the woman does not admit to it, and he may also make her swear that he did not consummate with her, as in al-Dhakhira. And the father's admission of having received the mahr when she denies and there is no evidence is not acceptable if at the time of the contract she was an adult non-virgin; otherwise it is acceptable. His admission that he received it when she was a minor, with her denial and no clarification, is not acceptable if at the time of the contract she was an adult; otherwise it is acceptable, and she recourses against the husband. The husband cannot recourse against the father unless he stipulated his discharge from the mahr at the time of acceptance, as in Fath al-Qadir and others. And in al-Dhakhira: the ruling between the agent, the debtor, and the creditor in such a case is analogous to the ruling between the father, the woman, and the husband. In al-Muhit: A man accepted the mahr of his daughter from the husband, then claimed return a second time. If the woman is a virgin, he is not believed except with evidence, because he has the right to accept but not to return. If she is a non-virgin, [he is believed?]. End. Unlike other guardians, they have the right to take her back to her home and prevent her from the husband until he pays her mahr to the one who has the right to accept it, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn and others. When an adult woman is given in marriage (zuffat), the father's right to accept the mahr is cut off, even if she is a virgin. He said: (And unless) (so it is acceptable) meaning if she is not an adult non-virgin, his admission is acceptable. Under this are three scenarios: if she is an adult virgin. He said in al-Bazzaziyya: If the father admits to having received the mahr, if she is a virgin he is believed, if she is a non-virgin he is not. End. Or if at the time of the contract she was a minor absolutely, then in these three it is accepted. The apparent meaning of al-Bazzaziyya is that it is not accepted for a minor non-virgin, because he makes the criterion maidenhood vs. defloration. Al-Ramli said: And in Jami' al-Fusulayn the correct view is to make minority the criterion. End. The majority base the ruling on maidenhood and defloration except for a minor non-virgin, where the ruling is like that of a minor virgin. This is transmitted in Jami' al-Fusulayn from fatawa of Rashid al-Din and from al-Jami' and al-Fatawa, and is transmitted here from al-Dhakhira, for his restriction to the adult non-virgin regarding the father's authority to accept her mahr is what he presented at the beginning of the statement. Similar is in al-Bazzaziyya, Majma' al-Fatawa, al-Zahiriyya, and most fatwa books; so it should be relied upon. And all this is on condition that she has not prohibited him from accepting. If she prohibits him, he does not own it and the husband is not discharged. Many of our scholars have explicitly stated this, so know it. End. And the author has also explicitly mentioned it, saying: (And in al-Dhakhira: the father may litigate with the husband regarding the mahr of the adult virgin...) al-Ramli said: that is without agency from her, as in al-Mudmarat. And in Majma' al-Fatawa: a man married a virgin woman and paid the mahr to the father; he is discharged and not...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents several distinct rulings. First, the father cannot accept gifts given by the husband to his adult virgin daughter before consummation; such acceptance is invalid without her permission, and the husband may reclaim the gift. Second, regarding the mahr of a minor daughter, only the father, grandfather, and the executor (wasi) have the right to accept it. If paid to the mother without authorization, the daughter has a choice upon majority to claim the mahr from either the father or the mother. Third, no one has the right to accept the mahr of an adult non-virgin (thayyib) except herself. The father's word is taken if there is a dispute about consummation. Fourth, the passage discusses the father's admission (iqrar) of having received the mahr. If at the time of the contract the daughter was an adult non-virgin, his admission is not accepted against her denial. If she was a virgin or a minor, it is accepted. There is a difference of opinion whether minority or virginity is the criterion; the majority hold that virginity is the basis, except for a minor non-virgin who is treated like a minor virgin. The passage concludes by reiterating the earlier condition: if the adult virgin daughter prohibits her father from accepting, his acceptance is void. The repetition at the end confirms the importance of this rule.

Key Terms
ثيب (thayyib): A non-virgin woman, previously married or otherwise.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession by the father that he has received the mahr.
بكر (bikr): A virgin woman.
صغيرة (saghira): A minor girl, below the age of majority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A father accepts the mahr of his 20-year-old virgin daughter. She later forbids him. Is his acceptance valid?
A) Yes, because he accepted before she forbade him.
B) No, because her prohibition retroactively invalidates it.
C) Yes, because a virgin cannot forbid her father.
D) No, because he never had authority over an adult virgin.

Key Takeaway
A father's authority to accept gifts and mahr hinges on the daughter's status: fully for minors, conditionally for adult virgins, and not at all for adult non-virgins.
